Equivalence of Indefinite and Particular Propositions
Author explains equivalence and addresses objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to demonstrate the mutual entailment between indefinite and particular propositions and evaluate the objection about a third category.

Core Idea
An indefinite proposition is logically equivalent to a particular proposition.

The text asserts that an indefinite proposition (muhmala) is in the force of a particular (juz'iyya), meaning they are mutually entailing. This is an immediate inference that has practical implications in Islamic legal reasoning. The author then addresses a potential objection about judgments on both individuals and nature. Let's analyze this logical equivalence and the beginning of the response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim of equivalence
The text says the indefinite is 'in the force of the particular' (في قوة الجزئية), meaning they are mutually entailing (متلازمتان).
2
Understand the two directions of entailment
If the indefinite 'Humans are in loss' is true, then the particular 'Some humans are in loss' is true. Conversely, if the particular is true, the indefinite is also true.
The text states this is evident (ظاهر).
3
Consider the objection
A questioner proposes a third category: judgment on both individuals and nature together. Does this fit natural or indefinite?
4
Analyze the author's response start
The author responds that they are discussing propositions used in the sciences, implying that such a mixed judgment is covered by one of the existing types (likely indefinite, since it involves individuals).
The text is truncated, but the response likely resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وإن لم يبين مقدار أفراد الموضوع (فإن لم يصلح القضية لأن تقصد بها كلية أو جزئية) بأن يكون الحكم فيها على نفس طبيعة الموضوع مطلقة أو مقيدة بالعموم (سميت) القضية التي حكم فيها على نفس طبيعة الموضوع (طبعية، وإلا) أي: وإن صلحت القضية لأن تصدق كلية أو جزئية (فمهملة) أي: سميت مهملة؛ لإهمال بيان كمية الأفراد فيها مع الاحتمال، كقولنا : الحيوان (جنس) مثال القضية الطبعية، فإنه حكم فيها على طبيعة الحيوان من حيث إنه عام وكقولنا الإنسان مقول، والحيوان مقوم(۳)، فإنه حكم فيها على طبيعة الإنسان والحيوان مطلقا، وكقولنا : (الإنسان في خسر) مثال القضية المهملة. (و) القضية المهملة في قوة الجزئية) يعنى أنهما متلازمتان؛ إذ متى صدق «الإنسان في خسر» صدق «بعض الإنسان في خسر وبالعكس) يعني متى صدق «بعض الإنسان في خسر » صدق «الإنسان في خسر» وذلك ظاهر. فإن قيل: بقي قسم آخر، وهو أن يكون الحكم على الأفراد والطبيعة معا، قلنا: إن بحثنا في القضايا المستعملة في العلوم والقضية التي يكون الحكم فيها على أفراد الموضوع
And if the quantity of the individuals of the subject is not specified: then if the proposition is not suitable to be intended as universal or particular (i.e., the judgment is on the nature itself, absolute or restricted to generality), it is called 'natural' (tabi'iyya) — the proposition in which the judgment is on the nature itself. And if not (i.e., the proposition can be true as universal or particular), then it is 'indefinite' (muhmala), called so because the quantity of individuals is neglected despite the possibility. Example of the natural proposition: 'Animal is a genus', for it judges on the nature of animal insofar as it is general; and also 'Human is [a predicate] and animal is [a constituent]', for it judges on the nature of human and animal absolutely. And example of the indefinite: 'Humans are in loss.' And the indefinite proposition is in the force of the particular, meaning they are mutually entailing: for whenever 'Humans are in loss' is true, 'Some humans are in loss' is true, and vice versa. That is evident. For if it is said: Another category remains, which is that the judgment be on the individuals and the nature together. We say: We are discussing the propositions used in the sciences, and the proposition in which the judgment is on the individuals of the subject...
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage covers two more types of categorical propositions based on whether the quantity of the subject is specified. If it is not specified, the proposition is either (a) natural (tabi'iyya): when the judgment is on the nature itself, not intended as universal or particular, e.g., 'Animal is a genus' (judging on the nature of animal) or 'Human is an inferior [predicable]' (judging on the nature); or (b) indefinite (muhmala): when the proposition could be taken as universal or particular, e.g., 'Humans are in loss.' The author then asserts that the indefinite is equivalent in force to the particular (fi quwwa al-juz'iyya), meaning they are mutually entailing: if 'Humans are in loss' is true, then 'Some humans are in loss' is true, and vice versa. A questioner objects that another category remains: a judgment on both individuals and nature together. The author begins to answer that the discussion concerns propositions used in the sciences, and the proposition in which the judgment is on the individuals of the subject... (text cuts off). This answer likely explains that such a case is covered by one of the existing types. In fiqh, an indefinite proposition like 'The scholars are fallible' is understood as particular in force, not universal.

Key Takeaway
Indefinite propositions are equivalent to particular propositions in logical force.

?

The Proper Placement of Quantifiers in Propositions
Author explains the proper placement of quantifiers and defines deviated propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a quantifier is placed on the subject or predicate and determine if a proposition is deviated (munharifa) based on the four divisions.

Core Idea
Quantifiers logically belong on the subject; placing them on the predicate creates a deviated proposition.

In Islamic logic, quantifiers like 'all' and 'some' have a natural home on the subject of a proposition. This lesson explains why deviating from this placement changes the logical character of the proposition, and how it leads to four possible forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier
Look for words like 'all', 'every', 'some', 'none' in the proposition. In Arabic, quantifiers like 'كُلُّ' (all) or 'بَعْضُ' (some) indicate the quantity.
Quantifiers can also be implied.
2
Check quantifier placement
Determine if the quantifier is attached to the subject (e.g., 'كل إنسان فانٍ' – every human is mortal) or to the predicate (e.g., 'إنسان كل فانٍ' – a human is every mortal).
Proper placement is on the subject.
3
Recognize deviated propositions
If the quantifier is on the predicate, the proposition is called 'deviated' (munharifa) because it has deviated from the standard form.
The author gives the example of quantifying the predicate.
4
Determine the four divisions
The quantified predicate can be universal or particular, and the subject can also be universal or particular. Multiply the possibilities (2×2) to get four types of deviated propositions.
The author mentions this but says the full discussion is too long for this abridged work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس السور محصورا بما ذكر، بل في كل لغة سور مخصوص بها، ومن حق السور أن يرد على الموضوع؛ لأن الذي يحمل عليه الشيءُ قد يُشك في كون الحكم على كل أفراده أو على البعض، وقلما يتحقق ذلك في المحمول؛ لأن المراد منه المفهوم، ولا تعدد فيه حتى يُشك، اللهم إلا إذا جعل المحمول أفراده، فإذا أورد السور على المحمول فقد انحرف عن الواجب، فسميت القضية حينئذ منحرفة وأقسامها أربعة؛ لأن المحمول المسوَّر إما جزئي أو كلي، وكيفما كان فالموضوع أيضا كذلك، فتحصل أربعة أقسام بضرب الاثنين في الاثنين وبحث المنحرفات طويل الذيل لا يليق استقصاؤه بهذا المختصر . وإن لم يبين مقدار أفراد الموضوع بأن لا تصلح القضية لأن تقصد بها كلية أو جزئية بأن يكون الحكم فيها على نفس طبيعة الموضوع مطلقة أو مقيدة بالعموم سميت القضية التي حكم فيها على نفس طبيعة الموضوع طبعية، وإلا فمهملة سميت مهملة؛ لإهمال بيان كمية الأفراد فيها مع الاحتمال، كقولنا : الحيوان جنس مثال القضية الطبعية، فإنه حكم فيها على طبيعة الحيوان من حيث إنه عام وكقولنا الإنسان مقول، والحيوان مقوم، فإنه حكم فيها على طبيعة الإنسان والحيوان مطلقا، وكقولنا : الإنسان في خسر مثال القضية المهملة. والقضية المهملة في قوة الجزئية يعنى أنهما متلازمتان؛ إذ متى صدق الإنسان في خسر صدق بعض الإنسان في خسر وبالعكس يعني متى صدق بعض الإنسان في خسر صدق الإنسان في خسر وذلك ظاهر. فإن قيل: بقي قسم آخر، وهو أن يكون الحكم على الأفراد والطبيعة معا، قلنا: إن بحثنا في القضايا المستعملة في العلوم والقضية التي يكون الحكم فيها على أفراد الموضوع والطبيعة معا ليست منها، كذا أجيب، وفيه بحث؛ لأن القضية الطبعية أيضا غير مستعملة في العلوم، فلم ذكره؟
And the quantifier is not limited to what was mentioned; rather in every language there are quantifiers specific to it. And it is the right of the quantifier to be placed on the subject, because the thing that is predicated of something may be doubted regarding whether the judgment is on all its individuals or some of them, and that rarely applies to the predicate, because what is intended from it is the concept, and there is no multiplicity in it to be doubted, unless the predicate is considered as its individuals. So if the quantifier is placed on the predicate, it has deviated from what is required, so the proposition is then called 'deviated', and its divisions are four: because the quantified predicate is either particular or universal, and whatever it is, the subject is likewise, so four divisions are obtained by multiplying two by two, and the discussion of deviated propositions is lengthy, not suitable for full investigation in this abridged work. And if the extent of the individuals of the subject is not clarified (so that the proposition is not fit to be intended as universal or particular) by the judgment being on the very nature of the subject unconditionally or restricted by generality, the proposition in which judgment is on the very nature of the subject is called 'natural', otherwise it is called 'indefinite', because the quantity of individuals is neglected while having probability, like our saying: 'Animal is a genus' is an example of a natural proposition, for it is a judgment on the nature of animal insofar as it is general, and like our saying: 'Man is a predicate' and 'Animal is a constitutive' is a judgment on the nature of man and animal absolutely, and like our saying: 'Man is in loss' is an example of an indefinite proposition. And the indefinite proposition is equivalent to the particular, meaning they are mutually entailing; for whenever 'Man is in loss' is true, 'Some man is in loss' is true, and vice versa, meaning whenever 'Some man is in loss' is true, 'Man is in loss' is true, and that is apparent. If it is said: There remains another division, which is that the judgment is on the individuals and the nature together. We say: Our discussion is on the propositions used in the sciences, and the proposition in which the judgment is on the individuals of the subject and the nature together is not among them. Thus it was answered, and there is discussion in it; because the natural proposition is also not used in the sciences, so why mention it?
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies propositions in logic, focusing on quantifiers and the types that arise from their placement or omission. The author begins by stating that quantifiers are language-specific and logically belong on the subject, because the predicate is a concept that does not have individuals in the same way. Placing a quantifier on the predicate leads to a 'deviated' (munharifa) proposition, which has four divisions based on the quantity (particular or universal) of both predicate and subject. The author then discusses two special types: 'natural' (tabi'iyya) propositions, where the judgment is directly on the nature of the subject (e.g., 'Animal is a genus'), and 'indefinite' (muhmala) propositions, where no quantity is specified but could be either universal or particular (e.g., 'Man is in loss'). Importantly, the author states that the indefinite proposition is logically equivalent to the particular: if 'Man is in loss' is true, then 'Some man is in loss' is true, and vice versa. This equivalence rests on the assumption that the statement about the nature is taken as about some individuals. The passage concludes with an objection: what about a proposition that judges both individuals and nature? The author answers that such propositions are not used in the sciences, and the natural proposition itself is also not used, so the inclusion of the natural type is questioned. This reveals a critical reflection on which proposition types are actually employed in scientific discourse.

Key Terms
سور
منحرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the proper placement of a quantifier according to Islamic logic?
A) On the predicate
B) On the subject
C) On the copula
D) Either on subject or predicate

Key Takeaway
Quantifiers should be placed on the subject; placing them on the predicate yields a deviated proposition with four possible forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'Some creation is originated' (quantifier on subject) vs 'Creation is some originated' (quantifier on predicate), identify which is deviated and explain the four possible divisions if you treat it as deviated, using the author's classification of predicate and subject as universal or particular.

Natural and Indefinite Propositions in Islamic Logic
Author defines natural and indefinite propositions and their logical equivalence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between natural (tabi'iyya) and indefinite (muhmala) propositions and convert an indefinite proposition to an equivalent particular proposition.

Core Idea
Natural propositions judge the nature itself; indefinite propositions are equivalent to particulars.

Not all propositions explicitly quantify over individuals. Some judge the very nature of the subject, while others leave quantity unspecified. This lesson unpacks these two types and their logical relationships, including the equivalence of indefinite to particular.

Guided Path
1
Recognize a natural proposition
A natural proposition judges the nature of the subject itself, not its individuals. Example: 'Animal is a genus' – this is about the nature of animal, not each animal.
The author says the judgment is on the 'very nature' (nafs tabi'a).
2
Recognize an indefinite proposition
An indefinite proposition has no quantifier and does not judge the nature. Example: 'Man is in loss' – it is unclear whether it means all men or some men.
The quantity is neglected but implied.
3
Apply the equivalence rule
The author states that an indefinite proposition is equivalent to a particular one. For 'Man is in loss' to be true, 'Some man is in loss' must be true, and vice versa.
This equivalence is context-dependent and holds in scientific discourse.
4
Classify propositions yourself
Given a proposition, determine if it is natural, indefinite, universal, or particular based on the presence of quantifiers and whether the judgment is on the nature or individuals.
Use the author's criteria: if the judgment is on the nature, it's natural; if no quantifier and not on nature, it's indefinite.

And the quantifier is not limited to what was mentioned; rather in every language there are quantifiers specific to it. And it is the right of the quantifier to be placed on the subject, because the thing that is predicated of something may be doubted regarding whether the judgment is on all its individuals or some of them, and that rarely applies to the predicate, because what is intended from it is the concept, and there is no multiplicity in it to be doubted, unless the predicate is considered as its individuals. The Status of Natural Propositions in Scientific Discourse
Author raises and critiques an objection about the classification of propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection and response about why natural propositions are included even though they are not used in sciences, and evaluate the author's reasoning.

Core Idea
The author questions why natural propositions are mentioned if they are not used in sciences, revealing critical self-awareness in logical classification.

After defining natural and indefinite propositions, the author raises an objection: if natural propositions are not used in sciences, why include them? This lesson explores that critique and the attempted response, highlighting the methodological concerns in selecting which logical types to teach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection is that there is a type of proposition not covered: one where the judgment is both on the individuals and the nature of the subject.
The objector says: 'There remains another division...'.
2
Understand the author's response
The author responds that such propositions are not used in the sciences, so they are omitted from the classification.
The response is: 'Our discussion is on the propositions used in the sciences...'.
3
Identify the counter-critique
The author himself then critiques this response: if natural propositions are also not used in sciences, why are they included? This undermines the consistency of the criterion.
The author says: 'There is discussion in it; because the natural proposition is also not used in the sciences, so why mention it?'.
4
Evaluate the debate
Consider whether the author's inclusion of natural propositions is justified despite the critique. The author does not provide a resolution, leaving it as an open methodological question.
This shows that even classical logicians debated the scope of their subject.

وليس السور محصورا بما ذكر، بل في كل لغة سور مخصوص بها، ومن حق السور أن يرد على الموضوع؛ لأن الذي يحمل عليه الشيءُ قد يُشك في كون الحكم على كل أفراده أو على البعض، وقلما يتحقق ذلك في المحمول؛ لأن المراد منه المفهوم، ولا تعدد فيه حتى يُشك، اللهم إلا إذا جعل المحمول أفراده، فإذا أورد السور على المحمول فقد انحرف عن الواجب، فسميت القضية حينئذ منحرفة وأقسامها أربعة؛ لأن المحمول المسوَّر إما جزئي أو كلي، وكيفما كان فالموضوع أيضا كذلك، فتحصل أربعة أقسام بضرب الاثنين في الاثنين وبحث المنحرفات طويل الذيل لا يليق استقصاؤه بهذا المختصر . وإن لم يبين مقدار أفراد الموضوع بأن لا تصلح القضية لأن تقصد بها كلية أو جزئية بأن يكون الحكم فيها على نفس طبيعة الموضوع مطلقة أو مقيدة بالعموم سميت القضية التي حكم فيها على نفس طبيعة الموضوع طبعية، وإلا فمهملة سميت مهملة؛ لإهمال بيان كمية الأفراد فيها مع الاحتمال، كقولنا : الحيوان جنس مثال القضية الطبعية، فإنه حكم فيها على طبيعة الحيوان من حيث إنه عام وكقولنا الإنسان مقول، والحيوان مقوم، فإنه حكم فيها على طبيعة الإنسان والحيوان مطلقا، وكقولنا : الإنسان في خسر مثال القضية المهملة. والقضية المهملة في قوة الجزئية يعنى أنهما متلازمتان؛ إذ متى صدق الإنسان في خسر صدق بعض الإنسان في خسر وبالعكس يعني متى صدق بعض الإنسان في خسر صدق الإنسان في خسر وذلك ظاهر. فإن قيل: بقي قسم آخر، وهو أن يكون الحكم على الأفراد والطبيعة معا، قلنا: إن بحثنا في القضايا المستعملة في العلوم والقضية التي يكون الحكم فيها على أفراد الموضوع والطبيعة معا ليست منها، كذا أجيب، وفيه بحث؛ لأن القضية الطبعية أيضا غير مستعملة في العلوم، فلم ذكره؟
And the quantifier is not limited to what was mentioned; rather in every language there are quantifiers specific to it. And it is the right of the quantifier to be placed on the subject, because the thing that is predicated of something may be doubted regarding whether the judgment is on all its individuals or some of them, and that rarely applies to the predicate, because what is intended from it is the concept, and there is no multiplicity in it to be doubted, unless the predicate is considered as its individuals. So if the quantifier is placed on the predicate, it has deviated from what is required, so the proposition is then called 'deviated', and its divisions are four: because the quantified predicate is either particular or universal, and whatever it is, the subject is likewise, so four divisions are obtained by multiplying two by two, and the discussion of deviated propositions is lengthy, not suitable for full investigation in this abridged work. And if the extent of the individuals of the subject is not clarified (so that the proposition is not fit to be intended as universal or particular) by the judgment being on the very nature of the subject unconditionally or restricted by generality, the proposition in which judgment is on the very nature of the subject is called 'natural', otherwise it is called 'indefinite', because the quantity of individuals is neglected while having probability, like our saying: 'Animal is a genus' is an example of a natural proposition, for it is a judgment on the nature of animal insofar as it is general, and like our saying: 'Man is a predicate' and 'Animal is a constitutive' is a judgment on the nature of man and animal absolutely, and like our saying: 'Man is in loss' is an example of an indefinite proposition. And the indefinite proposition is equivalent to the particular, meaning they are mutually entailing; for whenever 'Man is in loss' is true, 'Some man is in loss' is true, and vice versa, meaning whenever 'Some man is in loss' is true, 'Man is in loss' is true, and that is apparent. If it is said: There remains another division, which is that the judgment is on the individuals and the nature together. We say: Our discussion is on the propositions used in the sciences, and the proposition in which the judgment is on the individuals of the subject and the nature together is not among them. Thus it was answered, and there is discussion in it; because the natural proposition is also not used in the sciences, so why mention it?
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies propositions in logic, focusing on quantifiers and the types that arise from their placement or omission. The author begins by stating that quantifiers are language-specific and logically belong on the subject, because the predicate is a concept that does not have individuals in the same way. Placing a quantifier on the predicate leads to a 'deviated' (munharifa) proposition, which has four divisions based on the quantity (particular or universal) of both predicate and subject. The author then discusses two special types: 'natural' (tabi'iyya) propositions, where the judgment is directly on the nature of the subject (e.g., 'Animal is a genus'), and 'indefinite' (muhmala) propositions, where no quantity is specified but could be either universal or particular (e.g., 'Man is in loss'). Importantly, the author states that the indefinite proposition is logically equivalent to the particular: if 'Man is in loss' is true, then 'Some man is in loss' is true, and vice versa. This equivalence rests on the assumption that the statement about the nature is taken as about some individuals. The passage concludes with an objection: what about a proposition that judges both individuals and nature? The author answers that such propositions are not used in the sciences, and the natural proposition itself is also not used, so the inclusion of the natural type is questioned. This reveals a critical reflection on which proposition types are actually employed in scientific discourse.

Classifying Propositions: Deviated vs. Simple (Ma'dula, Muhassala, Basita)
Author classifies propositions based on the role of the negative particle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposition is ma‘dūla (deviated in subject, predicate, or both), muḥaṣṣala, or basīṭa by examining the position of the negative particle and determining the nature of the relation.

Core Idea
The distinction between deviated and simple propositions hinges on whether negation modifies a term or the relation.

In this passage, the author classifies propositions based on where the negative particle appears. This classification is crucial for correctly analysing the logical form of statements in Islamic sciences. For instance, a proposition like 'The not-living is inanimate' looks negative but is actually affirmative regarding the relation. Let's break down the types step by step.

Guided Path
1
Locate the negative particle
Identify the Arabic negation words 'laysa' or 'lā' in the proposition. Note whether they are attached to the subject, predicate, or stand alone.
In Arabic, negation can be a separate word or a prefix like 'lā'.
2
Determine if the particle is part of a term or the relation
If the negation is part of the subject or predicate (e.g., 'al-lā-ḥayy'), the proposition is ma‘dūla. If it negates the relation alone, it is simple negative (basīṭa) or affirmative (muḥaṣṣala) if positive.
Example: 'Al-ḥayy laysa bi-jamād' — 'laysa' negates the relation, so it is basīṭa.
3
Classify the type of ma‘dūla
If the negation is only in the subject → ma‘dūlat al-mawḍū‘; only in predicate → ma‘dūlat al-maḥmūl; in both → ma‘dūlat al-ṭarafayn.
Examples: 'Al-lā-ḥayy jamād' (subject deviated), 'Al-ḥayy lā-‘ālim' (predicate deviated), 'Al-lā-ḥayy lā-‘ālim' (both deviated).
4
Determine affirmation/negation by the relation, not the terms
Even if terms contain negation, if the relation is positive (the predicate is affirmed of the subject), the proposition is affirmative. Example: 'Kull mā laysa bi-ḥayy fa-huwa lā-‘ālim' is affirmative because 'lā-‘ālim' is affirmed of 'mā laysa bi-ḥayy'.
This prevents confusion when analyzing deviated propositions with negative terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في العدول والتحصيل. حرف السلب كـ ليس ولا إن كان جزءا من الموضوع فقط، كقولنا : اللاحي جماد، أو جزءا من المحمول فقط، كقولنا الجماد لاحي، أو جزءا منهما : أي من المحمول والموضوع معا، كقولنا : اللاحي لاعالم سميت القضية معدولة الأولى معدولة الموضوع، والثانية معدولة المحمول، والثالثة معدولة الطرفين، موجبة كانت تلك القضية وأمثلتها قد مرت أو سالبة كقولنا : ليس اللاحي عالم وكقولنا : ليس العالم بلاحي، وكقولنا: ليس اللاحي بلا جماد. وإنما سميت معدولة؛ لأن حرف السلب وضع في الأصل للسلب والرفع، فإذا جعل مع غيره كشيء واحد يُثبت له شيء كما في الموجبة المعدولة الموضوع- أو يثبت هو لشيء كما في الموجبة المعدولة المحمول - أو يُسلب عنه شيء كما في السالبة المعدولة الموضوع أو يُسلب عن شيء كما في السالبة المعدولة المحمول- فقد عُدل عن موضوعه الأصلي. وإن لم يكن حرف السلب جزءا لشيء منهما أي: من المحمول والموضوع سميت القضية محصلة إن كانت موجبة كقولنا زيد كاتب وسميت بسيطة إن كانت سالبة؛ لأنها بسيطة بالنسبة إلى السالبة المعدولة، كقولنا: الحي ليس بجماد، وبعضهم يسمونها محصلةً، موجبةً كانت أو سالبة؛ لتحصل طرفيها. والاعتبار بالإيجاب أي بإيجاب القضية والسلب أي بسلب القضية بالنسبة لا بطرفيها يعني: إن كانت النسبة ثبوتية فالقضية موجبة وإن كانت سلبية فسالبة، سواء كانت الأطراف وجودية أو عدمية فإن قولنا كل ما ليس بحي فهو لا عالم، موجبة؛
Chapter: On Deviation and Affirmation. The letter of negation like 'laysa' and 'lā' — if it is part of the subject only, like our saying: 'The not-living is inanimate', or part of the predicate only, like our saying: 'The inanimate is not-living', or part of both, i.e., of the predicate and the subject together, like our saying: 'The not-living is not-knowing' — the proposition is called 'deviated' (ma‘dūla). The first is deviated in subject, the second deviated in predicate, the third deviated in both terms. That proposition may be affirmative, and its examples have passed, or negative, like our saying: 'The not-living is not knowing', and like our saying: 'The knowing is not not-living', and like our saying: 'The not-living is not not-inanimate'. And it is only called deviated because the letter of negation is originally placed for negation and removal. So if it is made with something else as one thing, something is affirmed of it — as in the affirmative deviated in subject — or it is affirmed of something — as in the affirmative deviated in predicate — or something is negated from it — as in the negative deviated in subject — or it is negated from something — as in the negative deviated in predicate — then it has deviated from its original usage. And if the letter of negation is not part of either of them, i.e., of the predicate and the subject, the proposition is called 'affirmative' (muḥaṣṣala) if it is affirmative, like our saying: 'Zayd is a writer', and is called 'simple' (basīṭa) if it is negative, because it is simple relative to the negative deviated, like our saying: 'The living is not inanimate'. And some call them 'affirmative' (muḥaṣṣala) whether affirmative or negative, because both terms are affirmed. And the consideration is by affirmation, i.e., by the affirmation of the proposition, and negation, i.e., by the negation of the proposition, with respect to the relation, not with respect to its terms. That is: if the relation is positive, the proposition is affirmative, and if it is negative, it is negative, whether the terms are existential or non-existential. So our saying: 'Everything that is not living is not-knowing' is affirmative;
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines two categories of propositions based on the role of the negative particle: deviated (ma‘dūla) and affirmative/simple (muḥaṣṣala/basīṭa). The author begins with deviated propositions: when the negation is part of the subject, predicate, or both, the proposition is called 'deviated' because the negative particle is attached to a term, altering its normal function. Examples are given: 'The not-living is inanimate' (subject deviated), 'The inanimate is not-living' (predicate deviated), and 'The not-living is not-knowing' (both deviated). The deviated can be affirmative or negative depending on whether the relation is positive or negative. The reason for the name 'deviated' is explained: the negative particle originally serves to negate the relation, but when made part of a term, it deviates from its original purpose. Next, if the negative particle is not part of either term, the proposition is called 'affirmative' (muḥaṣṣala) if it is affirmative (e.g., 'Zayd is a writer'), and 'simple' (basīṭa) if it is negative (e.g., 'The living is not inanimate'). Some logicians call both types 'affirmative' because the terms themselves are affirmed (i.e., not negated). The author then clarifies a crucial point: the evaluation of a proposition as affirmative or negative depends on the relation (nisba), not on the terms. So even if a term contains a negation, if the relation is positive, the proposition is affirmative. Example: 'Everything that is not living is not-knowing' is affirmative because the relation is positive (the predicate 'not-knowing' is affirmed of the subject 'not living').

معدولة
محصلة
بسيطة

Key Takeaway
A deviated proposition (ma‘dūla) has the negative particle embedded in a term, while simple propositions have the negation on the relation; the affirmation/negation of a proposition is determined by the relation, not the terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If so, which type?

Modified and Simple Propositions: The Role of Negation
Author defines modified and simple propositions based on the role of the negation particle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition as modified (معدولة) or determined/simple (محصلة/بسيطة) by identifying the role of the negation particle, and determine its quality (affirmative or negative) based on the relation, not the terms.

Core Idea
The same Arabic sentence can be either affirmative modified or simple negative depending on the intended scope of negation.

In Islamic logic, not every 'not' does the same job. Sometimes it modifies a term, creating a new attribute; sometimes it negates the relation between subject and predicate. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting propositions in Kalam and Fiqh, where rulings often hinge on the precise logical form of a statement. In this lesson, we will learn to parse the role of the negation particle and classify propositions accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Locate the negation particle
Identify any negation particle such as 'ليس' (laysa) or 'لا' (la) in the proposition. This is the key signal that the proposition may be modified or simple.
The negation particle can appear in subject, predicate, or as a separate copula negator.
2
Determine if it is part of a term
Ask: Is the negation particle an integral part of the subject or predicate (e.g., 'اللاحي' = the not-living, 'لاعالم' = not-knowing), or does it stand alone before the predicate (e.g., 'ليس بكاتب')? If it is part of a term, the proposition is modified (معدولة). If it is not part of either term, the proposition is determined (محصلة) if affirmative, or simple (بسيطة) if negative.
For 'ليس بكاتب', the 'ب' is often considered part of the predicate, making the negation particle part of the term.
3
Identify the type of modified proposition
If the proposition is modified, note whether the negation particle is in the subject only, predicate only, or both. This gives you modified subject, modified predicate, or modified both ends.
Example: 'اللاحي جماد' is modified subject; 'الجماد لاحي' is modified predicate.
4
Determine affirmative or negative by relation
Ignore the existential status of the terms. Look at the relation (نسبة) between subject and predicate: if the relation is one of affirmation (ثبوت), the proposition is affirmative (موجبة), even if terms are negative (e.g., 'كل ما ليس بحي فهو لا عالم' affirms 'not-knowingness'). If the relation is one of negation (سلب), the proposition is negative (سالبة), even if terms are positive (e.g., 'لا شيء من المتحرك بساكن').
This step resolves the apparent paradox of affirmative propositions with negative terms.
5
Resolve ambiguity in Arabic
For propositions like 'زيد ليس بكاتب', the same wording can be either affirmative modified predicate (if the negation is part of the predicate) or simple negative (if the negation acts on the copula). In Arabic with copula, word order helps: 'زيد هو ليس بكاتب' (copula before negation) indicates affirmation; 'زيد ليس هو بكاتب' (negation before copula) indicates simple negation. Without copula, intention decides.
This ambiguity is a common source of confusion; the author provides rules to resolve it.

شأن حرف السلب سلب الربط الذي بعده، فيكون سلبا، (وأما) الفرق بينهما (في) القضية (الثنائية) وهي التي لم يذكر فيها الرابطة (فبالنية) يعني: إن نوى ربط السلب تكون موجبة وإن نوى سلب الربط تكون سالبة، ويفهم من ظاهر العبارة أن هذا فرق لفظي، وليس كذلك.
Chapter: On Modification and Determination. The negation particle, like 'laysa' and 'la', if it is a part of the subject only, as in our saying 'the not-living is inanimate', or a part of the predicate only, as in our saying 'the inanimate is not-living', or a part of both, i.e., of the predicate and the subject together, as in our saying 'the not-living is not-knowing', the proposition is called 'modified' (ma'dula). The first is modified subject, the second modified predicate, and the third modified both ends. Whether that proposition is affirmative, and its examples have already passed, or negative, as in our saying 'the not-living is not knowing' and our saying 'the world is not not-living' and our saying 'the not-living is not not-inanimate'. It is called modified only because the negation particle was originally placed for negation and removal. So when it is made with something else as one thing, something is affirmed of it, as in the affirmative modified subject, or it is affirmed of something, as in the affirmative modified predicate, or something is negated from it, as in the negative modified subject, or it is negated from something, as in the negative modified predicate, then it has been turned away from its original purpose. And if the negation particle is not a part of either of them, i.e., of the predicate and the subject, the proposition is called 'determined' (muhassala) if it is affirmative, as in our saying 'Zayd is a writer', and it is called 'simple' (basita) if the proposition is negative, because it is simple relative to the negative modified, as in our saying 'the living is not inanimate'. Some call both 'determined', whether affirmative or negative, because their two ends are determined. The consideration is with affirmation, i.e., the affirmation of the proposition, and negation, i.e., the negation of the proposition, with respect to the relation, not with respect to its two ends. That is, if the relation is affirmative, then the proposition is affirmative, and if it is negative, then negative, whether the ends are existential or non-existential. For our saying 'Everything that is not living is not-knowing' is affirmative because therein is a judgment of the affirmation of not-knowingness of everything that 'not living' applies to, even though its two ends are non-existential, due to the presence of the negation particle in them. And our saying 'Nothing of the moving is at rest' is negative, because therein is a judgment of the negation of rest from everything that 'moving' applies to, even though its two ends are existential, due to the absence of the negation particle in them. In this example there is an indication that what is meant by the non-existentiality of the ends is that the negation particle is a part of their wording, not that non-existence is considered in their concept. So these are four propositions: affirmative determined, negative determined, affirmative modified, and negative modified. There is no confusion among the four in meaning and wording except between the simple negative and the affirmative modified predicate, for they are confused in wording, due to the presence of the negation particle in them, with the possibility that it is a part of the predicate, so the proposition is affirmative modified predicate, and the possibility that it is not a part of it, so the proposition is simple negative, as in our saying 'Zayd is not a writer'. Therefore he said: The difference between the simple negative and the affirmative modified predicate: As for concept, what preceded, namely that the judgment in the affirmative is by occurrence and in the negative by removal. As for material, the simple negative is more general than the affirmative modified. As for wording, in other than the Arabic language it is apparent, because the copula of affirmation is 'hast' and negation 'nist'. In the Arabic language, as for the triliteral (i.e., that in which the copula is mentioned), the proposition is affirmative modified if you put the copula before the negation particle, as in our saying 'Zayd huwa laysa bi-katib', because the copula connects what comes after it to the subject, so the negation particle and what is with it are connected, making it an affirmation. And it is simple negative if you delay the copula after it, i.e., after the negation particle, as in our saying 'Zayd laysa huwa bi-katib', because the function of the negation particle is to negate the copula that follows it, making it a negation. As for the difference between them in the bilateral proposition (which does not mention the copula), it is by intention, meaning: if one intends the connection of negation, then it is affirmative, and if one intends the negation of connection, then it is negative. And from the apparent wording, it is understood that this is a verbal difference, but it is not so.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies categorical propositions based on whether the negation particle is part of a term (modifying it) or acts on the relation (negating the copula). The author first defines modified propositions (معدولة): if the negation particle (like ليس or لا) is part of the subject only, predicate only, or both, the proposition is called modified. Examples are provided for each type. The reason for the name 'modified' is that the negation particle is turned away from its original function of simply negating the relation and instead becomes an integral part of a term. Next, the author defines determined propositions (محصلة) – affirmative propositions where the negation particle is not part of either term – and simple propositions (بسيطة) – negative propositions where the negation particle is not part of either term. Some scholars call both types 'determined' because their subject and predicate are genuine terms. The key criterion for classifying a proposition as affirmative or negative is the relation (نسبة) between subject and predicate, not the existential or non-existential nature of the terms. For example, 'Everything that is not living is not-knowing' is affirmative because it affirms 'not-knowingness' of things, even though both terms contain negation particles. Conversely, 'Nothing of the moving is at rest' is negative because it denies rest of moving things, even though both terms are positive. This shows that 'non-existential ends' means the wording contains a negation particle, not that non-existence is part of the concept. Thus, there are four proposition types: affirmative determined, negative determined, affirmative modified, and negative modified. The only confusion is between the simple negative and the affirmative modified predicate, because both have a negation particle in the wording, but in the former it negates the relation, while in the latter it is part of the predicate. For example, 'Zayd is not a writer' could be interpreted either way. The distinction is clarified in three respects: (1) In concept: affirmative modified predicates affirm the modified predicate of the subject, while simple negatives remove the predicate. (2) In material extension: the simple negative is more general than the affirmative modified predicate (the former covers all cases where the predicate is simply denied, while the latter only applies when the modified predicate is affirmed). (3) In wording: In non-Arabic languages, the copula distinguishes them (e.g., Persian 'hast' vs. 'nist'). In Arabic, for triliteral propositions (with an explicit copula like 'huwa'), if the copula precedes the negation particle, the proposition is affirmative modified (e.g., 'Zayd huwa laysa bi-katib' meaning 'Zayd is [such that he is] not-writer'), because the copula connects the entire phrase after it, making the negation part of the predicate. If the copula follows the negation particle, the proposition is simple negative (e.g., 'Zayd laysa huwa bi-katib' meaning 'Zayd is not a writer'), because the negation acts on the copula. For bilateral propositions (without an explicit copula), the distinction relies on the speaker's intention: if the intention is to affirm a negative predicate, it is affirmative modified; if to deny the predicate, it is simple negative. The author notes that this reliance on intention is not purely verbal but has conceptual implications.

محصلة
بسيطة
قضية موجبة
قضية سالبة
حرف السلب

Distinguishing the Modified Affirmative from the Simple Negative
Multiple definitions distinguishing modified affirmative from simple negative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given proposition is a modified affirmative or a simple negative based on the expectations of the subject's nature.

Core Idea
A modified affirmative affirms a negation where the subject is expected to have the negated attribute; a simple negative denies without expectation.

In logic, not all negations are the same. The modified affirmative (al-mawjuba al-ma'dula) looks like a negative because its predicate is a negation, but it is actually an affirmative proposition. The simple negative (al-saliba al-muhassala), on the other hand, is a true denial. The key difference lies in whether the subject is naturally expected to possess the negated attribute. This lesson presents multiple definitions from the tradition to sharpen your ability to distinguish them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the form of the proposition
Look at the wording: does it use 'ghayr'/'la' or 'laysa'? According to the first opinion, 'ghayr'/'la' indicates modified affirmative, 'laysa' indicates simple negative (or vice versa). This gives a quick but superficial distinction.
2
Check the expectation of the subject
Determine if the subject is normally expected to possess the attribute being negated at the time of judgment. In the second definition, a modified affirmative occurs when the subject would normally have that attribute; a simple negative occurs when the subject is not expected to have it.
Example: a man of beard age without a beard is modified affirmative; a child without a beard is simple negative.
3
Apply the expanded time frame
The third definition considers whether the subject would have the attribute at any time (before or after). This changes the classification: a child's lack of beard becomes modified affirmative because the child will grow a beard later.
Woman's lack remains simple negative under this definition.
4
Consider species and proximate genus
The fourth definition looks at whether the subject's species or proximate genus would normally have the attribute. A woman and a donkey lack beards, but their species (human for woman, donkey for donkey) or genus (mammal) normally would have beards, so their lack is modified affirmative. A tree's lack is simple negative because trees never have beards.
5
Summarize the variety of opinions
Note that different logicians define the distinction differently. The core idea remains: a modified affirmative involves an expectation that the attribute would normally be present, while a simple negative does not.
This variability shows the richness of the logical tradition.

Source Text

أو بالاصطلاح على تخصيص لفظ « غير» و « لا » بالإيجاب المعدول كقولنا: الحي غير جماد أو لا جماد، و تخصيص لفظ «ليس» بالسلب البسيط كقولنا: الحي ليس بجماد، أو بالعكس وهو تخصيص لفظ «غير » و « لا» بالسلب البسيط، ولفظ «ليس» بالإيجاب المعدول. وقيل : الفرق بينهما بأن الموجبة المعدولة هي التي محمولها عدم شيء عما شأنه من أن يكون له ذلك الشيء وقتَ الحكم والسالبة المحصلة هي التي محمولها عدم شيء عما شأنه أن يكون له ذلك الشيء في ذلك الوقت، فعدم اللحية عن إنسان في سن اللحية إيجاب معدول، وعن الطفل والمرأة سلب محصل. وقيل: الموجبة المعدولة هي التي محمولها عدم الشيء عما من شأنه أن يكون له ذلك الشيء في وقت الحكم أو قبله أو بعده، والسالبة عدم شيء عما من شأنه أن لا يكون له ذلك في وقت من الأوقات، فعلى هذا يكون عدم اللحية من الطفل إيجاب عدولي، ومن المرأة سلب محصل. وقيل: الموجبة المعدولة هي التي محمولها عدم شيء عما من شأنه أو من شأن نوعه أو جنسه القريب أن يكون له ذلك الشيء، والسالبة المحصلة عدم شيء عما ليس من شأن نوعه ولا من شأن جنسه القريب أن يكون له ذلك الشيء، فعدم اللحية من المرأة والحمار إيجاب معدول، وعدم اللحية عن الشجر سلب محصل.
Or by convention, the specification of the word 'ghayr' and 'la' for the modified affirmative, as we say: 'the living is non-inanimate or no inanimate', and the specification of the word 'laysa' for the simple negative, as we say: 'the living is not inanimate', or the opposite, which is the specification of the word 'ghayr' and 'la' for the simple negative, and the word 'laysa' for the modified affirmative. And it was said: the difference between them is that the modified affirmative is that whose predicate is the non-existence of something of what is such that it would have that thing at the time of judgment, and the simple negative is that whose predicate is the non-existence of something of what is such that it would have that thing at that time. So the non-existence of a beard for a man of beard age is a modified affirmative, and for a child and a woman it is a simple negative. And it was said: the modified affirmative is that whose predicate is the non-existence of something of what is such that it would have that thing at the time of judgment or before or after, and the simple negative is the non-existence of something of what is such that it would not have that at any time. So according to this, the non-existence of a beard for a child is a modified affirmative, and for a woman it is a simple negative. And it was said: the modified affirmative is that whose predicate is the non-existence of something of what is such that its species or its proximate genus would have that thing, and the simple negative is the non-existence of something of what is not such that its species nor its proximate genus would have that thing. So the non-existence of a beard for a woman and a donkey is a modified affirmative, and the non-existence of a beard for a tree is a simple negative.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between two types of propositions: the modified affirmative (al-mawjuba al-ma'dula) and the simple negative (al-saliba al-muhassala). The author presents three different opinions on the distinction. The first opinion distinguishes based on the words used: 'ghayr' and 'la' are for modified affirmative, 'laysa' for simple negative, or the opposite. The second opinion distinguishes based on the nature of the subject: in a modified affirmative, the predicate is the negation of something that the subject is normally expected to have at the time of judgment (e.g., a man of beard age lacking a beard), while in a simple negative, the subject is not expected to have that thing at all (e.g., a child or woman lacking a beard). The third opinion expands the time frame to before or after the judgment. The fourth opinion considers the species or proximate genus. The examples illustrate: a man of beard age without a beard is a modified affirmative (because he is expected to have one), a child and woman without a beard are simple negatives (because they are not expected to have one). However, the fourth opinion reclassifies woman and donkey as modified affirmative, showing the variation in definitions. The commentary must follow the author's presentation without adding external critique.

Foundations of Modal Propositions
Author introduces modal propositions and defines the first simple type: absolute necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the 'madda' and 'jiha' of a proposition and identify whether a proposition is modal (muwajjaha) or absolute (mutlaqa).

Core Idea
Every proposition has a modality; the aspect (jiha) is the linguistic indicator.

In this passage, the author introduces the key concepts needed to understand modal logic. He distinguishes between the underlying modality (madda) and the expression of it (jiha), and between propositions that mention the aspect (muwajjaha) and those that do not (mutlaqa). Mastering these distinctions is essential for later discussions on conversion, contradiction, and syllogism with modal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of modality
Recognize that every proposition has a modality in reality (madda) and a linguistic or mental indicator (jiha). The madda is the intrinsic modality (e.g., necessity, perpetuity), while the jiha is the explicit expression.
In earlier texts, madda and jiha were sometimes conflated; here they are clearly distinguished.
2
Define muwajjaha and mutlaqa
A proposition that includes the jiha is called muwajjaha (modal) or munawwa'a (typed), and also ruba'iyya (quadripartite) because it has four components: subject, predicate, copula, and aspect. A proposition without the jiha is called mutlaqa (absolute).
3
Analyze the four elements of a modal proposition
Note that the term ruba'iyya indicates the modal proposition has four 'letters' (elements): subject, predicate, copula, and aspect. This is a mnemonic for its structure.
4
Classify examples as modal or absolute
Take a proposition like 'Every human is an animal' – without the word 'necessarily' it is absolute. Adding 'necessarily' makes it modal. Practice with other Islamic examples such as 'Zakat is obligatory' (absolute) vs 'Zakat is necessarily obligatory' (modal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في القضايا الموجهة، واعلم أن كل نسبة بين الموضوع والمحمول - إيجابية كانت أو سلبية لها كيفية في نفس الأمر من الضرورة والدوام ومقابليهما ونحوهما، وسميت تلك الكيفية في نفس الأمر مادة القضية وعنصرها واللفظ الدال عليها أو حكم العقل بها يسمى جهة ونوعا والقضية التي ذكرت فيها الجهة تسمى موجهة ومنوعة؛ لاشتمالها على الجهة والنوع ورباعية؛ لكونها ذات أربعة أحرف، والتي لم تذكر فيها الجهة تسمى مطلقة. والقضايا الموجهة كثيرة، لكن التي جرى الاصطلاح أي: اصطلاح المنطقيين بالبحث عنها وعن أحكامها من العكس والتناقض والإنتاج، ثلاث عشرة قضية، بعضها بسيطة بالنسبة إلى المركبات، وبعضها مركبة. أما البسائط - وهي التي حقيقتها أي: معناها إيجاب فقط كقولنا: كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، أو سلب فقط كقولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة، أي: لا يكون فيها إلا حكم واحد إيجاب أو سلب فستة: الأولى: الضرورية المطلقة وهي التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع هذا في الموجبة أو بضرورة سلبه أي سلب المحمول عنه أي عن الموضوع، هذا في السالبة ما دام ذات الموضوع أي ما يصدق عليه الموضوع موجودا في الخارج أو في الذهن، فلا ينتقض بقولنا: لا شيء من الممتنع بموجود بالضرورة، ولا يرد النقض بالقضية الممكنة الخاصة التي محمولها الوجود؛ لأن الضرورة ههنا إنما تتحقق بشرط وجود الموضوع، لا في جميع أوقات وجود الموضوع، وبينهما بون بعيد. كقولنا: بالضرورة كل إنسان حيوان هذا مثال الموجبة، وكقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر هذا مثال السالبة، وإنما سميت ضرورية؛ لاشتمالها على الضرورة ومطلقة؛ لعدم تقييد الضرورة فيها بشيء.
Section on modal propositions. Know that every relation between subject and predicate – whether affirmative or negative – has a modality in reality from necessity, perpetuity, their opposites, and the like. That modality in reality is called the matter (madda) of the proposition and its element; the word indicating it or the mind's judgment of it is called the aspect (jiha) and the type (naw'). The proposition in which the aspect is mentioned is called modal (muwajjaha) and typed (munawwa'a) because it includes the aspect and the type, and quadripartite (ruba'iyya) because it consists of four letters. The proposition in which the aspect is not mentioned is called absolute (mutlaqa). Modal propositions are many, but those which convention – i.e., the convention of logicians – has examined, regarding their rules of conversion, contradiction, and syllogistic production, are thirteen propositions, some simple relative to compound ones, and some compound. As for the simple ones – which are those whose reality (i.e., meaning) is only affirmation, as we say: 'Every human is an animal necessarily', or only negation, as we say: 'No human is a stone necessarily' – i.e., there is in them only one judgment, affirmative or negative – they are six. The first: the absolute necessary (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa), which is that in which one judges the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject – this in the affirmative – or the necessity of its negation (i.e., the negation of the predicate from it, i.e., from the subject) – this in the negative – as long as the essence of the subject (i.e., what the subject applies to) exists, externally or in the mind. So it is not invalidated by our saying: 'Nothing impossible is existent necessarily' and the objection is not raised by the particular possible proposition whose predicate is existence, because necessity here is realized only with the condition of the subject's existence, not in all times of the subject's existence, and between them is a great difference. As we say: 'Of necessity, every human is an animal' – this is an example of the affirmative; and 'Of necessity, no human is a stone' – this is an example of the negative. It is called necessary because it includes necessity, and absolute because the necessity is not restricted by anything.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage introduces modal propositions (al-qadaya al-muwajjaha). The author begins by defining the concepts: every proposition, affirmative or negative, has a modality in reality (kayfiyya fi nafs al-amr) such as necessity, perpetuity, etc. This modality is called the 'matter' (madda) of the proposition, and the linguistic expression indicating it is called the 'aspect' (jiha) or 'type' (naw'). A proposition that explicitly mentions the aspect is called modal (muwajjaha) and also 'quadripartite' because it consists of four elements: subject, predicate, copula, and aspect. A proposition without aspect is called absolute (mutlaqa). The author then states that there are thirteen modal propositions that logicians study regarding their rules of conversion, contradiction, and syllogistic production. These are divided into simple and compound. The simple ones, which involve only a single judgment (affirmation or negation) are six. The first of these six is the 'absolute necessary' (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa). This proposition judges the necessity of the predicate's affirmation or negation as long as the subject's essence exists (either in the external world or in the mind). The author clarifies that this necessity is conditional upon the subject's existence, not a timeless necessity. Thus, the proposition 'Nothing impossible is existent necessarily' does not refute this, because the impossible does not exist. Similarly, the proposition 'Every possible thing exists necessarily' is not a counterexample because necessity here is only with the condition of the subject's existence. The author provides examples: 'Of necessity, every human is an animal' (affirmative) and 'Of necessity, no human is a stone' (negative). The proposition is called 'necessary' because it includes necessity, and 'absolute' because the necessity is not restricted by any further condition.

Key Takeaway
Modal propositions explicitly mention the modality (jiha), while absolute propositions do not; the modality itself is the 'madda' in reality.

The Absolute Necessary Proposition
Author introduces modal propositions and defines the first simple type: absolute necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the absolute necessary proposition and apply its condition that necessity holds as long as the subject exists.

Core Idea
Absolute necessary is the first simple modal, asserting necessity with the condition of subject existence.

After establishing the general framework, the author defines the first of the six simple modal propositions: the absolute necessary. This proposition judges the necessity of the predicate for the subject, but only as long as the subject exists. Understanding this condition is crucial to avoid counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of absolute necessary
Find where the author says it judges necessity of affirmation or negation 'as long as the subject's essence exists' (ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودا). This is the core condition.
2
Distinguish conditional necessity from timeless necessity
Note that the necessity here is not at all times, but only while the subject exists. For example, 'Every human is an animal necessarily' holds only as long as humans exist.
The author warns against confusing this with 'in all times of the subject's existence' – a different type of necessity.
3
Understand the defense against counterexamples
The author addresses two potential objections: the proposition about impossible things (e.g., 'Nothing impossible is existent necessarily') and the particular possible proposition about existence. He explains they do not refute the definition because necessity is only required under the condition of the subject's existence.
For impossible things, the subject does not exist, so the condition is not applicable.
4
Study the provided examples
The author gives two examples: affirmative (every human is an animal necessarily) and negative (no human is a stone necessarily). Both satisfy the condition: as long as humans exist, the predicate holds necessarily.
5
Analyze why it is called 'absolute'
The proposition is called 'absolute' because the necessity is not restricted by any further condition such as a specific time or state. It is simply conditional on the subject's existence.
Later simple modals add conditions like 'as long as the subject is described as such' (al-daruriyya bi-shart al-wasf) etc.

فصل في القضايا الموجهة، واعلم أن كل نسبة بين الموضوع والمحمول - إيجابية كانت أو سلبية لها كيفية في نفس الأمر من الضرورة والدوام ومقابليهما ونحوهما، وسميت تلك الكيفية في نفس الأمر مادة القضية وعنصرها واللفظ الدال عليها أو حكم العقل بها يسمى جهة ونوعا والقضية التي ذكرت فيها الجهة تسمى موجهة ومنوعة؛ لاشتمالها على الجهة والنوع ورباعية؛ لكونها ذات أربعة أحرف، والتي لم تذكر فيها الجهة تسمى مطلقة. والقضايا الموجهة كثيرة، لكن التي جرى الاصطلاح أي: اصطلاح المنطقيين بالبحث عنها وعن أحكامها من العكس والتناقض والإنتاج، ثلاث عشرة قضية، بعضها بسيطة بالنسبة إلى المركبات، وبعضها مركبة. أما البسائط - وهي التي حقيقتها أي: معناها إيجاب فقط كقولنا: كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، أو سلب فقط كقولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة، أي: لا يكون فيها إلا حكم واحد إيجاب أو سلب فستة: الأولى: الضرورية المطلقة وهي التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع هذا في الموجبة أو بضرورة سلبه أي سلب المحمول عنه أي عن الموضوع، هذا في السالبة ما دام ذات الموضوع أي ما يصدق عليه الموضوع موجودا في الخارج أو في الذهن، فلا ينتقض بقولنا: لا شيء من الممتنع بموجود بالضرورة، ولا يرد النقض بالقضية الممكنة الخاصة التي محمولها الوجود؛ لأن الضرورة ههنا إنما تتحقق بشرط وجود الموضوع، لا في جميع أوقات وجود الموضوع، وبينهما بون بعيد. كقولنا: بالضرورة كل إنسان حيوان هذا مثال الموجبة، وكقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر هذا مثال السالبة، وإنما سميت ضرورية؛ لاشتمالها على الضرورة ومطلقة؛ لعدم تقييد الضرورة فيها بشيء.
Section on modal propositions. Know that every relation between subject and predicate – whether affirmative or negative – has a modality in reality from necessity, perpetuity, their opposites, and the like. That modality in reality is called the matter (madda) of the proposition and its element; the word indicating it or the mind's judgment of it is called the aspect (jiha) and the type (naw'). The proposition in which the aspect is mentioned is called modal (muwajjaha) and typed (munawwa'a) because it includes the aspect and the type, and quadripartite (ruba'iyya) because it consists of four letters. The proposition in which the aspect is not mentioned is called absolute (mutlaqa). Modal propositions are many, but those which convention – i.e., the convention of logicians – has examined, regarding their rules of conversion, contradiction, and syllogistic production, are thirteen propositions, some simple relative to compound ones, and some compound. As for the simple ones – which are those whose reality (i.e., meaning) is only affirmation, as we say: 'Every human is an animal necessarily', or only negation, as we say: 'No human is a stone necessarily' – i.e., there is in them only one judgment, affirmative or negative – they are six. The first: the absolute necessary (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa), which is that in which one judges the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject – this in the affirmative – or the necessity of its negation (i.e., the negation of the predicate from it, i.e., from the subject) – this in the negative – as long as the essence of the subject (i.e., what the subject applies to) exists, externally or in the mind. So it is not invalidated by our saying: 'Nothing impossible is existent necessarily' and the objection is not raised by the particular possible proposition whose predicate is existence, because necessity here is realized only with the condition of the subject's existence, not in all times of the subject's existence, and between them is a great difference. As we say: 'Of necessity, every human is an animal' – this is an example of the affirmative; and 'Of necessity, no human is a stone' – this is an example of the negative. It is called necessary because it includes necessity, and absolute because the necessity is not restricted by anything.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage introduces modal propositions (al-qadaya al-muwajjaha). The author begins by defining the concepts: every proposition, affirmative or negative, has a modality in reality (kayfiyya fi nafs al-amr) such as necessity, perpetuity, etc. This modality is called the 'matter' (madda) of the proposition, and the linguistic expression indicating it is called the 'aspect' (jiha) or 'type' (naw'). A proposition that explicitly mentions the aspect is called modal (muwajjaha) and also 'quadripartite' because it consists of four elements: subject, predicate, copula, and aspect. A proposition without aspect is called absolute (mutlaqa). The author then states that there are thirteen modal propositions that logicians study regarding their rules of conversion, contradiction, and syllogistic production. These are divided into simple and compound. The simple ones, which involve only a single judgment (affirmation or negation) are six. The first of these six is the 'absolute necessary' (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa). This proposition judges the necessity of the predicate's affirmation or negation as long as the subject's essence exists (either in the external world or in the mind). The author clarifies that this necessity is conditional upon the subject's existence, not a timeless necessity. Thus, the proposition 'Nothing impossible is existent necessarily' does not refute this, because the impossible does not exist. Similarly, the proposition 'Every possible thing exists necessarily' is not a counterexample because necessity here is only with the condition of the subject's existence. The author provides examples: 'Of necessity, every human is an animal' (affirmative) and 'Of necessity, no human is a stone' (negative). The proposition is called 'necessary' because it includes necessity, and 'absolute' because the necessity is not restricted by any further condition.

Understanding Modal Propositions: Matter vs Mode
Author defines modal propositions and their components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the 'matter' (māda) and 'mode' (jiha) of a proposition and identify whether a given proposition is modal or absolute.

Core Idea
The mode is the verbal/mind-indicator; the matter is the modal reality itself.

In this passage, the author introduces a crucial distinction for understanding modal logic: every proposition has a modal quality in reality, called its 'matter', but we often need a word or mental judgment to flag it—that's the 'mode'. By learning to separate these two, you will be able to analyze propositions more precisely, especially when dealing with necessity, possibility, and their opposites in Islamic theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic parts of the passage
Read the passage and locate where the author explains that every relation (between subject and predicate) has a quality in reality (kayfiyya fī nafs al-amr). This quality includes modality like necessity, perpetuity, etc.
The passage says 'لها كيفية في نفس الأمر' — this is the starting point.
2
Extract the definition of 'matter' (māda)
Find the sentence where the author says that quality in reality is called 'matter' (māda) and 'element' ('unṣur) of the proposition. This is the real modal property.
Māda is the ontological modality, not the expression.
3
Extract the definition of 'mode' (jiha)
Find where the author says the expression indicating that quality, or the mind's judgment upon it, is called 'mode' (jiha) and 'type' (nawʿ). This is the linguistic or mental indicator.
Jiha is the verbal or conceptual marker.
4
Distinguish modal vs absolute propositions
The author says a proposition that mentions the mode is 'modal' (mujawwaha) and one that does not is 'absolute' (muṭlaqa). Practice identifying whether a given statement includes an explicit modal qualifier.
Example: 'Every human is an animal necessarily' is modal; 'Every human is an animal' is absolute.
5
Account for the thirteen modal propositions
The author mentions that logicians customarily study thirteen modal propositions. Although not detailed here, note that these include simple and compound types, setting the stage for later lessons.
This is a preview; focus on the basic dichotomy first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في القضايا الموجهة، واعلم أن كل نسبة بين الموضوع والمحمول - إيجابية كانت أو سلبية لها كيفية في نفس الأمر من الضرورة والدوام ومقابليهما ونحوهما، وسميت تلك الكيفية في نفس الأمر مادة القضية وعنصرها واللفظ الدال عليها أو حكم العقل بها يسمى جهة ونوعا والقضية التي ذكرت فيها الجهة تسمى موجهة ومنوعة؛ لاشتمالها على الجهة والنوع ورباعية؛ لكونها ذات أربعة أحرف، والتي لم تذكر فيها الجهة تسمى مطلقة. والقضايا الموجهة كثيرة، لكن التي جرى (الاصطلاح) أي: اصطلاح المنطقيين بالبحث عنها أي عن القضايا الموجهة، وعن أحكامها من العكس والتناقض والإنتاج، (ثلاث عشرة قضية، بعضها بسيطة بالنسبة إلى المركبات، وبعضها (مركبة).
Chapter on modal propositions. Know that every relation between subject and predicate—whether affirmative or negative—has a quality in reality, of necessity, perpetuity, their opposites, and the like. That quality in reality is called the matter and element of the proposition, and the expression indicating it or the mind's judgment upon it is called mode and type. The proposition in which the mode is mentioned is called modal and typed, because it includes the mode and type, and quadruple because it has four letters [in Arabic: mūjaha]. The one in which the mode is not mentioned is called absolute. Modal propositions are numerous, but those which are customary (i.e., the custom of logicians) to investigate—that is, to investigate the modal propositions and their rulings of conversion, contradiction, and syllogistic production—are thirteen propositions, some of which are simple relative to the compound ones, and some are compound.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage introduces modal propositions in logic. The author explains that every categorical proposition (whether affirmative or negative) has a modal quality in reality, such as necessity, perpetuity, or their opposites. This inherent quality is called the 'matter' (māda) or 'element' ('unṣur) of the proposition. The linguistic term that expresses this quality, or the mental judgment about it, is called the 'mode' (jiha) or 'type' (nawʿ). A proposition that explicitly includes this mode is termed 'modal' (mujawwaha) or 'typed' (munawwaʿa), and also 'quadruple' because the word 'mujawwaha' has four letters in Arabic. A proposition without an explicit mode is 'absolute' (muṭlaqa). The author then states that although there are many modal propositions, logicians have customarily focused on thirteen specific ones, which are divided into simple and compound. This sets the stage for the detailed discussion of each type. The examples used later in the text (e.g., 'Every human is an animal necessarily') are standard logical examples rooted in the Islamic intellectual tradition, relating to ontology and causation.

The Absolute Necessary Proposition: Definition and Conditions
Definition of absolute necessary with condition and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the absolute necessary proposition and explain why its necessity depends on the subject's existence.

Core Idea
Absolute necessity applies only as long as the subject exists, not unconditionally.

Modal propositions are the building blocks of precise logical reasoning in Islamic thought. The first simple modal introduced by the author is the 'absolute necessary' (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa). Understanding this proposition is essential because many theological and juristic arguments rely on necessity claims. However, the necessity here is not universal – it is tied to the existence of the subject. This lesson unpacks that condition and shows why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition's domain
The absolute necessary proposition belongs to the six simple modals. It contains only one judgment: either affirmative or negative. Notice the author defines it as 'that in which is judged the necessity of the predicate's affirming the subject' (in affirmative) or 'negating the predicate' (in negative).
Simple modals have no internal quantification conditions.
2
Extract the temporal condition
The key phrase is 'as long as the subject's essence exists' (mā dāma dhātu l-mawḍūʿ mawjūdan). This condition limits necessity to periods when the subject has actual existence – either externally or mentally. The proposition does not claim necessity when the subject does not exist.
This is the defining differentia of absolute necessary: existence-contingent necessity.
3
Test the condition with examples
Take the affirmative example: 'Every human is an animal necessarily'. For any human that actually exists (in reality or mind), being an animal is necessary. But if no humans exist, the proposition is not about necessity at all. Similarly for the negative: 'No human is a stone necessarily' – as long as a human exists, he is necessarily not a stone.
The examples are from the Islamic logical tradition, relating to essential definitions.
4
Recognize what objections are blocked
The author pre-empts two objections. First, 'Nothing impossible is existent necessarily' is not a counterexample because the impossible has no subject existence to begin with. Second, the 'special possible' proposition (e.g., 'Every human is possibly existent') is not necessary. These show that the condition is not trivial.
Understanding these objections sharpens your grasp of modal conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما البسائط - وهي التي حقيقتها أي: معناها إيجاب فقط كقولنا: كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، أو سلب فقط كقولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة، أي لا يكون فيها إلا حكم واحد إيجاب أو سلب فستة: الأولى: (الضرورية المطلقة) وهي التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع هذا في الموجبة (أو) بضرورة (سلبه) أي سلب المحمول (عنه) أي عن الموضوع، هذا في السالبة ما دام ذات الموضوع أي ما يصدق عليه الموضوع (موجودا) في الخارج أو في الذهن، فلا ينتقض بقولنا: لا شيء من الممتنع بموجود بالضرورة، ولا يرد النقض بالقضية الممكنة الخاصة التي محمولها الوجود؛ لأن الضرورة ههنا إنما تتحقق بشرط وجود الموضوع، لا في جميع أوقات وجود الموضوع، وبينهما بون بعيد. كقولنا: بالضرورة كل إنسان حيوان هذا مثال الموجبة (و) كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر هذا مثال السالبة، وإنما سميت ضرورية لاشتمالها على الضرورة ومطلقة لعدم تقييد الضرورة فيها بشيء.
As for the simple ones—which are those whose reality (i.e., meaning) is only affirmation, like our saying: 'Every human is an animal necessarily', or only negation, like our saying: 'No human is a stone necessarily', meaning there is in them only one judgment, affirmation or negation—they are six. The first: (the absolute necessary) which is that in which is judged the necessity of the predicate's affirming the subject—this in the affirmative—or the necessity of its negation (i.e., the predicate's negation) from it (i.e., the subject)—this in the negative—as long as the subject's essence (i.e., that which the subject applies to) exists, externally or mentally. So it is not invalidated by our saying: 'Nothing impossible is existent necessarily', nor does the refutation apply by the special possible proposition whose predicate is existence, because necessity here is realized only with the condition of the subject's existence, not in all times of the subject's existence, and between them there is a great distance. Like our saying: 'Necessarily every human is an animal'—this is an example of the affirmative; and our saying: 'Necessarily no human is a stone'—this is an example of the negative. It is called necessary because it includes necessity, and absolute because the necessity is not restricted by anything.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage defines the first of the simple modal propositions: the 'absolute necessary' (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa). The author clarifies that simple propositions contain only one judgment, either affirmative or negative. The absolute necessary proposition asserts the necessity of the predicate's relation to the subject throughout the duration of the subject's existence, whether externally or in the mind. The author provides examples: 'Every human is an animal necessarily' (affirmative) and 'No human is a stone necessarily' (negative). Importantly, the necessity here is conditional on the subject's existence; it does not apply to times when the subject does not exist. This avoids objections like 'Nothing impossible is existent necessarily'—since the impossible has no subject existence—or the special possible proposition that ascribes existence as a predicate, which would not be necessary but possible. The proposition is thus named 'necessary' for including necessity, and 'absolute' because the necessity is not restricted by any additional condition (like a description). The Quranic or theological example of humans being animals is a standard logical illustration from the Islamic tradition, relating to composition of humans from animal and rational aspects.

Key Takeaway
