Defining the Absolute Perpetual Proposition
Author defines the absolute perpetual proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an absolute perpetual proposition and construct examples in both affirmative and negative forms.

Core Idea
Perpetuity means the predicate holds at every moment the subject exists—no more, no less.

We now turn to the second simple modal: the absolute perpetual. Unlike the necessary, this proposition only asserts that the predicate holds (or is negated) for as long as the subject exists. It does not imply that it must be so. In this lesson, we will dissect its definition and apply it to familiar examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Definition
Locate the definition in Arabic: 'which is that in which is judged the perpetuity of the predicate's affirming the subject... as long as its essence exists.' Understand that it judges perpetual affirmation in affirmative and perpetual negation in negative.
2
Identify the Two Forms
Note that for affirmative, it judges perpetuity of affirmation; for negative, perpetuity of negation. Both are absolute perpetual.
3
Check the Time Condition
The perpetuity is conditional on the subject's existence ('as long as its essence exists'). If the subject ceases to exist, the proposition does not apply.
4
Review the Examples
The author gives 'perpetually every human is an animal' and 'perpetually no human is a stone'. Confirm they fit: as long as humans exist, they are animals; as long as humans exist, they are not stones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية: (الدائمة المطلقة) وهي التي حكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع هذا في الموجبة، (أو) حكم فيها بدوام (سلبه) أي سلب المحمول (عنه) أي عن الموضوع، هذا في السالبة ما دام ذاته أي ذات الموضوع (موجودة) خارجا أو ذهنا، (وقد مر مثالها إيجابا وسلبا في الضرورية المطلقة، وهو قولنا دائما كل إنسان حيوان، ودائما لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر. وهي أعم من الضرورية المطلقة مطلقا؛ لأن الضرورة بحسب الذات تستلزم الدوام بحسبها من غير عكس كلي؛ لأن معنى الضرورة امتناع انفكاك النسبة، ومعنى الدوام شمول الأزمنة والأوقات، فمتى تحقق الأول تحقق الثاني من غير عكس كلي؛ لجواز أن يكون دائما ولا يمتنع انفكاكها، والمراد بكون الدائمة أعم من الضرورية أن علة الدوام غير ملحوظة بها للحاكم، فلا يحكم بالضرورة، فلا يرد ما قيل: إن الدائمة يجب أن تكون مساوية للضرورية؛ لأن دوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أمر ممكن يحتاج إلى علة دائمة، فيكون ثبوت المحمول للموضوع ضروريا؛ لدوام علته فافهم وإنما سميت دائمة لاشتمالها على الدوام، ومطلقة؛ لما مر.
The second: (the absolute perpetual) which is that in which is judged the perpetuity of the predicate's affirming the subject—this in the affirmative—or the perpetuity of its negation (i.e., the predicate's negation) from it (i.e., the subject)—this in the negative—as long as its essence (i.e., the subject's essence) exists, externally or mentally. Its example in affirmative and negative has already passed in the absolute necessary: our saying 'perpetually every human is an animal' and 'perpetually no human is a stone'. And it is more general than the absolute necessary in every respect; because necessity in respect of essence entails perpetuity in respect of it, without total reciprocal implication; because the meaning of necessity is the impossibility of separation of the relation, and the meaning of perpetuity is the encompassing of times and periods. So whenever the first is realized, the second is realized, without total reciprocal implication, because it is possible that something is perpetual and yet its separation is not impossible. The intention in the perpetual being more general than the necessary is that the cause of perpetuity is not considered by the one judging, so he does not judge necessity. Thus it is not refuted by what is said: that the perpetual must be equivalent to the necessary, because the perpetuity of the predicate's affirming the subject is a possible matter that needs a perpetual cause, so the predicate's affirming the subject would be necessary due to the perpetuity of its cause. So understand. It is called perpetual because it includes perpetuity, and absolute for what has passed.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines the second simple modal: the 'absolute perpetual' (al-dāʾima al-muṭlaqa). It judges that the predicate perpetually holds for the subject (or is perpetually negated) as long as the subject exists. The examples are similar to the necessary: 'perpetually every human is an animal' and 'perpetually no human is a stone'. However, the perpetual is more general than the necessary. Why? Because necessity implies perpetuity, but perpetuity does not imply necessity. Necessity means the relation cannot be separated; perpetuity means the relation holds at all times. Every necessary relation is perpetual (since if it cannot be separated, it holds at all times), but a relation can be perpetual without being necessary—for instance, it could be that the predicate always holds but still could possibly not hold (i.e., it is not impossible to separate). The author then addresses a potential objection: one might argue that if a predicate is perpetual, it must have a perpetual cause, and thus the relation is necessary. The author replies that in the perpetual proposition, the judge does not consider the cause; he only affirms the perpetuity without judging the modality of necessity. Therefore, the perpetual remains more general. This is a subtle point in modal logic. The text emphasizes the distinction between judging mere temporal continuity and judging essential necessity. The example from Islamic theology: the perpetuity of creation's dependence on God can be perpetual but not necessary in itself, as it is contingent on God's will.

Key Terms
دائمة مطلقة
دوام
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The predicate holds necessarily for the subject.
B) The predicate holds at all times the subject exists, without implying necessity.
C) The predicate holds only at some times.
D) The predicate is negated only when the subject does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The absolute perpetual proposition affirms or denies the predicate for the subject at every moment of the subject's existence, without committing to necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a perpetual proposition about a created being (e.g., 'Fire is perpetually hot' as long as fire exists) and identify whether it is affirmative or negative.
?

Why Perpetuity Does Not Entail Necessity
Author explains the generality of the perpetual over the necessary and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the absolute perpetual is more general than the absolute necessary, and address the objection regarding a perpetual cause.

Core Idea
Necessity implies perpetuity, but perpetuity does not imply necessity—they are not equivalent.

The author clarifies a crucial distinction: while every necessary proposition is perpetual, not every perpetual proposition is necessary. This has subtle implications, as a potential objection argues that perpetuity must have a cause, which would make it necessary. The author shows why this objection fails. In this lesson, we will trace the logical relationship and apply it to Islamic theological contexts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Claim
The author states: 'It (the perpetual) is more general than the absolute necessary in every respect.' This is the main claim: the perpetual proposition has a wider extension.
2
Reason Through the Entailment
Necessity means the relation cannot be separated; perpetuity means it holds at all times. If it cannot be separated, it must hold at all times, so necessity implies perpetuity.
3
Consider the Converse
Could something be perpetual without being necessary? Yes, because the relation might always hold but be separable in principle (e.g., a contingent fact that always obtains). The author gives the example: 'it is possible that something is perpetual and yet its separation is not impossible.'
4
Examine the Objection and Reply
Someone might object: if the predicate is perpetual, it must have a perpetual cause, making the relation necessary. The author replies that in the perpetual proposition, the judge does not consider the cause; he only judges the perpetuity. So the perpetual proposition does not assert necessity.
5
Apply to Islamic Example
Consider 'Perpetually every human is changing'. It is perpetual (humans are always changing) but not necessary, because human nature could have been otherwise if God willed. This illustrates that perpetuity does not entail necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية: (الدائمة المطلقة) وهي التي حكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع هذا في الموجبة، (أو) حكم فيها بدوام (سلبه) أي سلب المحمول (عنه) أي عن الموضوع، هذا في السالبة ما دام ذاته أي ذات الموضوع (موجودة) خارجا أو ذهنا، (وقد مر مثالها إيجابا وسلبا في الضرورية المطلقة، وهو قولنا دائما كل إنسان حيوان، ودائما لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر. وهي أعم من الضرورية المطلقة مطلقا؛ لأن الضرورة بحسب الذات تستلزم الدوام بحسبها من غير عكس كلي؛ لأن معنى الضرورة امتناع انفكاك النسبة، ومعنى الدوام شمول الأزمنة والأوقات، فمتى تحقق الأول تحقق الثاني من غير عكس كلي؛ لجواز أن يكون دائما ولا يمتنع انفكاكها، والمراد بكون الدائمة أعم من الضرورية أن علة الدوام غير ملحوظة بها للحاكم، فلا يحكم بالضرورة، فلا يرد ما قيل: إن الدائمة يجب أن تكون مساوية للضرورية؛ لأن دوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أمر ممكن يحتاج إلى علة دائمة، فيكون ثبوت المحمول للموضوع ضروريا؛ لدوام علته فافهم وإنما سميت دائمة لاشتمالها على الدوام، ومطلقة؛ لما مر.
The second: (the absolute perpetual) which is that in which is judged the perpetuity of the predicate's affirming the subject—this in the affirmative—or the perpetuity of its negation (i.e., the predicate's negation) from it (i.e., the subject)—this in the negative—as long as its essence (i.e., the subject's essence) exists, externally or mentally. Its example in affirmative and negative has already passed in the absolute necessary: our saying 'perpetually every human is an animal' and 'perpetually no human is a stone'. And it is more general than the absolute necessary in every respect; because necessity in respect of essence entails perpetuity in respect of it, without total reciprocal implication; because the meaning of necessity is the impossibility of separation of the relation, and the meaning of perpetuity is the encompassing of times and periods. So whenever the first is realized, the second is realized, without total reciprocal implication, because it is possible that something is perpetual and yet its separation is not impossible. The intention in the perpetual being more general than the necessary is that the cause of perpetuity is not considered by the one judging, so he does not judge necessity. Thus it is not refuted by what is said: that the perpetual must be equivalent to the necessary, because the perpetuity of the predicate's affirming the subject is a possible matter that needs a perpetual cause, so the predicate's affirming the subject would be necessary due to the perpetuity of its cause. So understand. It is called perpetual because it includes perpetuity, and absolute for what has passed.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines the second simple modal: the 'absolute perpetual' (al-dāʾima al-muṭlaqa). It judges that the predicate perpetually holds for the subject (or is perpetually negated) as long as the subject exists. The examples are similar to the necessary: 'perpetually every human is an animal' and 'perpetually no human is a stone'. However, the perpetual is more general than the necessary. Why? Because necessity implies perpetuity, but perpetuity does not imply necessity. Necessity means the relation cannot be separated; perpetuity means the relation holds at all times. Every necessary relation is perpetual (since if it cannot be separated, it holds at all times), but a relation can be perpetual without being necessary—for instance, it could be that the predicate always holds but still could possibly not hold (i.e., it is not impossible to separate). The author then addresses a potential objection: one might argue that if a predicate is perpetual, it must have a perpetual cause, and thus the relation is necessary. The author replies that in the perpetual proposition, the judge does not consider the cause; he only affirms the perpetuity without judging the modality of necessity. Therefore, the perpetual remains more general. This is a subtle point in modal logic. The text emphasizes the distinction between judging mere temporal continuity and judging essential necessity. The example from Islamic theology: the perpetuity of creation's dependence on God can be perpetual but not necessary in itself, as it is contingent on God's will.

Key Terms
دائمة مطلقة
دوام
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the absolute perpetual more general than the absolute necessary?
A) Because necessity implies perpetuity, but perpetuity does not imply necessity.
B) Because perpetuity implies necessity, but necessity does not imply perpetuity.
C) Because they are equivalent.
D) Because the necessary cannot be perpetual.

Key Takeaway
Necessity entails perpetuity, but perpetuity does not entail necessity; the perpetual is thus the broader modal proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'Perpetually every created thing is dependent on God'. Is it necessary or perpetual? Explain using the distinction from this lesson.
?
Construct an argument defending the claim that 'The world is perpetually changing' is more general than 'The world is necessarily changing'. Mention why the latter adds an impossibility of being otherwise.

The General Conditional Proposition in Islamic Logic
Author defines the 'general conditional' proposition with examples and naming reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a general conditional proposition and distinguish it from an absolute necessary proposition.

Core Idea
A general conditional ties necessity to a specific description of the subject, not to its essence.

In this passage, al-Badīʿ defines the third simple modal proposition: the general conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma). As you will see, this proposition is crucial when a property is necessary only under a specific condition, such as movement for a writer while writing. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly analyzing arguments in Islamic logic, especially in contexts where conditional necessity is involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the Arabic name for this proposition in the passage. It is 'المشروطة العامة' (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma). Note that it is called 'conditional' because it includes a condition (sharṭ) and 'general' because it is broader than the special conditional.
2
Extract the condition that makes it conditional
The condition is 'شرط الوصف' (condition of description): the predicate is necessary only while the subject possesses a specific description (waṣf). For example, 'writer' is the description in the given example.
3
Understand why it is called 'general'
The passage says it is 'general' because it is more general than the 'special conditional' that will come later. The special conditional has additional restrictions, making the general conditional broader in scope.
4
Examine the affirmative and negative examples
Work through the affirmative example: 'Necessarily every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.' The necessity holds only under the description 'writer'. Similarly, the negative: 'Necessarily nothing of the writer is still of fingers as long as he is a writer.' Notice that the necessity is tied to the condition.
The negative example in the source appears truncated; the pattern is parallel to the affirmative.
5
Compare with absolute necessary proposition
An absolute necessary (ḍarūrī muṭlaq) judges necessity without any condition, e.g., 'Necessarily every human is an animal.' In the general conditional, necessity depends on the description. This distinction is critical in modal logic applications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة: (المشروطة (العامة وإنما سميت مشروطة لاشتمالها على شرط الوصف وعامة؛ لأنها أعم من المشروطة الخاصة كما ستجيء في المركبات. (وهي) أي: المشروط العامة (التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع ) هذا في الموجبة، (أو) بضرورة (سلبه) أي: سلب المحمول (عنه) أي: عن الموضوع، هذا في السالبة، بشرط وصفه أي: وصف الموضوع، أي: يكون للوصف مدخل في الضرورة، (كقولنا: بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا هذا مثال الموجبة، (و) كقولنا: (بالضرورة لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا.
The third: (the conditional (general). It is called conditional because it includes a condition of description, and general because it is more general than the special conditional as will come in the compound ones. And it (i.e., the general conditional) is that in which is judged the necessity of the predicate's affirming the subject—this in the affirmative—or the necessity of its negation (i.e., the predicate's negation) from it (i.e., the subject)—this in the negative—on condition of its description (i.e., the subject's description), meaning that the description has an entry in the necessity. Like our saying: 'Necessarily every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer'—this is an example of the affirmative; and our saying: 'Necessarily nothing of the writer is still of fingers as long as he is a writer'.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines the third simple modal: the 'general conditional' (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma). It is called 'conditional' because it includes a condition related to a description (waṣf) of the subject, and 'general' because it is broader than the 'special conditional' that will be discussed among compound propositions. The proposition judges the necessity of the predicate for the subject, but the necessity is conditional upon the subject being described by a specific attribute. For example: 'Necessarily every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' — the necessity of finger movement holds only while the subject is actually writing. The negative example is similar: 'Necessarily nothing of the writer is still of fingers as long as he is a writer'. The key point is that the description (being a writer) is a condition for the necessity; the predicate is not necessary absolutely, but only under that description. This is distinct from the absolute necessary which is unconditional. In Islamic logic, such propositions are used in arguments where a property is necessary only under certain conditions, e.g., the necessity of movement for a living being when it is alive, not after death.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
شرط الوصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What makes the 'general conditional' proposition conditional (mashrūṭa)?
A) It includes a condition of the predicate
B) It includes a condition of the subject's description
C) It includes a temporal condition
D) It is dependent on the existence of the subject

Key Takeaway
The general conditional proposition requires the predicate to be necessary only under a specific description of the subject, not absolutely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each proposition is a general conditional or absolute necessary: (a) 'Necessarily every human is an animal as long as he is human.' (b) 'Necessarily every human is an animal.' Provide reasoning.
?
Write a general conditional proposition relevant to Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh) involving a condition such as 'prayer' or 'fasting'. For example, 'Necessarily every person in prayer is facing the qibla as long as he is praying.'

The Absolute Perpetual Proposition (al-Dā'ima al-Muṭlaqa)
Definition and comparison of perpetual proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the absolute perpetual proposition from the absolute necessary proposition by analyzing the definition and the relationship between perpetuity and necessity.

Core Idea
Perpetuity does not imply necessity: a judgment can hold at all times without being necessary.

So far you have learned about necessary propositions. Now the author introduces a closely related but distinct type: the absolute perpetual proposition. How do we define it? What is the difference between saying something holds always (perpetually) and saying it holds necessarily? This passage clarifies the logical relationship between the two.

Guided Path
1
1. Locate the definition in the Arabic
Find the sentence starting with "الثانية: (الدائمة المطلقة)". Identify that the definition specifies the judgment as perpetual affirmation or negation, and includes the condition "ما دام ذاته موجودة" (as long as its essence exists).
2
2. Parse the temporal condition
Understand the phrase "ما دام ذاته موجودة". This means the judgment covers all times when the subject exists, not all times absolutely. This is the "perpetual" aspect.
3
3. Compare with the necessary proposition
The author says "وهي أعم من الضرورية المطلقة مطلقا". Analyze why: necessity entails perpetuity but not vice versa. Read the explanation about impossibility of separation vs. inclusiveness of times.
4
4. Examine the objection and response
An objector might argue that perpetual affirmation implies a perpetual cause, thus necessity. The author responds by noting that the judge does not consider the cause; only the constancy is judged.
This clarifies the epistemic stance of the perpetual proposition.
5
5. Apply the distinction
Given examples like "Perpetually, every human is an animal" (both perpetual and necessary) and "Perpetually, every human breathes" (perpetual but not necessary), verify which category each falls into.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية: (الدائمة المطلقة وهي التي حكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع هذا في الموجبة، (أو) حكم فيها بدوام (سلبه) أي سلب المحمول (عنه) أي: عن الموضوع، هذا في السالبة ما دام ذاته أي: ذات الموضوع (موجودة) خارجا أو ذهنا، (وقد مر مثالها إيجابا وسلبا في الضرورية المطلقة، وهو قولنا دائما(١)كل إنسان حيوان، ودائما (٢) لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر.
وهي أعم من الضرورية المطلقة مطلقا؛ لأن الضرورة بحسب الذات تستلزم الدوام بحسبها من غير عكس كلي؛ لأن معنى الضرورة امتناع انفكاك النسبة، ومعنى الدوام شمول الأزمنة والأوقات، فمتى تحقق الأول تحقق الثاني من غير عكس كلي؛ لجواز أن يكون دائما ولا يمتنع انفكاكها، والمراد بكون الدائمة أعم من الضرورية أن علة الدوام غير ملحوظة بها للحاكم، فلا يحكم بالضرورة، فلا يرد ما قيل: إن الدائمة يجب أن تكون مساوية للضرورية؛ لأن دوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أمر ممكن يحتاج إلى علة دائمة، فيكون ثبوت المحمول للموضوع ضروريا؛ لدوام علته فافهم وإنما سميت دائمة لاشتمالها على الدوام، ومطلقة؛ لما مر .
The second: The absolute perpetual (al-dā'ima al-muṭlaqa). It is that in which judgment is made of the perpetual affirmation of the predicate to the subject – this in the affirmative – or judgment is made of the perpetual negation of it, i.e. the predicate, from it, i.e. the subject – this in the negative – as long as its essence, i.e. the subject's essence, exists externally or mentally. Its example in affirmation and negation was given in the absolute necessary, namely our saying 'Perpetually, every human is an animal' and 'Perpetually, no human is a stone.' And it is more general than the absolute necessary in every respect, because necessity with respect to essence entails perpetuity with respect to it without universal converse, because the meaning of necessity is the impossibility of the separation of the relation, and the meaning of perpetuity is the inclusiveness of all times and moments. Thus whenever the first is realized, the second is realized without universal converse, because it is possible that a judgment be perpetual without the impossibility of its separation. And the intended meaning of the perpetual being more general than the necessary is that the cause of perpetuity is not considered by the judge, so he does not judge necessity; thus what was said—that the perpetual must be equal to the necessary because the perpetual affirmation of the predicate to the subject is a possible matter requiring a perpetual cause, so the affirmation of the predicate to the subject would be necessary due to the perpetuity of its cause—is not accepted. Understand this. And it is called 'perpetual' for containing perpetuity, and 'absolute' for what has passed.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of categorical proposition: the absolute perpetual (al-dā'ima al-muṭlaqa). The author begins by stating that in the affirmative form, it judges the perpetual affirmation of the predicate for the subject as long as the subject's essence exists, whether externally or mentally. In the negative form, it judges the perpetual negation of the predicate from the subject. He then gives examples: 'Perpetually, every human is an animal' and 'Perpetually, no human is a stone.' These examples mirror those of the absolute necessary proposition, but the key difference is that here the judgment is merely about constancy over all times when the subject exists, without requiring that the connection be necessary. The author then clarifies that the perpetual proposition is more general than the necessary proposition in every respect. Why? Because necessity with respect to essence entails perpetuity (if a predicate is necessarily attached to a subject, it must hold at all times the subject exists), but the converse is not always true: a predicate might hold perpetually without being necessary. For example, 'Every human breathes' might be perpetually true for living humans but is not strictly necessary (since breathing could be interrupted). The author addresses an objection: someone might argue that perpetual affirmation requires a perpetual cause, which would make the relation necessary. But the author rejects this by stating that the judge (the one making the proposition) does not consider the cause of perpetuity; the proposition merely reports observation of constancy without committing to necessity. Hence the term 'perpetual' (dā'ima) because it involves duration, and 'absolute' (muṭlaqa) as previously explained, meaning it is unconditional with respect to mode.

Key Terms
الدائمة المطلقة
الضرورية المطلقة
دوام
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the absolute perpetual proposition (الدائمة المطلقة) and the absolute necessary proposition (الضرورية المطلقة)?
A) They are equivalent; every perpetual proposition is necessary.
B) The perpetual is more general than the necessary because necessity entails perpetuity but not vice versa.
C) The necessary is more general than the perpetual because it covers all times.
D) They are unrelated; one deals with essence, the other with existence.

Key Takeaway
The absolute perpetual proposition is more general than the absolute necessary proposition because necessity entails perpetuity but perpetuity does not entail necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the following proposition: 'Every human is an animal.' According to the passage, is this proposition perpetual, necessary, or both? Explain your reasoning using the definitions.
?
Consider the proposition 'Every prophet is truthful.' (Assume prophets exist in all ages.) Is this proposition perpetual? Is it necessary? Why or why not?

The General Conditional Proposition: Two Meanings
Author defines the general conditional proposition with two meanings and compares it to other modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two meanings of the general conditional proposition and identify examples of each in Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
The general conditional ties necessity to a description, not to essence alone.

The general conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-‘āmma) is a key modal proposition in Islamic logic. It expresses necessity that is dependent on a specific description of the subject. The author presents two distinct meanings: one where the necessity is conditional on the description being present, and another where the necessity holds in all times the description exists. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting modal syllogisms in kalām and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first meaning
Read the definition: the general conditional judges the necessity of the predicate (affirmation or negation) for the subject with the condition that the subject is described by a certain attribute. The description must play a role in the necessity.
The example given: 'Every writer is necessarily a finger-mover as long as he is a writer.' The necessity of finger-movement is tied to the description 'writer'.
2
Identify the second meaning
The author also applies the term to a proposition that judges necessity in all times when the description exists. This is a stronger condition: the necessity must hold at every moment the description is present.
This meaning is broader than the first. For separable descriptions, the first holds but not necessarily the second.
3
Compare the two meanings
The author explains that the two meanings coincide when the description is the essence or a necessary attribute (e.g., 'human' or 'rational'). They diverge for separable descriptions like 'writer'.
For 'writer', the first meaning holds (necessity under the condition of writing) but the second may not, because the necessity might not be present at every moment the description exists (e.g., finger-movement is not necessary at every moment the person is a writer).
4
Analyze the relationship with other modal propositions
The first meaning is more general than the necessary and perpetual in some respects, while the second is absolutely more general than the necessary and more general than the perpetual in some respects.
This step helps place the general conditional in the broader system of modal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة: (المشروطة (العامة وإنما سميت مشروطة لاشتمالها على شرط الوصف وعامة؛ لأنها أعم من المشروطة الخاصة كما ستجيء في المركبات.
(وهي) أي: المشروط العامة (التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع ) هذا في الموجبة، (أو) بضرورة (سلبه) أي: سلب المحمول (عنه) أي: عن الموضوع، هذا في السالبة، بشرط وصفه أي: وصف الموضوع، أي: يكون للوصف مدخل في الضرورة، (كقولنا : بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا هذا مثال الموجبة، فإن تحرك الأصابع ضروري لذات الكاتب بشرط اتصافه بوصف الكتابة، (و) كقولنا: (بالضرورة لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا هذا مثال السالبة، فإن سلب سكون الأصابع عن ذات الكاتب ضروري بشرط اتصافه بالكتابة.
وقد تطلق المشروطة العامة على القضية التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه ما دام وصف الموضوع موجودا أي يحكم فيها بضرورة الثبوت أو السلب في جميع أوقات اتصاف الذات بالوصف العنواني والنسبة بين المعنيين عموم وخصوص من وجه؛ لتصادقهما في مادة الضرورة الذاتية إذا كان العنوان نفس الذات أو وصفا لازما لها، كقولنا كل إنسان أو كل ناطق حيوان بالضرورة، وصدق الأولى بدون الثانية في مادة يكون المحمول ضروريا للذات بشرط وصف مفارق، كقولنا : كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة، فإن تحرك الأصابع ضروري لذات الكاتب بشرط اتصافه بالكتابة لا في جميع أوقات الكتابة وصدقِ الثانية بدون الأولى في مادة الضرورة الذاتية، إذا كان العنوان وصفا مفارقا كقولنا كل كاتب حيوان بالضرورة.
والمشروطة العامة بالمعنى الأول أعم من الضرورية والدائمة من وجه؛ لتصادقهما في مثل قولنا : كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة أو دائما ما دام إنسانا وصدقهما دونها(١) في مثل قولنا : كل كاتب حيوان بالضرورة أو دائما، وصدقها دونهما في المثال المذكور في المتن.
وأما بالمعنى الثاني فهي أعم من الضرورية مطلقا ؛ لأنه كلما ثبت الضرورة في جميع أوقات الذات ثبت في جميع أوقات الوصف من غير عكس، ومن الدائمة من وجه؛ لتصادقهما في مادة الضرورة المطلقة وصدق الدائمة بدونها في مادة الدائمة المطلقة الخالية عن الضرورة، وصدقها بدون الدائمة حيث تكون الضرورة في جميع أوقات الوصف ولا يكون الدوام في جميع أوقات الذات.
The third: The general conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-‘āmma). It is called conditional for containing a condition of description, and general because it is more general than the particular conditional, as will come in the compound propositions. And it – i.e. the general conditional – is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject – this in the affirmative – or of the necessity of its negation – i.e. the negation of the predicate from it, i.e. from the subject – this in the negative, with the condition of its description, i.e. the description of the subject, meaning that the description has an input in the necessity. Like our saying: 'Perforce, every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer' – this is an example of the affirmative; for the movement of fingers is necessary for the essence of the writer under the condition of his being described with the description of writing. And like our saying: 'Perforce, no writer is finger-still as long as he is a writer' – this is an example of the negative; for the negation of finger-stillness from the essence of the writer is necessary under the condition of his being described with writing. And the general conditional may be applied to the proposition in which judgment is made of the necessity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject or its negation from it as long as the description of the subject exists, i.e., judgment is made of the necessity of affirmation or negation in all times of the subject's being described with the definitional description. And the relationship between the two meanings is generality and particularity in some respect; because they coincide in the matter of essential necessity when the defining description is the essence itself or a necessary attribute of it, like our saying 'Every human' or 'Every rational being is an animal by necessity'; and the first (meaning) is true without the second in a case where the predicate is necessary for the essence under the condition of a separable description, like our saying 'Every writer is a finger-mover by necessity' – for finger-movement is necessary for the essence of the writer under the condition of his being described with writing, but not in all times of writing. And the second is true without the first in a case of essential necessity when the defining description is a separable description, like our saying 'Every writer is an animal by necessity.' And the general conditional in the first meaning is more general than the necessary and the perpetual in some respect, because they coincide in examples like our saying 'Every human is an animal by necessity or perpetually as long as he is human' and they are true without it in examples like our saying 'Every writer is an animal by necessity or perpetually' and it is true without them in the example mentioned in the text. As for the second meaning, it is more general than the necessary absolutely, because whenever necessity is established in all times of the essence, it is established in all times of the description, without converse; and it is more general than the perpetual in some respect, because they coincide in the matter of absolute necessity and the perpetual is true without it in the matter of the absolute perpetual free from necessity, and it is true without the perpetual where necessity is in all times of the description but perpetuity is not in all times of the essence.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage defines the third major type of modal proposition: the general conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-‘āmma). The author explains why it is called 'conditional': because it contains a condition related to a description (waṣf) of the subject. It is 'general' because it is broader than the particular conditional, which will be discussed later. The first meaning: the proposition judges the necessity of the predicate (affirmation or negation) for the subject under the condition that the subject is described with a certain attribute. The description must be involved in the necessity. For example, 'Every writer is necessarily a finger-mover as long as he is a writer' – the movement of fingers is necessary for the writer only while he is actually writing; it is not a permanent necessity of his essence. Similarly for the negative example. The author then introduces a second meaning of the general conditional: it can also be applied to propositions that judge necessity of the predicate for the subject in all times when the description exists, i.e., whenever the subject is described with that defining attribute. This second meaning is broader than the first. The author compares the two meanings using the concepts of 'essential necessity' (al-ḍarūra al-dhātiyya) and 'separable description' (waṣf mufāriq). When the description is the essence itself (e.g., 'human') or a necessary attribute (e.g., 'rational'), the two meanings coincide. When the description is separable (e.g., 'writer'), the first meaning holds (necessity under condition of the description) but not necessarily the second (necessity in all times the description exists). Conversely, the second meaning holds in cases of essential necessity with a separable description (e.g., 'Every writer is necessarily an animal' – because animality is essential to humans, not conditional on writing). The passage then discusses the relationships between the general conditional (in both meanings) and the necessary and perpetual propositions. In the first meaning, the general conditional is more general than both in some respects, with examples showing overlap and divergence. In the second meaning, it is absolutely more general than the necessary (since necessity in all times of the essence implies necessity in all times of the description, but not vice versa) and more general than the perpetual in some respects, again with overlap and divergence in specific cases. This careful analysis is crucial for understanding modal logic in the Islamic tradition, particularly for evaluating arguments in kalām and fiqh that rely on conditional necessity.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة (al-mashrūṭa al-‘āmma): A proposition that judges the necessity of the predicate for the subject under the condition of a specific description (first meaning) or in all times when that description exists (second meaning). [Note: In some later texts, the term may refer specifically to the second meaning; this text distinguishes both.]
الضرورة الذاتية (al-ḍarūra al-dhātiyya): Necessity that arises from the essence of the subject itself, independent of any external condition. [Note: A standard term in modal logic; no significant shift.]
الوصف المفارق (al-waṣf al-mufāriq): A description that is not necessary to the essence, i.e., it can be separated from the subject without destroying its identity (e.g., 'writer' for a human). [Note: Standard usage in Islamic logic for contingent attributes.]
الوصف العنواني (al-waṣf al-‘unwānī): The defining description that serves as the subject-term in a conditional proposition; it may be the essence itself or a necessary attribute. [Note: This term appears in later commentaries to specify the description in conditional propositions.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Every writer is an animal by necessity.
D) Every human is a rational being by necessity.

Key Takeaway
The general conditional proposition expresses necessity that is dependent on a description, with two distinct interpretations: conditional necessity (first meaning) and temporal necessity (second meaning).

The General Conditional Proposition: Two Meanings and Their Relationships
Defines the general conditional proposition with two meanings and compares it with necessity and duration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two meanings of the general conditional proposition and identify examples of each in Islamic logic texts.

Core Idea
The general conditional ties necessity to a description, not to the essence.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified based on modality and time. The general conditional (al-mashruta al-ʿāmma) is a type of modal proposition that judges the necessity of a predicate for a subject as long as a certain description holds. However, this term has two distinct meanings in the tradition. This lesson unpacks both meanings and shows how they relate to essential necessity (ḍarūra dhātiyya) and perpetual (dāʾima) propositions. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly analyzing arguments that involve conditional necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings
First meaning: The judgment of necessity (affirmation or negation) is made for the subject as long as the description (title) exists. Second meaning: The judgment of necessity is made for all times the description holds, not necessarily for all times of the essence.
The second meaning is essentially a variant that focuses on the time of the description rather than the condition itself.
2
Recognize the relationship between the two meanings
The two meanings are 'general and specific from an aspect' – they coincide in some cases and diverge in others. The author provides examples where they agree (essential necessity) and where they differ (separable description).
3
Examine examples for each meaning
For the first meaning: 'Every writer is a finger-mover necessarily' – the necessity is conditional on the description 'writer'. For the second meaning: 'Every writer is an animal necessarily' – the necessity is due to essential necessity, but the description 'writer' is separable; the proposition is true at all times the subject is described as a writer.
The example 'Every writer is an animal necessarily' is used to show that the second meaning can be true without the first in cases of essential necessity with a separable description.
4
Compare with necessary and perpetual propositions
The first meaning is more general than the necessary and perpetual from an aspect: they coincide in cases where the description is essential, but diverge when the description is separable. The second meaning is absolutely more general than the necessary, and more general than the perpetual from an aspect.
Understanding these relationships helps in evaluating the scope of modal claims in arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تطلق المشروطة العامة على القضية التي يحكم فيهـا بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه ما دام وصف الموضوع موجودا أي يحكم فيها بضرورة الثبوت أو السلب في جميع أوقات اتصاف الذات بالوصف العنواني والنسبة بين المعنيين عموم وخصوص من وجه؛ لتصادقهما في مادة الضرورة ،الذاتية إذا كان العنوان نفس الذات أو وصفا لازما لها، كقولنا كل إنسان أو كل ناطق حيوان بالضرورة، وصدق الأولى بدون الثانية في مادة يكون المحمول ضروريا للذات بشرط وصف مفارق، كقولنا : كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة، فإن تحرك الأصابع ضروري لذات الكاتب بشرط اتصافه بالكتابة لا في جميع أوقات الكتابة وصدقِ الثانية بدون الأولى في مادة الضرورة الذاتية، إذا كان العنوان وصفا مفارقا كقولنا كل كاتب حيوان بالضرورة.
والمشروطة العامة بالمعنى الأول أعم من الضرورية والدائمة من وجه؛ لتصادقهما في مثل قولنا : كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة أو دائما ما دام إنسانا وصدقهما دونها في مثل قولنا : كل كاتب حيوان بالضرورة أو دائما، وصدقها دونهما في المثال المذكور في المتن.
وأما بالمعنى الثاني فهي أعم من الضرورية مطلقا ؛ لأنه كلما ثبت الضرورة في جميع أوقات الذات ثبت في جميع أوقات الوصف من غير عكس، ومن الدائمـة مـن وجـه؛ لتصادقهما في مادة الضرورة المطلقة وصدق الدائمة بدونها في مادة الدائمة المطلقة الخالية عن الضرورة، وصدقها بدون الدائمة حيث تكون الضرورة في جميع أوقات الوصف ولا يكون الدوام في جميع أوقات الذات.
(۱) + خ: (وصدقهما دونها، أي: صدق الضرورية والدائمة دونها أي: دون المشروطة العامة).
And the general conditional (al-mashruta al-ʿāmma) is used for the proposition in which it is judged that the predicate is necessarily affirmed of the subject or its negation from it as long as the description of the subject exists, i.e., it is judged with necessary affirmation or negation in all times of the subject's being qualified by the titular description. The relationship between the two meanings is general and specific from an aspect because they coincide in the matter of essential necessity if the title is the essence itself or an inseparable attribute, as in our saying: 'Every human' or 'Every rational animal' necessarily, and the first is true without the second in a matter where the predicate is necessary for the essence under the condition of a separable description, as in our saying: 'Every writer is a finger-mover necessarily', for the movement of fingers is necessary for the essence of a writer under the condition of his being described as writing, not in all times of writing. And the second is true without the first in the matter of essential necessity if the title is a separable description, as in our saying: 'Every writer is an animal necessarily.' And the general conditional in the first sense is more general than the necessary and the perpetual from an aspect because they coincide in such as our saying: 'Every human is an animal necessarily' or 'perpetually as long as he is human', and the two (necessary and perpetual) are true without it (the general conditional) in such as our saying: 'Every writer is an animal necessarily' or 'perpetually', and it is true without them in the example mentioned in the text. As for the second sense, it is absolutely more general than the necessary because whenever necessity is established in all times of the essence, it is established in all times of the description, but not conversely. And [it is] more general than the perpetual from an aspect because they coincide in the matter of absolute necessity, and the perpetual is true without it in the matter of absolute perpetual devoid of necessity, and it is true without the perpetual when there is necessity in all times of the description but perpetuity is not in all times of the essence. (1) Variant: 'and the two are true without it, i.e., the necessary and perpetual are true without it, i.e., without the general conditional.)
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage defines the general conditional proposition (al-mashruta al-ʿāmma) and clarifies its two meanings. The author first presents the first meaning: a judgment of necessary affirmation or negation of a predicate for a subject as long as the subject is described by a certain title. Then he explains that the two meanings (the first meaning and the essential necessity) are related as general and specific from an aspect because they coincide in examples where the title is the essence itself (e.g., 'Every human is an animal necessarily') but differ when the title is a separable description. The author then compares the first meaning with necessary and perpetual propositions, showing that the general conditional is more general in some cases, while in others it is less general. Finally, he discusses the second meaning of the general conditional, which is more general than the necessary absolutely and more general than the perpetual from an aspect, and provides examples. The footnote adds a clarification that the necessary and perpetual propositions are true without the general conditional in certain examples.

Key Takeaway
The general conditional proposition (al-mashruta al-ʿāmma) has two meanings: necessity conditioned on the description, and necessity in all times of the description; understanding their relationship with essential necessity and perpetual propositions is key to analyzing modal claims.

The General Customary Proposition: Definition and Comparison
Defines the general customary proposition and compares it with other conditional types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the general customary proposition and distinguish it from the general conditional and the two perpetual propositions.

Core Idea
The general customary judges perpetuity under description, not necessity like the conditional.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified by their temporal conditions. The general customary proposition (al-ʿurfiyya al-ʿāmma) is a type of conditional proposition that focuses on perpetual affirmation or negation as long as the subject's description holds. This lesson unpacks its definition, its naming, and how it relates to other proposition types.

Guided Path
1
Understand the naming rationale
The proposition is called 'customary' because common custom (al-ʿurf al-ʿāmm) interprets negative statements like 'Nothing of the sleeper is awake' as meaning that the predicate is negated as long as the subject has the description (sleeping). It is 'general' because it covers more cases than the specific customary.
Focus on why custom is invoked: it reflects everyday understanding of natural language conditionals.
2
Extract the technical definition
The definition states: a judgment of perpetual affirmation or negation of the predicate for the subject under the condition of the subject's titular description. This means the predicate holds (or is negated) at all times the subject possesses that description.
The key term is 'perpetuity' (al-dawām) not 'necessity' (al-ḍarūra).
3
Relate to the general conditional
The author says the general customary is 'absolutely more general' than the general conditional. Because whenever necessity (as in the conditional) holds under the description, perpetuity also holds, but not vice versa. Example: 'Every human is rational' is necessary (as long as human), but 'Every sleeper is asleep' is perpetual (as long as sleeping) but not necessary (he might wake up).
Necessity implies perpetuity, but perpetuity does not imply necessity.
4
Relate to the two perpetuals
The author compares with the two perpetual propositions (likely the absolute perpetual and the perpetual conditional?). He states that whenever necessity or perpetuity holds in all times of the essence (i.e., of the subject's identity), then perpetuity holds in all times of the description, but not vice versa. This shows that the general customary is broader in scope.
This step requires careful attention to the difference between essence times and description times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وهي) أي: العرفية العامة (التي يحكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو) بدوام (سلبه) أي: سلب المحمول (عنه) أي: عن الموضوع (بشرط وصفه) أي: الوصف العنواني للموضوع، أي: بشرط اتصاف ذات الموضوع بالوصف العنواني، (وقد مر مثالها إيجابا وسلبا في المشروطة العامة، فلا حاجة إلى الإعادة.

This passage defines the general customary proposition (al-ʿurfiyya al-ʿāmma). It is 'general' because it applies to a broader range than the specific customary. Then he gives its technical definition: a judgment of perpetual affirmation or negation of the predicate for the subject as long as the subject is described by the titular description. The author notes that examples have already been given in the section on the general conditional, so he does not repeat them. Finally, he compares the general customary with the general conditional and the two perpetuals (absolute perpetual and perpetual conditional?), showing that the general customary is absolutely more general than both in certain ways.

The General Absolute Proposition
Defines the general absolute proposition and explains its terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general absolute proposition and explain why it is called 'absolute' despite being restricted to actuality.

Core Idea
The general absolute proposition judges actuality; its name is a metaphorical extension.

In modal logic, propositions are classified by their modality: necessity, perpetuity, possibility, etc. But one important type is the general absolute proposition, which simply states what is the case right now. This lesson unpacks its definition and the surprising reason it is called 'absolute', even though it is restricted to actuality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Read the definition: 'The general absolute proposition is the proposition which is judged with affirmation of the predicate for the subject or its negation in actuality.' Focus on the phrase 'in actuality' (bil-fiʿl).
Actuality means the state of affairs at the time of speaking.
2
Examine the examples
The text gives two examples: 'Every human is breathing' and 'Nothing of the human is breathing.' Note that these are about the present fact, not about necessity or permanence. A human might not always be breathing, but at the moment of utterance, it is true.
These are standard examples from the text.
3
Understand the terminological puzzle
The author points out that 'absolute' originally meant a proposition without any modal qualification. However, due to common usage, it has come to mean a proposition restricted to actuality. This is a metaphorical shift, like calling a negative proposition a 'conditional'.
The key phrase: 'تسمية المقيد باسم المطلق عند غلبة الاستعمال' – naming the restricted by the name of the unrestricted due to prevalent usage.
4
Connect to modal classification
Although actuality is not a genuine modality (it is the same as the judgment itself), the general absolute is counted among modalized propositions (al-muwajjahāt) metaphorically. This helps in the systematic classification of propositions.
This explains the placement of al-muṭlaqa in the chapter on modal propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة: (المطلقة العامة، وهي القضية التي يحكـم فيهـا بثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه) أي: سلب المحمول (عنه) أي عن الموضوع (بالفعل، كقولنا: بالإطلاق العام كل إنسان متنفس (و) كقولنا : (به) أي بالإطلاق العام (لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس).
وإنما وقع الاصطلاح على تسمية هذه القضية بالمطلقة مع أنها في الأصل عبارة عن القضية التي يتعرض فيها للحكم بالإيجاب والسلب فقط من غير التقييد بالفعل والجهة باعتبار غلبة الاستعمال وتسارع الفهم إلى النسبة الفعلية عند الإطلاق لغة وعرفا، ولا امتناع في تسمية المقيد باسم المطلق عند غلبة الاستعمال، وإنما عُدّ المطلقة في الموجهات مجازا كما عُدَّ السالبة من الحمليات والشرطيات - ؛ لأن الفعل ليس كيفية للنسبة؛ لانتفاء التغاير بينه وبين الحكم.
The fifth: (The general absolute [proposition], and it is the proposition which is judged with affirmation of the predicate for the subject or its negation) i.e., negation of the predicate (from it) i.e., from the subject (in actuality, as in our saying: by the general absolute, 'Every human is breathing', and as in our saying: by it, i.e., by the general absolute, 'Nothing of the human is breathing'.) And the convention came to name this proposition 'absolute' despite it originally being the expression for the proposition that deals with judgment of affirmation and negation only, without restriction to actuality and modality, due to the prevalence of usage and the quickness of understanding to the actual relation when 'absolute' is used in language and custom. And there is no prohibition in naming the restricted by the name of the unrestricted when usage is prevalent. Rather, the absolute is counted among the modalized propositions metaphorically, just as the negative is counted among the categorical and conditional [propositions] — because actuality is not a modality of the relation, due to the lack of distinction between it and the judgment.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines the general absolute proposition (al-muṭlaqa al-ʿāmma). The author states that it is a proposition that judges the affirmation or negation of the predicate for the subject in actuality (bil-fiʿl). He gives examples: 'Every human is breathing' and 'Nothing of the human is breathing'. Then he explains the terminological rationale: strictly speaking, 'absolute' means a proposition without any modal qualification (neither necessity nor perpetuity nor actuality). However, due to common usage, the term 'absolute' has been applied to the proposition that is restricted to actuality. This is considered a metaphorical usage, similar to how negative propositions are classified among categorical and conditional ones even though negation is a quality, not a quantity. The author clarifies that actuality is not a genuine modality (kayfiyya) of the relation between subject and predicate because it is identical to the judgment itself.

Key Terms
المطلقة العامة
بالفعل
الموجهات

Key Takeaway
The general absolute proposition judges actuality; its name is a metaphorical extension from the unqualified absolute due to prevalent usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a real-world Islamic statement (e.g., 'Zayd is praying') and determine whether it is a general absolute proposition. Explain why.
?
Take the proposition 'Every believer is righteous' and rewrite it as a general absolute proposition by adding the phrase 'in actuality'. Then check whether the meaning changes.

The General Customary Proposition: Definition and Scope
Definition and comparison of the general customary proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general customary proposition and distinguish it from the general conditioned and permanent propositions by analyzing its definition and examples.

Core Idea
The general customary proposition ties a judgment to a temporary description of the subject.

Modal propositions in logic are classified by the condition and time of judgment. The general customary proposition ('urfiyya 'amma) is a key type that expresses a judgment that holds as long as the subject possesses a certain description. This lesson unpacks its definition and shows why it is more general than other modal types.

Guided Path
1
Understand why it is called 'urfiyya
Consider the statement: 'No one who is in a state of ritual impurity may pray.' Common custom understands that the prohibition lasts only as long as the person is in that state. That is the core intuition behind the term 'customary'.
2
Extract the formal definition
The text defines it as a proposition that judges the permanence of the predicate's affirmation or negation to the subject, conditioned on the subject being described by its attributive description. Identify the three key components: the judgment, the condition (description), and the time (permanence while the condition holds).
3
Compare with the general conditioned proposition
The general conditioned proposition (musharrata 'amma) requires necessity with respect to the description, while the 'urfiyya 'amma requires only permanence. The text states that if necessity holds, then permanence holds (but not vice versa). Thus 'urfiyya 'amma is more general.
4
Compare with the two permanent propositions
The permanent propositions (da'imtan) require necessity or permanence in all times of the essence. The 'urfiyya 'amma requires it only during the time of the description. Again, the text's reasoning shows that if the judgment holds for all times of the essence, it certainly holds for all times of the description, so 'urfiyya 'amma is more general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وهي) أي: العرفية العامة (التي يحكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو) بدوام (سلبه) أي: سلب المحمول (عنه) أي: عن الموضوع (بشرط وصفه) أي: الوصف العنواني للموضوع، أي: بشرط اتصاف ذات الموضوع بالوصف العنواني، (وقد مر مثالها إيجابا وسلبا في المشروطة العامة، فلا حاجة إلى الإعادة.
أعم مطلقا وهي
من المشروطة العامة؛ لأنه متى ثبتت الضرورة بحسب الوصف ثبت الدوام بحسبه من غير عكس، ومن الدائمتين؛ لأنه متى تثبت الضرورة أو الدوام في جميع أوقات الذات ثبت الدوام في جميع أوقات الوصف من غير عكس.
The fourth: (The general customary proposition). It was called 'customary' because common custom understands this meaning from the negative proposition, such as our saying: 'Nothing that is sleeping is awake,' for custom understands from it that wakefulness is negated of the sleeper as long as he is sleeping. And it was called 'general' because it is more general than the particular customary proposition. (And it is) i.e. the general customary proposition (that in which judgment is made of the permanence of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject or) of the permanence of (its negation) i.e. the negation of the predicate (from it) i.e. from the subject (on condition of its description) i.e. the attributive description of the subject, i.e. on condition that the essence of the subject is qualified by the attributive description. (And its example in affirmation and negation has already passed in the general conditioned proposition, so there is no need to repeat.) It is absolutely more general than the general conditioned proposition, because whenever necessity is established with respect to the description, permanence is established with respect to it without the converse, and than the two permanent propositions, because whenever necessity or permanence is established in all times of the essence, permanence is established in all times of the description without the converse.

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of proposition: the general customary proposition ('urfiyya 'amma). The author explains why it is called 'urfiyya: because common custom (al-'urf al-'amm) understands the meaning of a negative proposition like 'No sleeper is awake' as meaning that wakefulness is negated only as long as the subject is sleeping. It is called 'amma (general) because it is more general than the particular customary proposition. The definition is that it is a proposition that judges the permanence (dawam) of the predicate's affirmation or negation to the subject, conditioned on the subject being described by its attributive description. The example for both affirmation and negation was already given in the general conditioned proposition (musharrata 'amma), so it is not repeated. Then the author compares this proposition's level of generality: the 'urfiyya 'amma is absolutely more general than the musharrata 'amma because if necessity is proven with respect to the description, then permanence (dawam) is proven with respect to that description, but not vice versa. Likewise, it is more general than the two permanent propositions (al-da'imtan) because if necessity or permanence is proven in all times of the essence, then permanence is proven in all times of the description, but not conversely.

Definition of the General Absolute Proposition
Author defines the general absolute proposition and illustrates it with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general absolute proposition and distinguish it from other modal propositions based on its definition.

Core Idea
The general absolute proposition judges actuality, not possibility or necessity.

In this lesson, we examine the fifth type of proposition: the general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-'amma). The author defines it clearly and provides examples. You will learn its defining characteristic—judging by actuality—and see how it fits within the classification of propositions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition in the Arabic text
Find the phrase 'المطلقة العامة، وهي القضية التي يحكم فيها بثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه بالفعل'. Note that it is a nominal sentence specifying the definiendum and definiens.
Identify the term being defined: al-mutlaqa al-'amma.
2
Understand the key qualifier 'بالفعل' (bi'l-fi'l)
This qualifier restricts the judgment to actuality. Compare with other modalities: necessary (daruri), permanent (da'im), etc.
Actuality means the predicate holds for the subject at the present moment.
3
Analyze the examples provided
The author gives a positive example: 'كل إنسان متنفس' and a negative example: 'لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس'. Both are general absolute propositions because they assert the actuality of breathing (or its negation) for every human.
These examples show the two forms: universal affirmative and universal negative, but with actuality.
4
Test the definition by constructing your own example
Try to form a general absolute proposition on an Islamic topic. For instance, 'Every believer prays' is a general absolute if taken as actual. But it is not necessary (some believers might not pray sometimes).
Be careful: if a proposition is necessary, it also entails actuality, but the converse is not true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة: (المطلقة العامة، وهي القضية التي يحكـم فيهـا بثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه) أي: سلب المحمول (عنه) أي عن الموضوع (بالفعل، كقولنا: بالإطلاق العام كل إنسان متنفس (و) كقولنا : (به) أي بالإطلاق العام (لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس).
وإنما وقع الاصطلاح على تسمية هذه القضية بالمطلقة مع أنها في الأصل عبارة عن القضية التي يتعرض فيها للحكم بالإيجاب والسلب فقط(۱) من غير التقييد بالفعل والجهة باعتبار غلبة الاستعمال وتسارع الفهم إلى النسبة الفعلية عند الإطلاق لغة وعرفا، ولا امتناع في تسمية المقيد باسم المطلق عند غلبة الاستعمال، وإنما عُدّ المطلقة في الموجهات مجازا كما عُدَّ السالبة من الحمليات والشرطيات - ؛ لأن الفعل ليس كيفية للنسبة؛ لانتفاء التغاير بينه وبين الحكم.
(۱) ط: (بحكم الإيجاب والسلب فقط).
وإنما سميت عامة؛ لكونها أعم من الوجودية اللاضرورية واللادائمة، وهي أعم مطلقا
من الدائمتين والعامتين؛ لأنه متى تحقق دوام النسبة بحسب الذات أو الوصف تحقق فعليتها من غير عكس.
The fifth: (The general absolute proposition, which is the proposition in which judgment is made of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject or its negation) i.e. the negation of the predicate (from it) i.e. from the subject (in actuality, like our saying with general absoluteness: 'Every human is breathing' and) like our saying (with it) i.e. with general absoluteness ('Nothing that is human is breathing'). And the convention has come to name this proposition as the absolute proposition despite the fact that originally it is an expression for the proposition that concerns itself with judgment of affirmation and negation only (1) without restriction to actuality and modality, due to predominance of usage and the quickness of the understanding to the actual relation upon absolute expression linguistically and customarily. There is no impossibility in naming the restricted with the name of the absolute when usage predominates. And it is counted among the modal propositions metaphorically, just as the negative is counted among categorical and conditional propositions—because actuality is not a modality of the relation, due to the lack of distinction between it and the judgment. (1) From the copy: (with judgment of affirmation and negation only). And it was called general because it is more general than the existential non-necessary and non-permanent propositions, and it is absolutely more general than the two permanent and the two general propositions; because whenever the permanence of the relation with respect to the essence or the description is realized, its actuality is realized without the converse.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth type: the general absolute proposition (al-mutlaqa al-'amma). It is a proposition that judges the actual (bi'l-fi'l) affirmation or negation of the predicate to the subject. Examples: 'Every human is breathing' and 'No human is breathing' when stated with general absoluteness (bi'l-itlaq al-'amm). The author then discusses why this proposition is called mutlaqa despite its restriction to actuality. Originally, the term mutlaqa refers to a proposition without any modality or time restriction. But due to common usage, when a proposition is uttered without qualification, the mind immediately understands it as actual. Hence, by convention, this restricted sense is named mutlaqa figuratively (majazan). This is similar to how the term 'negative' (saliba) is applied in categorical and conditional propositions. The author clarifies that actuality is not a true modality because it does not add a distinct quality to the relation; it is merely the judgment itself. Lastly, he explains why it is called 'amma (general): because it is more general than existential non-necessary and non-permanent propositions, and also more general than the two permanent propositions (al-da'imtan) and the two general propositions (i.e., the 'urfiyya 'amma and musharrata 'amma). This is because if permanence is realized in any time (by essence or by description), then actuality is necessarily realized, but not vice versa.

Key Terms
مطلقة عامة
بالفعل

A) It judges the predicate as necessary.
B) It judges the predicate as actual.
C) It judges the predicate as possible.
D) It judges the predicate as eternal.

Key Takeaway
The general absolute proposition (mutlaqa 'amma) is defined by its judgment of actuality, not necessity or possibility.

Terminological Nuances of the General Absolute
Author justifies the naming of the general absolute proposition despite its restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reasons behind naming the general absolute proposition as mutlaqa and as 'amma despite its apparent restriction.

Core Idea
The name 'mutlaqa' is conventional due to predominant usage; 'amma' indicates its wide applicability.

You might wonder: if the general absolute is restricted to actuality, why is it called 'absolute'? And why 'general'? The author addresses these questions, revealing the conventional nature of logical terminology. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the puzzle about the name 'mutlaqa'
Why call it absolute when it is restricted? The term 'mutlaqa' originally means unrestricted, but here it includes a restriction to actuality. The author acknowledges this.
2
Find the author's justification for the name
Look for the phrase starting with 'وإنما وقع الاصطلاح على تسمية هذه القضية بالمطلقة...' He argues that due to predominant usage (ghalabat al-isti'mal), when a proposition is uttered without qualification, the mind immediately understands it as actual. So by convention, this restricted sense is called mutlaqa.
3
Understand the analogy calling this 'metaphorical' (majazan)
The author says it is counted among modal propositions metaphorically. Actuality is not a true modality because it is indistinguishable from the judgment itself. So the name mutlaqa here is a metaphor. He compares it to calling the negative a type of categorical or conditional.
4
Analyze why it is called 'amma (general)'
Find the explanation: 'وإنما سميت عامة؛ لكونها أعم من الوجودية اللاضرورية واللادائمة...' The general absolute is more general than existential non-necessary and non-permanent propositions because if permanence holds, actuality holds, but not vice versa.
5
Apply the reasoning to an example
For instance, the proposition 'Zaid is standing' (if actual) is more general than 'Zaid is standing eternally'. The general absolute includes cases that are actual but not permanent.

The General Possible Proposition: Negating Absolute Necessity
Definition of the general possible proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and interpret the general possible proposition in modal logic, distinguishing it from other modal propositions using its definition of negating absolute necessity from the contradictory side.

Core Idea
The general possible only denies necessity of the opposite, not actuality.

In this lesson, we examine the sixth type of modal proposition: the general possible (al-mumkina al-'amma). Unlike the particular possible, which asserts contingency, the general possible merely negates absolute necessity from the contradictory. Understanding this proposition is essential for correctly analyzing modal statements in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where one often needs to distinguish what is necessary from what is merely not impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term being defined: 'الممكنة العامة' (the general possible). It is introduced as the sixth type of modal proposition. Note that it is a type of proposition (qadiyya).
2
Extract the definiens
The definition states: 'a proposition that judges the raising of absolute necessity from the side opposite to the judgment.' Break it down: absolute necessity (al-darura al-mutlaqa) is essential necessity, and the side opposite (al-janib al-mukhalif) is the contradictory of the actual judgment. So it means the contradictory is not necessary.
The definiens has two components: what is negated (absolute necessity) and from where (the opposite side).
3
Distinguish the two cases
The author explains that if the judgment is affirmative, the meaning is negation of the necessity of the negative; if negative, negation of the necessity of the affirmative. Work through each: for 'Every fire is hot' (affirmative), it means it is not necessary that fire is not hot. For 'Nothing hot is cold' (negative), it means it is not necessary that hot is cold.
This shows the definition applies uniformly to both quality forms.
4
Apply the examples
Take the affirmative example: 'Every fire is hot' is not a claim that fire is necessarily hot, but that the opposite (fire is not hot) is not necessary. Similarly, the negative example: 'Nothing hot is cold' means the opposite (hot is cold) is not necessary. Confirm that the defined meaning matches these examples.
5
Recognize the generality
The author asserts this proposition is more general than all others. Why? Because it only denies necessity, not actuality or possibility. Any proposition that is actually true or necessary also satisfies the condition that its opposite is not necessary. This makes it the widest in scope.
Think: if a proposition is true by absolute necessity, its opposite is impossible, so the general possible would be false. But if it is just true without necessity, the general possible holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة الممكنة العامة وهى القضية التي يحكم فيها) أي: في تلك القضية بارتفاع) أي: بسلب (الضرورة المطلقة :أي: الذاتية (عن الجانب) أي: الطرف (المخالف للحكم يعني إن كان الحكم بالإيجاب كان معناه سلب ضرورة السلب، وإن كان الحكم بالسلب كان معناه سلب ضرورة الإيجاب، مثال الموجبة (كقولنا: بالإمكان العام كل نار حارة فإن معناه أن سلب الحرارة عن النار ليس بضروري، ومثال السالبة كقولنا: (به) أي: بالإمكان العام (لا شيء من الحار ببارد فإن معناه أن إيجاب البرودة للحار ليس بضروري وهي أعم من جميع القضايا، وذلك ظاهر لا سترة له.
The sixth: The general possible proposition, which is the proposition that judges (i.e. in that proposition) the removal (i.e. the negation) of absolute necessity (i.e. essential necessity) from the side (i.e. the side) opposite to the judgment. That is, if the judgment is affirmative, its meaning is the negation of the necessity of the negative; if the judgment is negative, its meaning is the negation of the necessity of the affirmative. An example of the affirmative: our saying with general possibility: 'Every fire is hot' means that the negation of heat from fire is not necessary. An example of the negative: our saying with general possibility: 'Nothing hot is cold' means that the affirmation of coldness for hot is not necessary. And it is more general than all propositions, and that is apparent with no concealment.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth type: the general possible proposition (al-mumkina al-'amma). It is a proposition that judges the negation of absolute necessity from the side opposite to the actual judgment. In other words, it asserts that the contradictory of the judgment is not necessary. For an affirmative judgment, it means that the negative of the predicate is not essential; for a negative judgment, it means that the affirmative of the predicate is not essential. Examples: 'Every fire is hot' (affirmative) means that it is not necessary for fire to lack heat; 'Nothing hot is cold' (negative) means that it is not necessary for hot to be cold. The author notes that this proposition is more general than all other propositions, because it only denies necessity, making it the widest in scope.

Key Terms
ممكنة عامة
ضرورة مطلقة
جانب مخالف للحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fire is necessarily hot.
B) Fire is actually hot.
C) Fire is not necessarily not hot.
D) Fire is permanently hot.

Key Takeaway
The general possible proposition negates absolute necessity from the contradictory of the judgment, making it the most inclusive modal proposition.

Definition of Compound Proposition and Its First Subtype
Author defines compound proposition and begins listing its seven subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a compound proposition and distinguish its first subtype, the particular conditioned, from other compound propositions.

Core Idea
A compound proposition has two parts: one explicit, one implicit, differing in quality but agreeing in quantity.

In this section, the author defines the compound proposition, clarifying its structure and defending it against a misconception. He then begins to enumerate the seven subtypes, starting with the particular conditioned proposition. Understanding this classification is essential for analyzing complex logical statements in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Find the sentence that defines a compound proposition. What are its two components? How are they related?
The definition uses 'reality' meaning its logical meaning is composed of two propositions.
2
Understand the condition for composition
The author addresses an objection: not every combination of two propositions is compound. What additional condition is required?
The propositions must share the same subject (موضوع) and differ in quality (affirmation/negation).
3
Determine quality and quantity of the compound
How does the compound proposition take its quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular)? Which proposition determines them?
Quality follows the first explicit proposition; quantity must agree (both universal or both particular).
4
Examine the first subtype: particular conditioned
What is the particular conditioned proposition? How does it differ from the general conditioned? What additional condition does it have?
It adds non-permanence with respect to the essence, not the description.

(As for) the compound proposition, which is the proposition (the reality of which i.e. its meaning) is composed of two propositions, one mentioned explicitly, and the other not explicit, either by another word that by convention indicates it, like 'non-permanent' and 'non-necessary', or merely by consideration as in the particular possible proposition. So that the objection brought against the author that it follows from that that a compound of two propositions is itself a compound proposition does not hold. Rather, if we collect negatives and affirmatives with the same subject, that would be a compound proposition, but it is not so. (It is) contrary in quality (i.e. affirmation and negation) and agreeing in quantity (i.e. universality and particularity), considering its affirmation i.e. the compound proposition's affirmation and negation by the first proposition mentioned explicitly, not the second mentioned implicitly.
