Commentary
This passage introduces the compound proposition (al-qadiyya al-murakkaba). It is defined as a proposition whose meaning is composed of two propositions: one is stated explicitly, and the other is implied either by a conventional term like 'non-permanent' or 'non-necessary' or by mere consideration, as in the particular possible proposition. The author defends the definition against an objection that if it is composed of two propositions, then it would itself be a compound. He argues that only when we combine affirmatives and negatives with the same subject does it become compound, but that is not the case here. The compound proposition has the property of differing in quality (i.e., one part is affirmative, the other negative) while agreeing in quantity (both universal or both particular). Its overall quality is determined by the first explicit proposition. Then he begins to list the seven subtypes, starting with the particular conditioned proposition (al-musharrata al-khassa), which is the general conditioned proposition with the added condition of non-permanence with respect to the essence, not the description. The text cuts off here.

Compound Propositions: Definition and the First Two Types
Defines compound propositions and details the first two types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of compound propositions and distinguish between the special conditional and special customary modal propositions.

Core Idea
Compound propositions combine an explicit and an implicit proposition; their quality follows the explicit part.

In this passage, the author defines a compound proposition, explains how its quality is determined, and details the first two of seven types: the special conditional and the special customary. These types add the condition of non-permanence to the general conditional and general customary respectively. Understanding their composition is essential for mastering modal logic in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Define compound proposition
Identify that a compound proposition's meaning is composed of two propositions: one explicit, the other implicit. The implicit part is indicated by a conventional term like 'non-permanent' (al-ladawam) or by mere consideration, as in the special possible.
2
Understand quality determination
Realize that the compound proposition takes its quality (affirmation/negation) from the explicit proposition, not the implicit one. So if the explicit part is affirmative, the compound is affirmative; if negative, the compound is negative.
3
Analyze special conditional example
For the affirmative case 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always', break it into an affirmative general conditional and a negative general absolute. For the negative case, break into a negative general conditional and an affirmative general absolute.
4
Analyze special customary example
For the affirmative case 'Always, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always', break it into an affirmative general customary and a negative general absolute. For the negative case, break into a negative general customary and an affirmative general absolute.
5
Compare the two types
Notice that the special conditional and special customary are structurally identical, except the former uses necessity (bi al-darura) and the latter uses always (da'iman). Both are more specific than the general unconditional and general customary and are distinct from the permanent types.

As for the compound proposition, it is the proposition whose reality (its meaning) is composed of two propositions: one mentioned explicitly, and the other not explicit, either by another term conventionally indicating it like 'non-permanent' and 'non-necessary', or by mere consideration as in the special possible. So the objection raised against the author that from this it would follow that a compound of two propositions is a compound proposition does not apply. Rather, if we collect negations and affirmations with a unified subject, that would be a compound proposition, and it is not like that. It opposes with respect to quality (affirmation and negation) and agrees with respect to quantity (universality and particularity), considering its affirmation and negation (i.e., of the compound proposition) according to the first proposition mentioned explicitly, not the second mentioned summarily. So if the first proposition is affirmative, the compound proposition is affirmative; if negative, then negative. Thus there are seven: The first is the special conditional, which is the general conditional with the addition of the condition of non-permanence with respect to essence, not description, otherwise a contradiction would follow. And it was not restricted to the condition of eternal non-permanence and eternal non-necessity because the discussion is on well-known, frequently used propositions, and propositions restricted to those two conditions are not among them. Non-permanence, in their usage, is an expression for a general absolute proposition agreeing with the first in subject, predicate, and quantity, and differing in quality. So the special conditional, if it is affirmative like our saying: 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always' i.e., 'No writer moves his fingers with absolute generality', then its composition (the special conditional) is from an affirmative general conditional (the first part) and a negative general absolute (the meaning of non-permanence with respect to essence). And if it is negative like our saying: 'Necessarily, no writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer, not always' i.e., 'Every writer is still-fingered with absolute generality', then it is from a negative general conditional (the first part) and an affirmative general absolute (the meaning of non-permanence). And it is distinct from the two permanent types and more specific than the general conditional and the remaining types. The second: the special customary, which is the general customary with the condition of non-permanence with respect to essence. It, if affirmative like our saying: 'Always, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always', then its composition (the special customary) is from an affirmative general customary (the first part) and a negative general absolute (the meaning of non-permanence). And if it is negative like our saying: 'No writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer, not always', then its composition is from a negative general customary (the first part) and an affirmative general absolute (the meaning of non-permanence). And its example (for the special customary) in affirmation and negation has already passed in the special conditional, except that 'necessity' is replaced by our saying 'always'.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage defines compound propositions and details the first two of seven types. The author begins by explaining that a compound proposition's meaning is composed of two propositions: one explicit and one implicit. The implicit one is indicated either by a conventional term like 'non-permanent' (al-ladawam) or 'non-necessary' (al-ladarura), or by mere consideration as in the special possible. He then dismisses an objection that a compound of two propositions would itself be a compound proposition, clarifying that this is not the case. The compound proposition is judged for its quality (affirmation/negation) based on the explicit proposition, not the implicit one. Thus, if the explicit proposition is affirmative, the compound is affirmative; if negative, the compound is negative. He then lists seven types, but only discusses the first two in this passage. The first is the special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa), which is the general conditional with the additional condition of non-permanence with respect to essence (bi hasab al-dhat). The author explains that the term 'non-permanence' means a general absolute proposition that agrees with the first proposition in subject, predicate, and quantity, but opposes it in quality. For example, the affirmative special conditional 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always' is composed of an affirmative general conditional ('Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer') and a negative general absolute ('No writer moves his fingers with absolute generality'). Similarly, the negative version is composed accordingly. The special conditional is distinct from the two permanent types (al-da'imatan) and is more specific than the general conditional. The second type is the special customary (al-'urfiyya al-khassa), which is the general customary with the condition of non-permanence with respect to essence. Its composition follows the same pattern, with 'always' replacing 'necessity' in the first part. The examples are analogous to those of the special conditional, but with 'daima' instead of 'darura'. This careful breakdown clarifies how modal propositions can be formed from simpler components.

The Specific Conditional: Composition and Definition
Definition and composition of the specific conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two component propositions of any specific conditional and determine its quality composition.

Core Idea
The specific conditional is a compound of a general conditional and an opposite absolute universal.

In Islamic logic, modal propositions are not always simple. The specific conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa) is a key compound proposition, built from a general conditional and an absolute universal linked by the concept of 'non-permanence' (al-ladawam). This lesson unpacks its definition and structure, showing how it differs from simpler propositions. You will learn to recognise its components in both affirmative and negative forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components
Every specific conditional consists of a general conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma) and an absolute universal (al-mutlaqa al-'amma). These two differ in quality (affirmative/negative) but share subject, predicate, and quantity.
2
Understand 'non-permanence' (al-ladawam)
The absolute universal that is paired with the general conditional expresses the 'non-permanence' of the predicate with respect to the subject. It is always of the opposite quality to the general conditional.
3
Composition of an affirmative specific conditional
If the specific conditional is affirmative (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always'), it is composed of an affirmative general conditional and a negative absolute universal.
4
Composition of a negative specific conditional
If the specific conditional is negative (e.g., 'No writer is still in fingers as long as he is a writer, not always'), it is composed of a negative general conditional and an affirmative absolute universal.
5
Distinguish from other propositions
The specific conditional is more specific than the general conditional and distinct from the two 'permanent' propositions (al-dā'imatān). It always involves a restriction (condition) while also stating a non-permanence beyond that condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللادوام الأزلي واللاضرورة الأزلية؛ لأن البحث في القضايا المشهورة الكثيرة الاستعمال، والقضايا المقيدة بالقيدين المذكورين ليست منها.
(واللادوام) عندهم (عبارة) أي: معبرة عن مطلقة عامة موافقة للأولى في الموضوع والمحمول وفي الكم، ومخالفة لها في الكيف، (فـ) القضية المشروطة الخاصة إن كانت موجبة كقولنا: بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما) أي: لا شيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالإطلاق العام (فتركيبها) أي: المشروطة الخاصة (من موجبة مشروطة عامة وهي الجزء الأول من القضية المركبة، ومن سالبة مطلقة عامة وهي: مفهوم اللادوام بحسب الذات (وإن كانت أي المشروطة الخاصة (سالبة كقولنا : بالضرورة لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما أي كل كاتب ساكن الأصابع بالإطلاق العام (فمن سالبة :أي: فهي مركبة من سالبة (مشروطة عامة) وهي الجزء الأول من القضية المركبة، وموجبة مطلقة عامة) وهي مفهوم اللادوام بحسب الذات، وهي مباينة للدائمتين، وأخص من المشروطة العامة ومن البواقي.
The eternal non-permanence and the eternal non-necessity; because the investigation is in the well-known frequently used propositions, and propositions restricted by the two mentioned restrictions are not among them. (And non-permanence) according to them is an expression meaning an absolute universal agreeing with the first in subject and predicate and in quantity, and differing from it in quality. (So) the specific conditional proposition, if it is affirmative such as our saying: 'By necessity, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always,' i.e., [by] the absolute universal nothing of the writer is moving his fingers. So its composition (i.e., the specific conditional) is from an affirmative general conditional which is the first part of the compound proposition, and from a negative absolute universal which is the concept of non-permanence with respect to essence. And if the specific conditional is negative, such as our saying: 'By necessity, nothing of the writer is still in fingers as long as he is a writer, not always,' i.e., every writer is still in fingers by the absolute universal. Then it is from a negative (i.e., it is composed of a negative general conditional which is the first part of the compound proposition, and an affirmative absolute universal which is the concept of non-permanence with respect to essence. And it is distinct from the two permanent ones, and more specific than the general conditional and the remaining ones.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage defines the 'specific conditional' proposition (al-mashruta al-khassa) and explains its logical composition. The author first introduces the concepts of 'non-permanence' (al-ladawam) and 'non-necessity' (al-ladurura) as eternal (azali) concepts, noting that the investigation focuses on commonly used propositions; propositions restricted by these two terms (i.e., with non-permanence and non-necessity) are not among the well-known ones. Then he defines 'non-permanence' as an expression for an absolute universal that agrees with the first proposition in subject, predicate, and quantity, but differs in quality. Using this, he builds the specific conditional: if affirmative, it is composed of an affirmative general conditional and a negative absolute universal (the concept of non-permanence). If negative, it is composed of a negative general conditional and an affirmative absolute universal. The example given is about a writer moving fingers as long as he is a writer, not always. The specific conditional is distinct from the two permanent propositions and is more specific than the general conditional and the remaining ones.

Definition and Composition of the Specific Customary Proposition
Definition and composition of the specific customary proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the specific customary proposition, identify its composition (affirmative and negative forms), and distinguish it from the specific conditional proposition.

Core Idea
The specific customary is the general customary restricted by non-permanence, formed by combining a general customary and an absolute universal.

In this passage, the author defines a key proposition type: al-'urfiyya al-khassa (the specific customary). It builds on the general customary by adding the condition of non-permanence with respect to essence. Understanding its composition — both affirmative and negative forms — is essential for grasping how permanence and non-permanence interact in logical propositions. We will also see how it differs from the specific conditional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the proposition being defined: al-'urfiyya al-khassa. Note its relationship to the general customary – it is built upon it.
2
Extract the definition
The specific customary is defined as the general customary with the added restriction of non-permanence (al-ladawam) with respect to essence.
3
Analyze the composition for affirmative form
For an affirmative specific customary, it is composed of an affirmative general customary (first part) and a negative absolute universal (the concept of non-permanence). Example: 'Always every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always' -> first part: 'Always every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer'; second part: 'Nothing of the writer is moving his fingers absolutely.'
The negative absolute universal captures the 'not always' part.
4
Analyze the composition for negative form
For a negative specific customary, it is composed of a negative general customary (first part) and an affirmative absolute universal (non-permanence). Example: 'Nothing of the writer is still as long as he is a writer, not always' -> first part: 'Nothing of the writer is still as long as he is a writer'; second part: 'Every writer is still absolutely.'
Note the reversal: the first part is negative general customary, the second part is affirmative absolute universal.
5
Compare with the specific conditional
The only difference is that the specific customary uses 'always' (da'iman) where the specific conditional uses 'necessarily' (bi-l-darura). The specific customary is more general because necessity implies permanence but not vice versa.

الثانية: العرفية الخاصة (وهي) أي: العرفية الخاصة هي (العرفية العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات وهي أي العرفية الخاصة ( إن كانت موجبة) كقولنا: دائما كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما (فتركيبها ) : أي : تركيب العرفية الخاصة (من موجبة عرفية عامة وهي الجزء الأول، وسالبة مطلقة عامة) وهي مفهوم اللادوام كقولنا: لا شيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالإطلاق العام، (وإن كانت سالبة) كقولنا: لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما، فتركيبها) أي: العرفية الخاصة (من) سالبة عرفية عامة وهي الجزء الأول، وموجبة مطلقة عامة) وهي مفهوم اللادوام كقولنا : كل كاتب ساكن الأصابع بالإطلاق العام (ومثالها) أي: مثال العرفية الخاصة إيجابا وسلبا قد مر) في المشروطة الخاصة بعينه إلا أن الضرورة تُبدل بقولنا: «دائما». وهي أعم من المشروطة الخاصة؛ لأنه متى ثبتت الضرورة بحسب الوصف لا دائما ثبت الدوام بحسبه لا دائما من غير عكس، ومباينة للدائمتين ضرورة تقيدها باللادوام المنافي للدوام وأعم من وجه من المشروطة العامة؛ لصدق المشروطة العامة بدون العرفية الخاصة في مادة الضرورة الذاتية، كقولنا بالضرورة كل إنسان ناطق ما دام إنسانا، وصدق العرفية الخاصة بدون المشروطة العامة في مادة الدوام الصرف بحسب الوصف، وصدقهما معا في مادة المشروطة الخاصة، كقولنا: كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة ما دام كاتبا لا دائما، وأخص من العرفية العامة؛ لأن المقيد أخص من المطلق، وكذا من الباقيين؛ لكونهما أعم من العرفية العامة.
Second: the specific customary (al-'urfiyya al-khassa). It is the general customary with the restriction of non-permanence with respect to essence. It, if affirmative, such as our saying: 'Always every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not always,' so its composition (i.e., the composition of the specific customary) is from an affirmative general customary which is the first part, and a negative absolute universal which is the concept of non-permanence, such as our saying: 'Nothing of the writer is moving his fingers by the absolute universal.' And if it is negative, such as our saying: 'Nothing of the writer is still in fingers as long as he is a writer, not always,' so its composition is from a negative general customary which is the first part, and an affirmative absolute universal which is the concept of non-permanence, such as our saying: 'Every writer is still in fingers by the absolute universal.' And its example (i.e., the example of the specific customary affirmative and negative) has already passed in the specific conditional exactly, except that necessity is replaced by our saying 'always'. And it is more general than the specific conditional; because whenever necessity is established according to the description not always, permanence is established according to it not always without converse. And it is distinct from the two permanents necessarily due to its restriction by non-permanence which contradicts permanence. And it is more general in one respect than the general conditional; because the general conditional is true without the specific customary in the matter of essential necessity, such as our saying: 'By necessity every human is rational as long as he is human.' And the specific customary is true without the general conditional in the matter of pure permanence according to the description. And both are true together in the matter of the specific conditional, such as our saying: 'Every writer is moving his fingers by necessity as long as he is a writer, not always.' And it is more specific than the general customary; because the restricted is more specific than the absolute, and likewise from the remaining two; because they are more general than the general customary.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage defines the 'specific customary' proposition (al-'urfiyya al-khassa). The author explains that it is the general customary (al-'urfiyya al-'amma) with the added restriction of non-permanence (al-ladawam) with respect to essence. Its composition mirrors that of the specific conditional: if affirmative, it consists of an affirmative general customary and a negative absolute universal; if negative, it consists of a negative general customary and an affirmative absolute universal. The examples are the same as those for the specific conditional, except that 'necessity' (al-darura) is replaced by 'always' (da'iman). The passage then compares the specific customary with other propositions: it is more general than the specific conditional (because the establishment of necessity implies establishment of permanence, but not vice versa), it is distinct from the two permanent propositions (since it is restricted by non-permanence), it is more general in one respect than the general conditional (because the general conditional can be true without the specific customary in cases of essential necessity, like 'every human is rational as long as he is human'), and it is more specific than the general customary (because a restricted term is more specific than the absolute). This analysis shows the logical relationships among these proposition types.

Comparative Relations of the Specific Customary with Other Propositions
Comparative analysis of the specific customary with other proposition types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the specific customary proposition with the specific conditional, the general conditional, the two permanent propositions, and the general customary in terms of generality and specificity.

Core Idea
The specific customary's logical relations are defined by its restriction to non-permanence, making it more general than some and more specific than others.

After defining the specific customary, the author systematically compares it with four other proposition types: the specific conditional, the two permanent propositions, the general conditional, and the general customary. These comparisons reveal the precise logical scope of the specific customary and how it fits into the broader family of modal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Compare with the specific conditional
The specific customary is more general (a'amm) than the specific conditional because necessity implies permanence but not vice versa. Example: If something is necessarily true, it is always true; but not every always-true is necessary.
The passage says: 'It is more general than the specific conditional; because whenever necessity is established... permanence is established... without converse.'
2
Compare with the two permanent propositions
The specific customary is distinct (mubayina) from the two permanent propositions because it includes non-permanence, which contradicts permanence. They cannot be true together.
The passage explicitly says it is 'distinct from the two permanents necessarily due to its restriction by non-permanence which contradicts permanence.'
3
Compare with the general conditional
They are more general in one respect (min wajh): the general conditional can be true without the specific customary in matters of essential necessity (e.g., 'By necessity every human is rational as long as he is human' is true but not a specific customary because it is permanent without non-permanence). Conversely, the specific customary can be true without the general conditional in matters of pure permanence (e.g., 'Always every writer moves fingers as long as he writes' is permanent by convention, not necessity). They both hold in the realm of the specific conditional.
4
Compare with the general customary
The specific customary is more specific (akhass) than the general customary because it is the general customary with an added restriction. So every specific customary is a general customary, but not every general customary is specific.
The passage says: 'And it is more specific than the general customary; because the restricted is more specific than the absolute.'
5
Compare with the remaining two (likely the absolute universal and the general conditional?)
The passage says 'and likewise from the remaining two; because they are more general than the general customary.' This likely refers to the absolute universal and the general conditional being more general than the general customary, and thus more general than the specific customary as well.
The text may be alluding to the two permanents? But the wording is ambiguous. For our purposes, note that the specific customary is more specific than any proposition that is more general than the general customary.

Definition and Composition of the Non-Necessary Existential Proposition
Definition of the non-necessary existential proposition and its composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and decompose the non-necessary existential proposition into its absolute and possible components.

Core Idea
The non-necessary existential modal proposition negates necessity while affirming actuality.

In this passage, the author defines the third type of existential modal proposition: the non-necessary existential (al-wujūdiyya al-lāḍurūriyya). This proposition combines an absolute universal (affirmative or negative) with a general possible proposition that differs in quality. Understanding its composition is essential for correctly analyzing modal arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: 'al-wujūdiyya al-lāḍurūriyya' (the non-necessary existential). This is the subject of the definition.
2
Extract the genus
The text says it is 'al-muṭlaqa' (the absolute) with a restriction. So the genus is the absolute existential proposition (qaḍiyya muṭlaqa wujūdiyya).
3
Extract the differentia
The restriction is 'lāḍurūra' (non-necessity) with respect to essence. This distinguishes it from other absolute propositions.
4
Analyze the composition
The non-necessary existential is compound: it has two parts: an absolute universal (affirmative or negative) and a general possible (differing in quality). Verify the pattern for both affirmative and negative cases.
5
Apply with an example
Use the given example: 'Every human is actually laughing, not by necessity.' This means 'Nothing of the human is laughing by the general possibility.' Break it into the absolute part (affirmative absolute universal) and the possible part (negative general possible).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة: (الوجودية اللاضرورية وهي :أي: الوجودية اللاضرورية هي (المطلقة مع قيد اللاضرورة بحسب (الذات وإنما قيد اللاضرورة بحسب الذات - وإن أمكن تقييد المطلقة العامة باللاضرورة بحسب الوصف - ؛ لأنهم لم يعتبروا الوجودية اللاضرورية بحسب الوصف من القضايا المشهورة الكثيرة الاستعمال، ولم يعدوها منها.
( واللاضرورة) عندهم (عبارة) أي: معبرة ( عن ممكنة عامة) مخالفة للجزء الأول في الكيف، موافقة له في الكم، فالوجودية اللاضرورية إن كانت موجبة كقولنا : كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لا بالضرورة أي لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالإمكان العام (فمن موجبة أي فتركيبها من موجبة مطلقة عامة وهى الجزء الأول (وسالبة ممكنة عامة) وهي مفهوم اللاضرورة، وإن كانت أي: الوجودية اللاضرورية سالبة كقولنا : لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل لا (بالضرورة) أي : كل إنسان ضاحك بالإمكان العام (فمن سالبة أي فتركيبها من سالبة (مطلقة عامة وهي الجزء الأول، وموجبة ممكنة عامة وهي مفهوم اللاضرورة.
Third: the non-necessary existential (al-wujūdiyya al-lāḍurūriyya). It is the absolute with the restriction of non-necessity with respect to essence. And non-necessity is restricted with respect to essence – even though it is possible to restrict the absolute universal by non-necessity with respect to description – because they did not consider the non-necessary existential with respect to description among the well-known frequently used propositions, and they did not count it among them. (And non-necessity) according to them is an expression for a general possible (mumkina 'āmma) differing from the first part in quality, agreeing with it in quantity. So the non-necessary existential, if it is affirmative such as our saying: 'Every human is actually laughing, not by necessity,' i.e., nothing of the human is laughing by the general possibility, then from affirmative (i.e., its composition is from an affirmative absolute universal which is the first part, and a negative general possible which is the concept of non-necessity). And if it is negative such as our saying: 'Nothing of the human is actually laughing, not by necessity,' i.e., every human is laughing by the general possibility, then from negative (i.e., its composition is from a negative absolute universal which is the first part, and an affirmative general possible which is the concept of non-necessity).
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage defines the 'non-necessary existential' proposition (al-wujūdiyya al-lāḍurūriyya). The author begins by stating that it is the absolute proposition (al-muṭlaqa) with the added condition of non-necessity (al-lāḍurūra) with respect to essence. He notes that although it is possible to restrict the absolute universal by non-necessity with respect to description, the logicians did not consider that variety among the well-known frequently used propositions. Then he defines 'non-necessity' as an expression for a general possible (mumkina 'āmma) that differs from the first part (the absolute) in quality but agrees in quantity. The composition of the non-necessary existential follows the same pattern as the previous propositions: if affirmative, it is composed of an affirmative absolute universal and a negative general possible; if negative, it is composed of a negative absolute universal and an affirmative general possible. The example given is about humans laughing: 'Every human is actually laughing, not by necessity' means that it is not the case that no human can laugh (i.e., it is possible). This shows how the proposition negates necessity while affirming actual occurrence.

اللاضرورة
الممكنة العامة

Key Takeaway

Defining the Contingent Existential Proposition
Author defines the third composite proposition and its composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the composition of the contingent existential proposition and distinguish it from the absolute and possible propositions.

Core Idea
The contingent existential combines actuality with non-necessity.

In this passage, the author introduces the third modal composite known as al-wujudiyya al-la-daruriyya. Understanding its dual composition—an absolute proposition plus a general possible—is crucial for grasping how modal logic classifies statements about the world. We'll break down the definition and see how it differs from simpler types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its components
The passage calls it 'al-wujudiyya al-la-daruriyya'. Note that it is the absolute proposition with the condition of non-necessity (la-darura) with respect to essence.
The author specifies that only the essential qualification is considered.
2
Analyze the interpretation of non-necessity
The author states that la-darura is a general possible (mumkina 'amma) differing in quality from the first part (the absolute proposition).
General possible is a proposition asserting possibility in a general sense.
3
Construct the affirmative case
For 'Every human is laughing actually, not necessarily' (meaning no human is laughing by general possibility), the composition is an affirmative universal absolute plus a negative general possible.
The negative possible is the contrapositive of 'not necessarily'.
4
Construct the negative case
For 'No human is laughing actually, not necessarily' (meaning every human is laughing by general possibility), the composition is a negative universal absolute plus an affirmative general possible.
The quality of the possible part flips relative to the absolute.
5
Summarize the pattern
The quality of the absolute part is preserved, and the possible part has the opposite quality. This pattern holds for both affirmative and negative forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة: (الوجودية اللاضرورية) وهي المطلقة مع قيد اللاضرورة بحسب الذات. وإنما قيد اللاضرورة بحسب الذات - وإن أمكن تقييد المطلقة العامة باللاضرورة بحسب الوصف - لأنهم لم يعتبروا الوجودية اللاضرورية بحسب الوصف من القضايا المشهورة الكثيرة الاستعمال، ولم يعدوها منها. واللاضرورة عندهم عبارة عن ممكنة عامة مخالفة للجزء الأول في الكيف، موافقة له في الكم. فالوجودية اللاضرورية إن كانت موجبة كقولنا: كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لا بالضرورة أي لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالإمكان العام فمن موجبة أي فتركيبها من موجبة مطلقة عامة وهي الجزء الأول وسالبة ممكنة عامة وهي مفهوم اللاضرورة. وإن كانت الوجودية اللاضرورية سالبة كقولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل لا بالضرورة أي كل إنسان ضاحك بالإمكان العام فمن سالبة أي فتركيبها من سالبة مطلقة عامة وهي الجزء الأول وموجبة ممكنة عامة وهي مفهوم اللاضرورة. وهي أعم من الخاصتين؛ لأن صدق الضرورة أو الدوام بحسب الوصف لا دائما يستلزم صدق فعلية النسبة لا بالضرورة من غير عكس كلي. ومباينة للضرورية؛ ضرورة تقيدها باللاضرورة المنافية للضرورة. وأعم من الدائمة من وجه لصدقهما معا في مادة الدوام الصرف أي الخالي عن الضرورة، وصدق الدائمة بدونها في مادة الضرورة الذاتية، وبالعكس في مادة اللادوام. وكذا من المشروطة والعرفية العامتين؛ لصدقها في مادة المشروطة الخاصة، وصدقهما بدونها في مادة الضرورة الذاتية وبالعكس في مادة اللادوام بحسب الوصف. وأخص من المطلقة العامة؛ لأن المقيد أخص من المطلق، ومن الممكنة العامة؛ لأنها أعم من المطلقة العامة.
The third: the contingent existential proposition (al-wujudiyya al-la-daruriyya). It is the absolute [proposition] with the condition of non-necessity (la-darura) with respect to essence (bi-hasab al-dhat). The condition of non-necessity with respect to essence was specified (although it is possible to restrict the universal absolute by non-necessity with respect to description) because they did not consider the contingent existential with respect to description among the well-known and frequently used propositions, and they did not count it among them. And 'non-necessity' with them is an expression for a general possible (mumkina 'amma) differing from the first part in quality, agreeing with it in quantity. So the contingent existential, if it is affirmative as in our saying: 'Every human is laughing actually, not necessarily' — meaning no human is laughing by general possibility — then it is from an affirmative [component], i.e., its composition is from an affirmative universal absolute, which is the first part, and a negative general possible, which is the concept of non-necessity. And if the contingent existential is negative as in our saying: 'No human is laughing actually, not necessarily' — meaning every human is laughing by general possibility — then it is from a negative [component], i.e., its composition is from a negative universal absolute, which is the first part, and an affirmative general possible, which is the concept of non-necessity. And it is broader than the two particular [propositions] (al-khassatayn) because the truth of necessity or perpetuity according to description not always entails the truth of the actuality of the relation not necessarily, without full converse. And it is disjoint from the necessary (al-daruriyya) due to its restriction by non-necessity which contradicts necessity. And it is broader than the perpetual (al-da'ima) from one aspect: for their truth together in the matter of pure perpetuity, i.e., devoid of necessity, and the truth of the perpetual without it in the matter of essential necessity, and vice versa in the matter of non-perpetuity. Likewise it is [broader] than the general conditioned (al-mashruta) and general customary (al-'urfiyya al-'amma): for its truth in the matter of the particular conditioned, and their truth without it in the matter of essential necessity, and vice versa in the matter of non-perpetuity according to description. And it is narrower than the universal absolute (al-mutlaqa al-'amma) because the restricted is narrower than the unrestricted, and [narrower] than the general possible (al-mumkina al-'amma) because it [the general possible] is broader than the universal absolute.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage defines the third composite proposition known as al-wujudiyya al-la-daruriyya (the contingent existential). The author begins by stating that it is the absolute proposition restricted by non-necessity with respect to essence, explaining why only the essential qualification is considered: the descriptive qualification is not commonly used. Then he clarifies that 'non-necessity' is interpreted as a general possible proposition (mumkina 'amma) which differs in quality from the first part. He provides examples: for the affirmative case, 'Every human is laughing actually, not necessarily' means 'No human is laughing by general possibility' — thus the proposition combines an affirmative absolute with a negative possible. For the negative case, 'No human is laughing actually, not necessarily' means 'Every human is laughing by general possibility' — combining a negative absolute with an affirmative possible. After this, the author compares this third type with other modal propositions, showing where it is broader, narrower, or disjoint. This detailed comparison helps in understanding how modal propositions relate in terms of extension and logical implications.

Key Terms
الوجودية اللاضرورية
المطلقة العامة
الممكنة العامة

B) An affirmative absolute universal and a negative general possible.
C) A negative absolute universal and an affirmative general possible.
D) A negative absolute universal and a negative general possible.

Key Takeaway
The contingent existential proposition is a composite of a universal absolute and a general possible of opposite quality.

Comparing the Contingent Existential with Other Modal Types
Author compares contingent existential to other modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the logical relationships (broader, narrower, disjoint) between the contingent existential and other modal types such as necessary, perpetual, and conditioned.

Core Idea
The contingent existential is broader than some, narrower than others, and disjoint from necessity.

After defining the contingent existential, the author systematically compares it to other modal propositions: the two particular propositions (khassatayn), the necessary (daruriyya), the perpetual (da'ima), and the conditioned/customary. These comparisons reveal how modal types relate in terms of truth conditions, enabling precise reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first comparison: with the two particular propositions
The passage says it is broader than the two particular propositions. Reason: truth of necessity or perpetuity does not always entail actuality without necessity.
The 'two particular' are the necessary and perpetual.
2
Identify the second comparison: with the necessary
It is disjoint from the necessary because it is restricted by non-necessity, which contradicts necessity.
No proposition can be both necessary and contingent in the same sense.
3
Identify the third comparison: with the perpetual
It is broader than the perpetual from one aspect: they both hold in 'pure perpetuity' (always true without necessity), but differ in matter of essential necessity and non-perpetuity.
The perpetual is true in essential necessity while contingent existential is false there.
4
Identify comparisons with conditioned/customary
Similar to perpetual: contingent existential is true in the matter of particular conditioned, while conditioned/customary are true in other matters.
The 'general conditioned' and 'general customary' are two other modal types.
5
Identify the final comparisons: with absolute and possible
It is narrower than the universal absolute (because restricted adds a condition) and narrower than the general possible (because the possible is broader than absolute).
General possible includes both necessary and contingent, so it is wider.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة: (الوجودية اللاضرورية) وهي المطلقة مع قيد اللاضرورة بحسب الذات. وإنما قيد اللاضرورة بحسب الذات - وإن أمكن تقييد المطلقة العامة باللاضرورة بحسب الوصف - لأنهم لم يعتبروا الوجودية اللاضرورية بحسب الوصف من القضايا المشهورة الكثيرة الاستعمال، ولم يعدوها منها. واللاضرورة عندهم عبارة عن ممكنة عامة مخالفة للجزء الأول في الكيف، موافقة له في الكم. فالوجودية اللاضرورية إن كانت موجبة كقولنا: كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لا بالضرورة أي لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالإمكان العام فمن موجبة أي فتركيبها من موجبة مطلقة عامة وهي الجزء الأول وسالبة ممكنة عامة وهي مفهوم اللاضرورة. وإن كانت الوجودية اللاضرورية سالبة كقولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل لا بالضرورة أي كل إنسان ضاحك بالإمكان العام فمن سالبة أي فتركيبها من سالبة مطلقة عامة وهي الجزء الأول وموجبة ممكنة عامة وهي مفهوم اللاضرورة. وهي أعم من الخاصتين؛ لأن صدق الضرورة أو الدوام بحسب الوصف لا دائما يستلزم صدق فعلية النسبة لا بالضرورة من غير عكس كلي. ومباينة للضرورية؛ ضرورة تقيدها باللاضرورة المنافية للضرورة. وأعم من الدائمة من وجه لصدقهما معا في مادة الدوام الصرف أي الخالي عن الضرورة، وصدق الدائمة بدونها في مادة الضرورة الذاتية، وبالعكس في مادة اللادوام. وكذا من المشروطة والعرفية العامتين؛ لصدقها في مادة المشروطة الخاصة، وصدقهما بدونها في مادة الضرورة الذاتية وبالعكس في مادة اللادوام بحسب الوصف. وأخص من المطلقة العامة؛ لأن المقيد أخص من المطلق، ومن الممكنة العامة؛ لأنها أعم من المطلقة العامة.

Defining the Non-Perpetual Existential
Author defines the fourth composite proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the non-perpetual existential proposition and construct its composition from two absolute propositions.

Core Idea
The non-perpetual existential says something is actually true, but not always.

In this passage, the author defines the fourth type of composite proposition: al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima, or the non-perpetual existential. This proposition is built from two absolute (non-modal) statements: one affirmative and one negative. Understanding its construction is essential for distinguishing it from other modal propositions like the necessary existential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base: universal absolute
The passage states that the non-perpetual existential is the universal absolute (al-mutlaqa al-'amma) with an added condition. First, recognize that the underlying proposition is an absolute statement without modality.
The universal absolute is a categorical statement without any temporal or necessity qualification.
2
Add the condition of non-perpetuity
The added condition is qayd al-la-dawam: the actuality does not hold at all times. This transforms the absolute into a composite that asserts existence but not perpetuity.
Non-perpetuity is a negative condition; it denies that the state holds at every time.
3
Recognize the dual composition
Whether affirmative or negative, the non-perpetual existential is composed of two absolute propositions: one affirmative and one negative. For example, 'Every human is laughing actually, not always' combines an affirmative absolute and a negative absolute.
The negative part negates the perpetuity of the affirmative.
4
Relate to the necessary existential example
The author notes that the example parallels the third type (al-wujudiyya al-daruriyya), but replaces 'not necessarily' with 'not always'. This highlights the shift from contingency to non-perpetuity.
The difference lies in the condition: necessity vs. perpetuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة من المركبات (الوجودية اللادائمة) وهي المطلقة العامة مع زيادة قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات. وهي سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة فمن مطلقتين عامتين: إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة. ومثالها ما مر في الوجودية الضرورية، غير أنك تبدل قولك: «لا بالضرورة» بقولك: «لا دائما» كقولك: كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لا دائما، ولا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل لا دائما. وهي أخص من الوجودية اللاضرورية؛ لأن صدق المطلقتين يستلزم صدق المطلقة والممكنة من غير عكس، وأعم من الخاصتين؛ لأن اللادوام مشترك، والإطلاق الفعلي أعم من الضرورة والدوام الوصفيين، ومباينة للدائمتين، وهو ظاهر، وأعم من وجه من العامتين؛ لصدق الجميع في مادة المشروطة الخاصة، والافتراق في مادة الدوام الذاتي، ومادة اللادوام الوصفي، وأخص من المطلقة والممكنة العامتين، وهو ظاهر.
The fourth of the composites: the non-perpetual existential (al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima). It is the universal absolute with the addition of the condition of non-perpetuity (la-dawam) with respect to essence. And it, whether affirmative or negative, is from two absolute [propositions], one affirmative and the other negative. Its example is what was mentioned for the necessary existential (al-wujudiyya al-daruriyya), except that you replace your saying 'not necessarily' with 'not always', as in your saying: 'Every human is laughing actually, not always', and 'No human is laughing actually, not always'. And it is narrower than the contingent existential (al-wujudiyya al-la-daruriyya) because the truth of the two absolute propositions entails the truth of the absolute and the possible without converse. And it is broader than the two particular [propositions] because non-perpetuity is common, and actual absoluteness is broader than the necessity and perpetuity of description. And it is disjoint from the two perpetual [propositions] (al-da'imatayn), and that is clear. And it is broader from one aspect than the two general [propositions] (al-'ammatayn) because all are true in the matter of the particular conditioned, and they differ in the matter of essential perpetuity and the matter of descriptive non-perpetuity. And it is narrower than the universal absolute and the general possible, and that is clear.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth composite proposition, al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima (the non-perpetual existential). The author explains that it is the universal absolute (al-mutlaqa al-'amma) with an added condition of non-perpetuity (la-dawam) with respect to essence. Regardless of whether it is affirmative or negative, its composition always involves two absolute propositions: one affirmative and one negative. The example given mirrors the earlier example for the necessary existential, but replaces 'not necessarily' with 'not always'. For instance, 'Every human is laughing actually, not always' means that the laughing is actual but not perpetual; this is equivalent to affirming an absolute and a negative absolute. Similarly, 'No human is laughing actually, not always' means that the lack of laughing is actual but not perpetual. The author then compares this type with others: it is narrower than the contingent existential (third type) because the conjunction of two absolutes implies the combination of an absolute and a possible but not vice versa; it is broader than the two particular propositions (the necessary and perpetual according to description) because non-perpetuity is a common factor and actual absoluteness is broader than descriptive modalities; it is disjoint from the two perpetual propositions; it is broader from one aspect than the two general propositions (conditioned and customary) because they all agree in some matters and differ in others; and finally, it is narrower than the universal absolute and general possible. These relations help situate this fourth type within the modal system.

Key Terms
الوجودية اللادائمة
اللادوام
المطلقة العامة

Relations of the Non-Perpetual Existential to Other Modal Propositions
Author compares the fourth composite to other modal types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether the non-perpetual existential is narrower, broader, or disjoint from another modal proposition based on the conditions of perpetuity and absoluteness.

Core Idea
The non-perpetual existential sits between the contingent and perpetual types, being narrower than the contingent but broader than the particular descriptive ones.

The author not only defines the fourth composite but also maps out its logical relationships with other modal propositions: the contingent existential, the particular propositions, the perpetual ones, the general propositions, and the universal absolute and possible. Understanding these relations helps locate this type within the modal hierarchy and clarifies when it can be used in arguments.

Guided Path
1
Compare with the contingent existential
The non-perpetual existential is narrower (akhas) than the contingent existential (al-wujudiyya al-la-daruriyya) because the conjunction of its two absolutes entails the combination of an absolute and a possible, but not vice versa.
Contingency involves possibility, non-perpetuity involves actuality without continuation.
2
Compare with the two particular propositions
It is broader (a'am) than the two particular propositions (al-khassatayn: the necessary and perpetual according to description) because non-perpetuity is a common factor and actual absoluteness is broader than descriptive modalities.
Descriptive modalities tie the attribute to the subject's description, while actual absoluteness is unrestricted.
3
Check disjointness from the two perpetual propositions
It is disjoint (mubayina) from the two perpetual propositions (al-da'imatayn: the necessary and perpetual in the strict sense). There is no overlap because perpetuity and non-perpetuity are contradictory.
This is obvious from the definitions.
4
Examine the relation to the two general propositions
It is broader from one aspect (a'am min wajh) than the two general propositions (al-'ammatayn: the conditioned and customary) because all can be true in the matter of the particular conditioned, but they differ in cases of essential perpetuity and descriptive non-perpetuity.
This is a complex relation of partial overlap.
5
Contrast with the universal absolute and general possible
It is narrower (akhas) than the universal absolute (al-mutlaqa al-'amma) and the general possible (al-mumkina al-'amma). This is because the non-perpetual existential imposes an additional condition.
The universal absolute has no modal condition; the general possible includes possibility.

The Existence-Non-Permanent Modal Proposition: Definition, Composition, and Logical Scope
Defines the existence-non-permanent proposition and its composition from two absolute propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the existence-non-permanent proposition, identify its composition from two absolute propositions, and compare its logical scope with related modal propositions.

Core Idea
The existence-non-permanent proposition is the general absolute with the restriction of non-permanence in essence.

We now turn to the fourth type of compound modal proposition: the existence-non-permanent (al-wujūdiyya al-lā dā'ima). This proposition builds on the general absolute by adding the condition that the predicate does not hold permanently in essence. Understanding its composition and how it relates to other modal propositions is crucial for accurately classifying statements in logic and Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its components
Find the definition: the existence-non-permanent is the general absolute (al-muṭlaqa al-'āmma) plus the restriction of non-permanence in essence (qayd al-lā dawām bi-ḥasab al-dhāt). The genus is the general absolute, and the differentia is non-permanence.
Note that this is a real definition (hadd) using genus and differentia.
2
Analyze the composition from two absolute propositions
The passage states that every existence-non-permanent proposition, whether affirmative or negative, is composed of two general absolute propositions: one affirmative and one negative. This is because non-permanence requires both that the predicate applies actually (affirmative) and that it does not apply always (negative).
This composition is unique to this proposition; other compound propositions may have different compositions.
3
Examine the example
The example given is 'Every human is actually laughing not always' (affirmative) and 'No human is actually laughing not always' (negative). Replace 'not necessarily' from the earlier necessary-existence example with 'not always'.
The example illustrates the 'not always' condition corresponding to non-permanence.
4
Compare with other modal propositions using the scope relations
The passage lists five scope relations: more specific than existence-non-necessary, more general than the two specials, opposite to the two permanents, more general in one respect than the two generals, and more specific than general absolute and general possible. Trace each relation by considering truth conditions.
These relations are essential for accurate syllogistic and argument evaluation in kalam.

الرابعة من المركبات (الوجودية اللادائمة وهي :أي: الوجودية اللادائمة هي المطلقة العامة مع زيادة قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات وهي أي: الوجودية اللادائمة (سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة فمن مطلقتين أي فتركيبها من مطلقتين (عامتين: إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة، ومثالها ما مر في الوجودية الضرورية، غير أنك تبدل قولك: «لا» «بالضرورة بقولك : لا «دائما كقولك : كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لا دائما، ولا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل لا دائما.

The fourth of the compound [propositions]: the existence-non-permanent. It is: the existence-non-permanent is the general absolute with the addition of the restriction of non-permanence in terms of essence. And it, the existence-non-permanent, whether affirmative or negative, is composed of two absolute [propositions]: i.e., its composition is from two general absolute [propositions], one affirmative and the other negative. Its example is what passed in the necessary existence, except that you replace your saying 'not necessarily' with your saying 'not always', like your saying: every human is actually laughing not always, and no human is actually laughing not always.

It is more specific than the existence-non-necessary; because the truth of the two absolutes entails the truth of the absolute and the possible without converse. And it is more general than the two special [propositions]; because non-permanence is common, and actual absoluteness is more general than the two descriptive necessity and permanence. And it is opposite to the two permanent [propositions]; that is apparent. And it is more general in one respect than the two general [propositions]; because all are true in the matter of the special conditional, and they differ in the matter of essential permanence and the matter of descriptive non-permanence. And it is more specific than the general absolute and general possible; that is apparent.

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of compound modal proposition: the existence-non-permanent (al-wujūdiyya al-lā dā'ima). It begins by stating that this proposition is the general absolute (al-muṭlaqa al-'āmma) with the added restriction of non-permanence with respect to essence (bi-ḥasab al-dhāt). The author clarifies that whether affirmative or negative, it is composed of two general absolute propositions: one affirmative and one negative. The example given parallels the earlier example of the necessary existence proposition, but with 'not always' replacing 'not necessarily': 'Every human is actually laughing not always' (affirmative) and 'No human is actually laughing not always' (negative).

Following the definition, the passage compares this proposition to others in terms of logical scope. It is more specific than the existence-non-necessary (al-wujūdiyya al-lā ḍarūriyya) because the truth of the two absolutes entails the truth of the absolute and the possible, but not conversely. It is more general than the two special propositions (al-khāṣṣatān) because non-permanence is a common factor, and actual absoluteness is more general than descriptive necessity and permanence. It is opposite (mubāyina) to the two permanent propositions. It is more general in one respect than the two general propositions (al-'āmmatān) because all four are true in the matter of the special conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa), but they differ in matters of essential permanence and descriptive non-permanence. Finally, it is more specific than the general absolute and general possible, which is clear.

The Temporal Proposition: Composition and Relations
Defines temporal proposition and its composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decompose an affirmative or negative temporal proposition into its constituent parts and identify its relation to other modal propositions.

Core Idea
The temporal proposition combines necessity at a specific time with essential non-permanence.

The temporal proposition (al-waqtiyya) is a key modal type in Islamic logic. It asserts a predicate necessarily at a specific time while denying its permanence in essence. Understanding its compound structure is essential for analyzing temporally restricted statements in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the temporal proposition's definition
Find the definition in the passage: it judges with necessity the affirmation or negation of the predicate for the subject at a specific time, restricted by the condition of non-permanence in essence.
The definition has two key conditions: temporal necessity and essential non-permanence.
2
Analyze the composition for an affirmative temporal
For an affirmative temporal like 'necessarily every moon is eclipsed at time T, not always', note that it is composed of an affirmative temporal absolute (the simple part) and a negative general absolute (the concept of non-permanence).
The affirmative temporal absolute is itself simple but not counted among the simples because it is part of a compound.
3
Analyze the composition for a negative temporal
For a negative temporal like 'necessarily nothing of the moon is eclipsed at time T, not always', it is composed of a negative temporal absolute and an affirmative general absolute (the concept of non-permanence).
The pattern swaps: the absolute part becomes the opposite quality while the temporal part remains the same.
4
Relate the temporal to other proposition types
The passage compares the temporal to the two existence propositions and the two special propositions. Understand that the temporal is more specific than the existence propositions, and its relation to the special propositions is one of partial overlap.
Use the moon eclipse examples to grasp the logical relations: when the temporal is true, the existence propositions are also true, but not vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة (الوقتية (وهي) أي: الوقتية (التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه أي عن الموضوع في وقت معين من أوقات وجود الموضوع مقيدا بقيد اللادوام بحسب الذات وهى أي الوقتية إن كانت موجبة كقولنا بالضرورة كل قمر منخسف وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس لا دائما، فمن موجبة أي فتركيبها من موجبة وقتية مطلقة وهى الجزء الأول، البسيطة الغير المعدودة في البسائط، وسالبة مطلقة عامة وهي مفهوم اللادوام، أعني قولنا: لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف بالإطلاق العام، وإن كانت سالبة كقولنا : بالضرورة لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لا دائما، فمن سالبة وقتية مطلقة، وموجبة مطلقة عامة) وهي مفهوم اللادوام ، وهي قولنا كل قمر منخسف بالإطلاق العام.
وهي
أخص من الوجوديتين؛ لأنه متى صدقت الضرورة بحسب وقت معين مع اللادوام بحسب الذات صدق الإطلاق العام (۱) مع اللادوام واللاضرورة بحسب الذات(٢) من غير عكس، ومن الخاصتين من وجه لصدق الجميع في مادة الضرورة الوصفية مع اللادوام الذاتي، إذا كان الوصف ضروريا للذات بحسب وقت ما، كقولنا: كل منخسف مظلم مادام منخسفا لا دائما (۳)، وصدقهما بدون الوقتية إذا لم يكن الوصف ضروريا لذات الموضوع في وقت ما كقولنا كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع وبالعكس حيث لا يصدق الضرورة ولا الدوام بحسب الوصف كقولنا كل قمر منخسف وقـت حيلولة الأرض بينـه وبـين الشمس لا دائما؛ إذ يمتنع أن يصدق أن الانخساف دائم ما دام القمر قمرا.
The fifth: the temporal. It is: the temporal in which judgment is made about the necessary affirmation of the predicate to the subject or its negation from the subject at a specific time among the times of the subject's existence, restricted by the restriction of non-permanence in terms of essence. And it, the temporal, if affirmative, like our saying: necessarily every moon is eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, not always, then it is composed of an affirmative temporal absolute—that is the first part, a simple not counted among the simples—and a negative general absolute, which is the concept of non-permanence, namely our saying: nothing of the moon is eclipsed in the general absolute sense. And if negative, like our saying: necessarily nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always, then it is composed of a negative temporal absolute and an affirmative general absolute—which is the concept of non-permanence—and it is our saying: every moon is eclipsed in the general absolute sense.

It is more specific than the two existence [propositions]; because when the necessity according to a specific time together with non-permanence in essence is true, the general absolute with non-permanence and non-necessity in essence is true, without converse. And it is [more specific and general] in one respect than the two special [propositions]; because all are true in the matter of descriptive necessity with essential non-permanence, if the description is necessary for the essence in some time, like our saying: every eclipsed [thing] is dark as long as it is eclipsed, not always; and both [the two special] are true without the temporal if the description is not necessary for the essence of the subject at any time, like our saying: every writer is finger-moving, and conversely when neither necessity nor permanence according to description is true, like our saying: every moon is eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, not always; for it is impossible that it be true that the eclipse is permanent as long as the moon is a moon.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth type of compound modal proposition: the temporal (al-waqtiyya). It begins by stating that the temporal proposition judges with necessity the affirmation or negation of the predicate for the subject at a specific time among the times of the subject's existence, and it is restricted by the condition of non-permanence in essence. 

The author then explains its composition separately for affirmative and negative cases. If affirmative, e.g., 'necessarily every moon is eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, not always', it is composed of an affirmative temporal absolute (the simple part, which is not counted among the simples because it is compound) and a negative general absolute (the concept of non-permanence: 'nothing of the moon is eclipsed in the general absolute sense'). If negative, e.g., 'necessarily nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always', it is composed of a negative temporal absolute and an affirmative general absolute (the concept of non-permanence: 'every moon is eclipsed in the general absolute sense').

Following the definition, the passage compares the temporal to other types. It is more specific than the two existence propositions (al-wujūdiyyatān, i.e., necessary existence and existence-non-necessary? The text says 'the two existence [propositions]' likely meaning the necessary and non-necessary existence) because whenever the necessity at a specific time with non-permanence in essence is true, the general absolute with non-permanence and non-necessity in essence is true, but not vice versa. It is related to the two special propositions (al-khāṣṣatān, i.e., the descriptive necessary and descriptive permanent) in a one-way specific/general relation: they are true together in the matter of descriptive necessity with essential non-permanence, e.g., 'every eclipsed thing is dark as long as it is eclipsed, not always', but the two special propositions can be true without the temporal when the description is not necessary for the essence at any time, e.g., 'every writer is finger-moving', and conversely the temporal can be true without the two special when neither necessity nor permanence according to description holds, e.g., the eclipse example, because it is impossible for the eclipse to be permanent as long as the moon exists.

Definition and Composition of the Temporal Proposition
Definition and composition of the temporal proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the temporal proposition and decompose an affirmative or negative temporal proposition into its two constituent parts.

Core Idea
A temporal proposition binds a predicate to a specific time while denying its permanent validity.

In this lesson, we examine the temporal proposition as defined by the author. You will learn what makes a proposition 'temporal' and how it is constructed from a temporal absolute and a universal absolute component. Understanding this composition is essential for distinguishing temporal propositions from other types in the logical system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the temporal marker
Look for words like 'at the time of' (وقت) and the phrase 'not always' (لا دائما). These indicate temporal restriction.
2
Find the necessary judgment
The proposition states 'necessarily every S is P' or 'necessarily no S is P' at that specific time.
3
Extract the two components
For affirmative: an affirmative temporal absolute (the simple temporal) and a universal absolute negative (the non-permanence clause). For negative: a negative temporal absolute and a universal absolute affirmative.
The temporal absolute is the part that asserts the predicate at the specific time; the universal absolute part expresses the 'not always' or 'always' opposite.
4
Verify with examples
Test with the moon eclipse example: 'Necessarily every moon is eclipsed at opposition, not always' -> components: 'Every moon is eclipsed at opposition' (affirmative temporal absolute) and 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed absolutely' (universal absolute negative).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة (الوقتية (وهي) أي: الوقتية (التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه أي عن الموضوع في وقت معين من أوقات وجود الموضوع مقيدا بقيد اللادوام بحسب الذات وهى أي الوقتية إن كانت موجبة كقولنا بالضرورة كل قمر منخسف وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس لا دائما، فمن موجبة أي فتركيبها من موجبة وقتية مطلقة وهى الجزء الأول، البسيطة الغير المعدودة في البسائط، وسالبة مطلقة عامة وهي مفهوم اللادوام، أعني قولنا: لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف بالإطلاق العام، وإن كانت سالبة كقولنا : بالضرورة لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لا دائما، فمن سالبة وقتية مطلقة، وموجبة مطلقة عامة) وهي مفهوم اللادوام ، وهي قولنا كل قمر منخسف بالإطلاق العام. وهي أخص من الوجوديتين؛ لأنه متى صدقت الضرورة بحسب وقت معين مع اللادوام بحسب الذات صدق الإطلاق العام (۱) مع اللادوام واللاضرورة بحسب الذات(٢) من غير عكس، ومن الخاصتين من وجه لصدق الجميع في مادة الضرورة الوصفية مع اللادوام الذاتي، إذا كان الوصف ضروريا للذات بحسب وقت ما، كقولنا: كل منخسف مظلم مادام منخسفا لا دائما (۳)، وصدقهما بدون الوقتية إذا لم يكن الوصف ضروريا لذات الموضوع في وقت ما كقولنا كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع وبالعكس حيث لا يصدق الضرورة ولا الدوام بحسب الوصف كقولنا كل قمر منخسف وقـت حيلولة الأرض بينـه وبـين الشمس لا دائما؛ إذ يمتنع أن يصدق أن الانخساف دائم ما دام القمر قمرا. وذهب بعضهم إلى أن المشروطة الخاصة أخص مطلقا من الوقتية؛ لامتناع صدق المشروطة الخاصة بدونها؛ لأنه متى صدقت الضرورة بشرط الوصف أو ما دام الوصف لا دائما صدقت الضرورة بحسب الوقت المعين، وهو وقت وجود الوصف لا دائما، فيصدق في قولنا : كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة في وقت الكتابة، ولا يخفى فساده، ومنشأه عدم الفرق بين الضرورة بشرط الوصف وما دام الوصف وقد حققناه، فلا تغفل عنه. نعم إذا فسر المشروطة الخاصة بالضرورة ما دام الوصف فيكون المشروطة الخاصة أخص من من الوقتية مطلقا، لأنه متى تحققت الضرورة في جميع أوقات الوصف -وجميع أوقات الوصف بعض أوقات الذات تحققت الضرورة في بعض أوقات الذات من غير عكس كلي. ومن العامتين أيضا من وجه؛ لتصادقها في مادة المشروطة الخاصة، وصدقهما بدونها في مادة الضرورة؛ لكذب اللادوام حينئذ، وبالعكس حيث لا دوام بحسب الوصف كالانخساف للقمر، ومباينة للدائمتين، وأخص من المطلقة والممكنة العامتين، وذلك ظاهر.
The fifth (temporal). It is the temporal proposition in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or its negation from it, i.e., from the subject, at a specific time among the times of the subject's existence, qualified by the restriction of non-permanence with respect to essence. And the temporal, if it is affirmative, such as our saying: 'Necessarily every moon is eclipsed at the time of the Earth's interposition between it and the sun, not always,' then it consists of an affirmative absolute temporal proposition, which is the first part, the simple one not counted among the simple propositions, and a universal absolute negative, which is the concept of non-permanence, i.e., our saying: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed with absolute universality.' And if it is negative, such as our saying: 'Necessarily nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always,' then it consists of a negative absolute temporal proposition and a universal absolute affirmative, which is the concept of non-permanence, and it is our saying: 'Every moon is eclipsed with absolute universality.' And it is more specific than the two existential propositions; because whenever the necessity according to a specific time together with non-permanence according to essence is true, the absolute universality is true with non-permanence and non-necessity according to essence, without reciprocity. And it is related to the two special propositions by mutual partial overlap (min wajh), because all are true in the matter of descriptive necessity with essential non-permanence, when the description is necessary for the essence according to some time, such as our saying: 'Every eclipsed thing is dark as long as it is eclipsed, not always'; and they are true without the temporal when the description is not necessary for the essence of the subject at any time, such as our saying: 'Every writer moves his fingers', and conversely where neither necessity nor permanence according to the description is true, such as our saying: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of the Earth's interposition between it and the sun, not always'; for it is impossible that it be true that the eclipse is permanent as long as the moon is a moon. And some have held that the special conditional proposition is absolutely more specific than the temporal; because it is impossible for the special conditional to be true without it; because whenever the necessity with the condition of the description or as long as the description is true, not always, then the necessity according to the specific time is true, which is the time of the existence of the description, not always. So it is true in our saying: 'Every writer necessarily moves his fingers at the time of writing.' And its corruption is not hidden, and its origin is the failure to distinguish between the necessity with the condition of the description and the necessity as long as the description, and we have verified it, so do not neglect it. Yes, if the special conditional is interpreted as the necessity as long as the description, then the special conditional is absolutely more specific than the temporal, because whenever the necessity is realized in all times of the description—and all times of the description are some times of the essence—the necessity is realized in some times of the essence, without universal reciprocity. And it is also related to the two universal propositions by mutual partial overlap, because they are true simultaneously in the matter of the special conditional, and they are true without it in the matter of necessity, because then the non-permanence is false, and conversely where there is no permanence according to the description, like the eclipse for the moon. And it is disjoint from the two permanent propositions, and more specific than the absolute and the universal possible, and that is apparent.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines the temporal proposition (al-waqtiyya), the fifth type of quantified proposition. The author begins by stating its definition: it judges the necessity or impossibility of the predicate for the subject at a specific time of the subject's existence, with the restriction of non-permanence (al-ladawam) with respect to essence. This means the proposition does not claim the predicate holds for all times. The author then explains its composition: an affirmative temporal proposition consists of an affirmative absolute temporal (a simple proposition asserting the predicate at the specific time) combined with a universal absolute negative expressing non-permanence. Similarly, a negative temporal is composed of a negative absolute temporal and a universal absolute affirmative. The author uses examples from astronomy: the moon's eclipse at opposition (for affirmative) and at quadrature (for negative), illustrating the temporal restriction. Next, the author compares the temporal proposition to other types. It is more specific (akhaṣṣ) than the two existential propositions (al-wujūdiyyatayn) because when temporal necessity holds, the absolute universal (i.e., the existence of the predicate at some time) also holds, but not vice versa. It has a reciprocal partial overlap (min wajh) with the two special propositions (al-khāṣṣatayn, i.e., the conditioned and the permanent-conditioned). They agree in cases of descriptive necessity with essential non-permanence, e.g., 'Every eclipsed thing is dark as long as it is eclipsed.' They diverge when the description is not essential. The author then addresses an objection: some claim the special conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa) is absolutely more specific than the temporal. He refutes this by distinguishing two interpretations of the special conditional: 'with the condition of the description' and 'as long as the description.' The former does not yield temporal necessity in all cases (e.g., the writer example). Only if interpreted as 'as long as the description' does it become more specific. The author insists this distinction is crucial. Finally, he notes the temporal also has a partial overlap with the two universal propositions, is disjoint from the two permanent propositions, and is more specific than the absolute and universal possible. This exhaustive comparison shows the precise logical relationships among proposition types.

اللادوام
المطلقة العامة

A) An affirmative temporal absolute and a universal absolute negative.
B) An affirmative temporal absolute and a universal absolute affirmative.
C) A negative temporal absolute and a universal absolute negative.
D) A negative temporal absolute and a universal absolute affirmative.

Key Takeaway
The temporal proposition is a composite of a specific-time necessity judgment and a non-permanence clause, making it distinct from existential and permanent propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

2. Construct an original affirmative temporal proposition from the domain of fiqh (e.g., regarding prayer times) and break it down.
