Relations of the Temporal Proposition with Other Types
Comparative relations of the temporal proposition with other types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the temporal proposition with the existential, special, universal, and permanent propositions in terms of specificity and overlap.

Core Idea
The temporal proposition is more specific than existential but overlaps partially with special and universal types.

The author systematically compares the temporal proposition to other proposition types. You will learn the precise logical relationships: which type is more general, which overlap, and which are disjoint. This comparative analysis is crucial for navigating the hierarchy of propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compared types
The author compares with: existential (wujūdiyyatayn), special (khāṣṣatayn), universal (āmmatayn), permanent (dā’imatayn), and absolute and possible (mutlaqa and mumkina).
2
Understand 'akhaṣṣ' and 'min wajh'
'Akhaṣṣ' means the temporal implies the other but not vice versa. 'Min wajh' means they agree in some cases and disagree in others.
3
Apply to each relation
For existential: temporal is more specific. For special: partial overlap. Use the 'writer' and 'moon' examples to see when they align and diverge.
The writer example shows the special conditional can be true without temporal necessity.
4
Analyze the debate over special conditional
The author refutes the claim that special conditional is absolutely more specific, distinguishing two interpretations: 'with the condition' and 'as long as'. Only 'as long as' yields absolute specificity.
The example 'Every writer moves fingers at the time of writing' shows the failure of the 'with condition' interpretation.
5
Check the remaining relations
Temporal is partially overlapping with universal, disjoint from permanent, and more specific than absolute/possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة (الوقتية (وهي) أي: الوقتية (التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه أي عن الموضوع في وقت معين من أوقات وجود الموضوع مقيدا بقيد اللادوام بحسب الذات وهى أي الوقتية إن كانت موجبة كقولنا بالضرورة كل قمر منخسف وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس لا دائما، فمن موجبة أي فتركيبها من موجبة وقتية مطلقة وهى الجزء الأول، البسيطة الغير المعدودة في البسائط، وسالبة مطلقة عامة وهي مفهوم اللادوام، أعني قولنا: لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف بالإطلاق العام، وإن كانت سالبة كقولنا : بالضرورة لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لا دائما، فمن سالبة وقتية مطلقة، وموجبة مطلقة عامة) وهي مفهوم اللادوام ، وهي قولنا كل قمر منخسف بالإطلاق العام. وهي أخص من الوجوديتين؛ لأنه متى صدقت الضرورة بحسب وقت معين مع اللادوام بحسب الذات صدق الإطلاق العام (۱) مع اللادوام واللاضرورة بحسب الذات(٢) من غير عكس، ومن الخاصتين من وجه لصدق الجميع في مادة الضرورة الوصفية مع اللادوام الذاتي، إذا كان الوصف ضروريا للذات بحسب وقت ما، كقولنا: كل منخسف مظلم مادام منخسفا لا دائما (۳)، وصدقهما بدون الوقتية إذا لم يكن الوصف ضروريا لذات الموضوع في وقت ما كقولنا كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع وبالعكس حيث لا يصدق الضرورة ولا الدوام بحسب الوصف كقولنا كل قمر منخسف وقـت حيلولة الأرض بينـه وبـين الشمس لا دائما؛ إذ يمتنع أن يصدق أن الانخساف دائم ما دام القمر قمرا. وذهب بعضهم إلى أن المشروطة الخاصة أخص مطلقا من الوقتية؛ لامتناع صدق المشروطة الخاصة بدونها؛ لأنه متى صدقت الضرورة بشرط الوصف أو ما دام الوصف لا دائما صدقت الضرورة بحسب الوقت المعين، وهو وقت وجود الوصف لا دائما، فيصدق في قولنا : كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة في وقت الكتابة، ولا يخفى فساده، ومنشأه عدم الفرق بين الضرورة بشرط الوصف وما دام الوصف وقد حققناه، فلا تغفل عنه. نعم إذا فسر المشروطة الخاصة بالضرورة ما دام الوصف فيكون المشروطة الخاصة أخص من من الوقتية مطلقا، لأنه متى تحققت الضرورة في جميع أوقات الوصف -وجميع أوقات الوصف بعض أوقات الذات تحققت الضرورة في بعض أوقات الذات من غير عكس كلي. ومن العامتين أيضا من وجه؛ لتصادقها في مادة المشروطة الخاصة، وصدقهما بدونها في مادة الضرورة؛ لكذب اللادوام حينئذ، وبالعكس حيث لا دوام بحسب الوصف كالانخساف للقمر، ومباينة للدائمتين، وأخص من المطلقة والممكنة العامتين، وذلك ظاهر.
The fifth (temporal). It is the temporal proposition in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or its negation from it, i.e., from the subject, at a specific time among the times of the subject's existence, qualified by the restriction of non-permanence with respect to essence. And the temporal, if it is affirmative, such as our saying: 'Necessarily every moon is eclipsed at the time of the Earth's interposition between it and the sun, not always,' then it consists of an affirmative absolute temporal proposition, which is the first part, the simple one not counted among the simple propositions, and a universal absolute negative, which is the concept of non-permanence, i.e., our saying: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed with absolute universality.' And if it is negative, such as our saying: 'Necessarily nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always,' then it consists of a negative absolute temporal proposition and a universal absolute affirmative, which is the concept of non-permanence, and it is our saying: 'Every moon is eclipsed with absolute universality.' And it is more specific than the two existential propositions; because whenever the necessity according to a specific time together with non-permanence according to essence is true, the absolute universality is true with non-permanence and non-necessity according to essence, without reciprocity. And it is related to the two special propositions by mutual partial overlap (min wajh), because all are true in the matter of descriptive necessity with essential non-permanence, when the description is necessary for the essence according to some time, such as our saying: 'Every eclipsed thing is dark as long as it is eclipsed, not always'; and they are true without the temporal when the description is not necessary for the essence of the subject at any time, such as our saying: 'Every writer moves his fingers', and conversely where neither necessity nor permanence according to the description is true, such as our saying: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of the Earth's interposition between it and the sun, not always'; for it is impossible that it be true that the eclipse is permanent as long as the moon is a moon. And some have held that the special conditional proposition is absolutely more specific than the temporal; because it is impossible for the special conditional to be true without it; because whenever the necessity with the condition of the description or as long as the description is true, not always, then the necessity according to the specific time is true, which is the time of the existence of the description, not always. So it is true in our saying: 'Every writer necessarily moves his fingers at the time of writing.' And its corruption is not hidden, and its origin is the failure to distinguish between the necessity with the condition of the description and the necessity as long as the description, and we have verified it, so do not neglect it. Yes, if the special conditional is interpreted as the necessity as long as the description, then the special conditional is absolutely more specific than the temporal, because whenever the necessity is realized in all times of the description—and all times of the description are some times of the essence—the necessity is realized in some times of the essence, without universal reciprocity. And it is also related to the two universal propositions by mutual partial overlap, because they are true simultaneously in the matter of the special conditional, and they are true without it in the matter of necessity, because then the non-permanence is false, and conversely where there is no permanence according to the description, like the eclipse for the moon. And it is disjoint from the two permanent propositions, and more specific than the absolute and the universal possible, and that is apparent.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines the temporal proposition (al-waqtiyya), the fifth type of quantified proposition. The author begins by stating its definition: it judges the necessity or impossibility of the predicate for the subject at a specific time of the subject's existence, with the restriction of non-permanence (al-ladawam) with respect to essence. This means the proposition does not claim the predicate holds for all times. The author then explains its composition: an affirmative temporal proposition consists of an affirmative absolute temporal (a simple proposition asserting the predicate at the specific time) combined with a universal absolute negative expressing non-permanence. Similarly, a negative temporal is composed of a negative absolute temporal and a universal absolute affirmative. The author uses examples from astronomy: the moon's eclipse at opposition (for affirmative) and at quadrature (for negative), illustrating the temporal restriction. Next, the author compares the temporal proposition to other types. It is more specific (akhaṣṣ) than the two existential propositions (al-wujūdiyyatayn) because when temporal necessity holds, the absolute universal (i.e., the existence of the predicate at some time) also holds, but not vice versa. It has a reciprocal partial overlap (min wajh) with the two special propositions (al-khāṣṣatayn, i.e., the conditioned and the permanent-conditioned). They agree in cases of descriptive necessity with essential non-permanence, e.g., 'Every eclipsed thing is dark as long as it is eclipsed.' They diverge when the description is not essential. The author then addresses an objection: some claim the special conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa) is absolutely more specific than the temporal. He refutes this by distinguishing two interpretations of the special conditional: 'with the condition of the description' and 'as long as the description.' The former does not yield temporal necessity in all cases (e.g., the writer example). Only if interpreted as 'as long as the description' does it become more specific. The author insists this distinction is crucial. Finally, he notes the temporal also has a partial overlap with the two universal propositions, is disjoint from the two permanent propositions, and is more specific than the absolute and universal possible. This exhaustive comparison shows the precise logical relationships among proposition types.

Key Takeaway
The temporal proposition's relations to other types are nuanced; careful interpretation of the special conditional is necessary to avoid false claims of absolute specificity.

The Distributed Proposition: Definition and Composition
Author defines the distributed proposition and its composition for affirmative and negative forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a distributed proposition and break it into its constituent affirmative and negative components.

Core Idea
Distributed propositions assert necessity at an unspecified time, composed of a simple absolute and a universal for non-permanence.

This lesson explains the sixth type of modal proposition: the distributed proposition. It is similar to the temporal proposition but the time is unspecified. Understanding its composition is key to distinguishing it from other modal propositions and applying it in logical arguments, particularly in kalam and fiqh where indefinite temporal necessity is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Read the first part of the passage: the distributed proposition judges the necessity of the predicate for the subject at an unspecified time, qualified by non-permanence.
Note: 'unspecified' means specification is not considered, not that non-specification is asserted.
2
Contrast with temporal
Compare the distributed proposition to the temporal proposition. Temporal specifies a time (e.g., 'at a particular time'), whereas distributed does not specify any time.
This difference is crucial for determining which proposition type is used.
3
Decompose affirmative distributed
From the passage, an affirmative distributed such as 'Necessarily every human is breathing at some time' comprises an affirmative distributed absolute (a simple proposition affirming the predicate at some time) and a universal absolute negative (the concept of non-permanence, i.e., 'Nothing of the human is breathing with absolute universality').
The universal absolute negative captures the 'not always' part.
4
Decompose negative distributed
A negative distributed such as 'Necessarily nothing of the human is breathing at some time' comprises a negative distributed absolute (a simple proposition denying the predicate at some time) and a universal absolute affirmative (the concept of non-permanence, i.e., 'Every human is breathing with absolute universality').
Note the symmetry: the universal component is the opposite of the simple component's mode.
5
Apply to an original example
Construct an original distributed proposition about a kalam concept, such as 'Every body is changeable at some time.' Identify its components: affirmative distributed absolute ('Every body is changeable at some time') and universal absolute negative ('No body is changeable with absolute universality').
Practice ensures you can recognize distributed propositions in Islamic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة: القضية المنتشرة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو بضرورة (سلبه عنه) أي: عن الموضوع (في وقت غير معين من أوقات وجود الموضوع) بمعنى أنه لا يعتبر التعيين لا بمعنى أنه يعتبر عدم التعيين؛ لاستحالته، مقيدا باللادوام بحسب الذات وهي إن كانت موجبة كقولنا بالضرورة كل إنسان متنفس في وقت ما لا دائما فمن موجبة منتشرة (مطلقة وهي بسيطة غير معدودة في البسائط، وسالبة مطلقة عامة وهي مفهوم اللادوام ، وهي قولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس بالإطلاق العام، وإن كانت سالبة كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس في وقت ما لا دائما فمن سالبة منتشرة مطلقة وموجبة مطلقة عامة) التي هي مفهوم اللادوام ، وهي قولنا كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق العام.
The sixth: the distributed proposition. It is the proposition in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or the necessity of its negation from it, i.e., from the subject, at an unspecified time among the times of the subject's existence, meaning that specification is not considered, not that non-specification is considered—because that is impossible—qualified by non-permanence with respect to essence. And it, if it is affirmative, such as our saying: 'Necessarily every human is breathing at some time, not always,' then it consists of an affirmative distributed absolute (which is a simple proposition not counted among the simple propositions) and a universal absolute negative, which is the concept of non-permanence, and it is our saying: 'Nothing of the human is breathing with absolute universality.' And if it is negative, such as our saying: 'Necessarily nothing of the human is breathing at some time, not always,' then it consists of a negative distributed absolute and a universal absolute affirmative, which is the concept of non-permanence, and it is our saying: 'Every human is breathing with absolute universality.'
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage defines the distributed proposition (al-muntashira), the sixth type. It is similar to the temporal but instead of a specific time, it asserts necessity at an unspecified time. The author clarifies that 'unspecified' means we do not consider specification, not that we consider non-specification (which would be contradictory). The composition mirrors the temporal: an affirmative distributed consists of an affirmative distributed absolute (a simple proposition asserting the predicate at some time) and a universal absolute negative for non-permanence. The negative distributed consists of a negative distributed absolute and a universal absolute affirmative. The example given is 'Every human is breathing at some time, not always' (affirmative) and 'Nothing of the human is breathing at some time, not always' (negative). This proposition type is used when necessity holds at some indefinite time, without specifying which time, as in the case of breathing for humans.

The Widespread Proposition (al-Muntashirah)
Defines the widespread modal proposition with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a widespread (muntashirah) modal proposition and distinguish it from temporal and permanent propositions.

Core Idea
The widespread proposition judges necessity at an unspecified time, not always.

The sixth modal proposition, al-muntashirah, deals with judgments of necessity that are true at some unspecified time during the subject's existence. Unlike temporal propositions that specify a time, this type qualifies necessity with non-permanence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing modal categories in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition
Read the definition: 'judgment of necessity of the predicate's affirmation or negation at an unspecified time qualified by non-permanence.' Identify key elements: necessity, unspecified time, non-permanence.
2
Contrast with temporal propositions
Note that in temporal propositions (waqtiyyah), time is specified. In widespread, time is absent of specification, not that non-specification is actively considered.
The author clarifies: 'specification is not considered, not that non-specification is considered.'
3
Examine affirmative and negative forms
For affirmative: e.g., 'By necessity, every human is breathing at some time, not always.' This yields two subordinate propositions: absolute widespread affirmative (simple) and absolute universal negative (concept of non-permanence). For negative: e.g., 'By necessity, no human is breathing at some time, not always.'
Check the interplay between affirmative/negative and absolute/universal components.
4
Apply the tool
Construct your own widespread proposition using an Islamic example, such as 'By necessity, every Muslim prays at some time, not always.' Verify it meets the condition: necessity, unspecified time, non-permanence.
Ensure the predicate is not always true of the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة: القضية المنتشرة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو بضرورة (سلبه عنه) أي: عن الموضوع (في وقت غير معين من أوقات وجود الموضوع) بمعنى أنه لا يعتبر التعيين لا بمعنى أنه يعتبر عدم التعيين؛ لاستحالته، مقيدا باللادوام بحسب الذات وهي إن كانت موجبة كقولنا بالضرورة كل إنسان متنفس في وقت ما لا دائما فمن موجبة منتشرة (مطلقة وهي بسيطة غير معدودة في البسائط، وسالبة مطلقة عامة وهي مفهوم اللادوام ، وهي قولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس بالإطلاق العام، وإن كانت سالبة كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس في وقت ما لا دائما فمن سالبة منتشرة مطلقة وموجبة مطلقة عامة) التي هي مفهوم اللادوام ، وهي قولنا كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق العام. البواقي. وهي أعم من الوقتية حيث لم يعتبر فيها تعيين الوقت وهي كالوقتية في النسبة إلى ...  
والسابعة الممكنة الخاصة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بارتفاع الضرورة المطلقة أي الذاتية (عن جانبي الوجود والعدم جميعا أي ثبوت الحكم ولا ثبوته، (وهي -سواء كانت موجبة كقولنا: بالإمكان الخاص كل إنسان كاتب) بمعنى أن ثبوت الكتابة للإنسان وسلب الكتابة عنه ليس بضروريين أو سالبة كقولنا : بالإمكان الخاص لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب فمن ممكنتين عامتين موجبة وسالبة ولا فرق بين الموجبة والسالبة في المعنى؛ لأن كليهما عبارة عن سلب الضرورة عن الطرفين، بل هو في اللفظ فقط؛ لأن في الموجبة الإيجاب صريحي والسلب ضمني، وفي السالبة بالعكس. وهي أعم مطلقا من سائر المركبات، وأخص من الممكنة العامة، وهو ظاهر، وأعم من وجه من الدائمة والعامتين والمطلقة العامة؛ لصدق الجميع في مادة الوجودية اللاضرورية إن كان ثبوت المحمول للموضوع دائما، وصدق الممكنة الخاصة بدونها حيث لا يقع الممكنة بالفعل، وبالعكس في مادة الضرورة الذاتية ومباينة للضرورية، وهو ظاهر. واعلم أن المعتبر في نسب القضايا صدقها في نفسها، لا صدق بعضها على بعض، فلا يرد ما أورد من أن القضايا لا يصح صدق بعضها على بعض، فما معنى اعتبار النسب فيها؟ هذا إذا اعتبرت النسب بحسب مواد الجهات أما إذا اعتُبرت بحسب المفهومات فيجري فيها التصادق، تأمل، ثم تأمل، والله الهادي إلى سبيل الرشاد.
The sixth: the widespread proposition, which is the one in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or of the necessity of its negation from it, i.e., from the subject, at an unspecified time from the times of the subject's existence, meaning that specification is not considered, not that non-specification is considered—because of its impossibility—qualified by non-permanence with respect to the essence. If it is affirmative, like our saying: 'By necessity, every human is breathing at some time, not always,' then it is from the absolute widespread affirmative, which is simple and not counted among the simples, and the absolute universal negative, which is the concept of non-permanence, and it is our saying: 'No human is breathing with absolute universality.' And if it is negative, like our saying: 'By necessity, no human is breathing at some time, not always,' then it is from the absolute widespread negative and the absolute universal affirmative, which is the concept of non-permanence, and it is our saying: 'Every human is breathing with absolute universality.' The remaining. It is more general than the temporal in that specification of time is not considered, and it is like the temporal in relation to... And the seventh: the special possible proposition, which is the one in which judgment is made of the removal of absolute necessity, i.e., essential necessity, from both sides of existence and non-existence together, i.e., the affirmation of the judgment and its non-affirmation. It—whether affirmative like our saying: 'By special possibility, every human is a writer,' meaning that the affirmation of writing for a human and its negation are not necessary, or negative like our saying: 'By special possibility, no human is a writer'—then from two general possible propositions, affirmative and negative. And there is no difference between the affirmative and the negative in meaning, because both are expressions of the negation of necessity from both sides. Rather, it is only in wording, because in the affirmative the affirmation is explicit and the negation implicit, and in the negative the opposite. It is absolutely more general than all compound propositions, and more specific than the general possible, which is apparent, and more general in aspect than the permanent, the two general, and the absolute general, because all are true in the material of existential non-necessary if the predicate's affirmation for the subject is permanent, and the special possible is true without it where the possible does not occur actually, and the opposite in the material of essential necessity, and it is divergent from the necessary, which is apparent. And know that what is considered in the relations of propositions is their truth in themselves, not one being true of another. So what is objected—that propositions cannot be truly predicated of each other, so what is the meaning of considering relations among them?—does not arise. This is if relations are considered according to the materials of modalities; but if they are considered according to concepts, then truth relations apply. Reflect, then reflect. And Allah guides to the path of rectitude.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth and seventh types of modal propositions, following a classification likely from earlier in the text. The sixth is al-Muntashira (the widespread proposition). It judges a predicate as necessarily true or false of a subject at some unspecified time during the subject's existence. The key is that 'time is not specified,' meaning we do not require a specific time, but we do not require the absence of specification either (since that would be impossible). It is qualified by non-permanence with respect to the essence. The author then breaks it into affirmative and negative forms. For example, 'By necessity, every human is breathing at some time, not always' is an affirmative widespread proposition. This yields two subordinate propositions: an absolute widespread affirmative (simple) and an absolute universal negative (concept of non-permanence). For the negative form, e.g., 'By necessity, no human is breathing at some time, not always,' it yields an absolute widespread negative and an absolute universal affirmative. The author notes that the widespread proposition is more general than the temporal proposition because it does not specify a time. The seventh proposition is al-Mumkinah al-Khassah (the special possible). It judges that absolute necessity is removed from both sides—that is, neither the affirmation nor the negation of the predicate is necessary. For example, 'By special possibility, every human is a writer' means that it is not necessary for a human to be a writer or not to be a writer. The author explains that affirmative and negative versions are equivalent in meaning, differing only in wording (the affirmative explicitly asserts and implicitly negates, and vice versa). Then the author compares this proposition's relations to others: it is absolutely more general than all compound propositions, more specific than the general possible, more general in aspect than the permanent, the two general, and the absolute general; it is divergent from the necessary. Finally, the author gives an important methodological note: when we compare propositions (e.g., in terms of generality), we consider their truth in themselves, not as predicates applied to each other. If we consider them conceptually (by their meanings), then logical relations of truth (like subalternation) hold. This resolves a potential confusion. The teacher encourages reflection using the phrase 'ta'ammal, thumma ta'ammal' (reflect, then reflect).

Key Takeaway
The widespread modal proposition restricts necessity to an unspecified time, making it more general than temporal but more specific than absolute.

The Special Possible (al-Mumkinah al-Khassah)
Defines the special possible modal proposition and its logical relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a special possible (mumkinah khassah) proposition and explain its logical relations to other modal types.

Core Idea
The special possible proposition negates necessity from both affirmation and negation, expressing genuine contingency.

The seventh modal proposition, al-mumkinah al-khassah, denotes the removal of absolute necessity from both sides—neither the predicate's affirmation nor its negation is necessary. This contrasts with the general possible, which only negates necessity on one side. Understanding this distinction is key to analyzing contingency in Islamic theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition
Read: 'judgment of the removal of absolute necessity from both sides of existence and non-existence.' This means the proposition asserts that neither the predicate nor its negation is necessary.
2
Understand affirmative-negative equivalence
Note that the author states there is no difference in meaning between affirmative and negative forms; e.g., 'By special possibility, every human is a writer' and 'By special possibility, no human is a writer' both mean it is not necessary for a human to be or not be a writer. The difference is only in wording.
In the affirmative, the affirmation is explicit and negation implicit; in the negative, the opposite.
3
Compare with other modal types
The author compares: it is absolutely more general than compound propositions, more specific than the general possible, more general in aspect than the permanent and the two general and absolute general (under certain conditions), and divergent from the necessary. Trace these comparisons using examples.
Use the material of existential non-necessary and essential necessity to verify.
4
Apply the tool
Construct your own special possible proposition using an Islamic example, e.g., 'By special possibility, every student fails the exam' (meaning failure is not necessary). Explain why it is special possible and how it relates to the general possible.
Also check the relation: Is it more general than the permanent? Yes, if the permanent is not true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة: القضية المنتشرة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو بضرورة (سلبه عنه) أي: عن الموضوع (في وقت غير معين من أوقات وجود الموضوع) بمعنى أنه لا يعتبر التعيين لا بمعنى أنه يعتبر عدم التعيين؛ لاستحالته، مقيدا باللادوام بحسب الذات وهي إن كانت موجبة كقولنا بالضرورة كل إنسان متنفس في وقت ما لا دائما فمن موجبة منتشرة (مطلقة وهي بسيطة غير معدودة في البسائط، وسالبة مطلقة عامة وهي مفهوم اللادوام ، وهي قولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس بالإطلاق العام، وإن كانت سالبة كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس في وقت ما لا دائما فمن سالبة منتشرة مطلقة وموجبة مطلقة عامة) التي هي مفهوم اللادوام ، وهي قولنا كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق العام. البواقي. وهي أعم من الوقتية حيث لم يعتبر فيها تعيين الوقت وهي كالوقتية في النسبة إلى ...  
والسابعة الممكنة الخاصة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بارتفاع الضرورة المطلقة أي الذاتية (عن جانبي الوجود والعدم جميعا أي ثبوت الحكم ولا ثبوته، (وهي -سواء كانت موجبة كقولنا: بالإمكان الخاص كل إنسان كاتب) بمعنى أن ثبوت الكتابة للإنسان وسلب الكتابة عنه ليس بضروريين أو سالبة كقولنا : بالإمكان الخاص لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب فمن ممكنتين عامتين موجبة وسالبة ولا فرق بين الموجبة والسالبة في المعنى؛ لأن كليهما عبارة عن سلب الضرورة عن الطرفين، بل هو في اللفظ فقط؛ لأن في الموجبة الإيجاب صريحي والسلب ضمني، وفي السالبة بالعكس. وهي أعم مطلقا من سائر المركبات، وأخص من الممكنة العامة، وهو ظاهر، وأعم من وجه من الدائمة والعامتين والمطلقة العامة؛ لصدق الجميع في مادة الوجودية اللاضرورية إن كان ثبوت المحمول للموضوع دائما، وصدق الممكنة الخاصة بدونها حيث لا يقع الممكنة بالفعل، وبالعكس في مادة الضرورة الذاتية ومباينة للضرورية، وهو ظاهر. واعلم أن المعتبر في نسب القضايا صدقها في نفسها، لا صدق بعضها على بعض، فلا يرد ما أورد من أن القضايا لا يصح صدق بعضها على بعض، فما معنى اعتبار النسب فيها؟ هذا إذا اعتبرت النسب بحسب مواد الجهات أما إذا اعتُبرت بحسب المفهومات فيجري فيها التصادق، تأمل، ثم تأمل، والله الهادي إلى سبيل الرشاد.
The sixth: the widespread proposition, which is the one in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or of the necessity of its negation from it, i.e., from the subject, at an unspecified time from the times of the subject's existence, meaning that specification is not considered, not that non-specification is considered—because of its impossibility—qualified by non-permanence with respect to the essence. If it is affirmative, like our saying: 'By necessity, every human is breathing at some time, not always,' then it is from the absolute widespread affirmative, which is simple and not counted among the simples, and the absolute universal negative, which is the concept of non-permanence, and it is our saying: 'No human is breathing with absolute universality.' And if it is negative, like our saying: 'By necessity, no human is breathing at some time, not always,' then it is from the absolute widespread negative and the absolute universal affirmative, which is the concept of non-permanence, and it is our saying: 'Every human is breathing with absolute universality.' The remaining. It is more general than the temporal in that specification of time is not considered, and it is like the temporal in relation to... And the seventh: the special possible proposition, which is the one in which judgment is made of the removal of absolute necessity, i.e., essential necessity, from both sides of existence and non-existence together, i.e., the affirmation of the judgment and its non-affirmation. It—whether affirmative like our saying: 'By special possibility, every human is a writer,' meaning that the affirmation of writing for a human and its negation are not necessary, or negative like our saying: 'By special possibility, no human is a writer'—then from two general possible propositions, affirmative and negative. And there is no difference between the affirmative and the negative in meaning, because both are expressions of the negation of necessity from both sides. Rather, it is only in wording, because in the affirmative the affirmation is explicit and the negation implicit, and in the negative the opposite. It is absolutely more general than all compound propositions, and more specific than the general possible, which is apparent, and more general in aspect than the permanent, the two general, and the absolute general, because all are true in the material of existential non-necessary if the predicate's affirmation for the subject is permanent, and the special possible is true without it where the possible does not occur actually, and the opposite in the material of essential necessity, and it is divergent from the necessary, which is apparent. And know that what is considered in the relations of propositions is their truth in themselves, not one being true of another. So what is objected—that propositions cannot be truly predicated of each other, so what is the meaning of considering relations among them?—does not arise. This is if relations are considered according to the materials of modalities; but if they are considered according to concepts, then truth relations apply. Reflect, then reflect. And Allah guides to the path of rectitude.

The Parts of a Conditional: Muqaddam and Tālin
Author defines muqaddam and tālin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the muqaddam and tālin in any conditional proposition from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Every conditional has two parts: the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (tālin).

This lesson focuses on the fundamental parts of a conditional proposition as defined by the author. Understanding these parts is crucial for analyzing conditional arguments used in Islamic legal reasoning and theology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the conditional particle
Find words like 'if' (in, law) or 'either' (immā) that signal a conditional proposition.
In Arabic, the particle often precedes the muqaddam.
2
Identify the muqaddam (antecedent)
The statement immediately following the conditional particle is the muqaddam. It usually comes first.
The passage mentions it may be delayed in wording, as in 'The day exists if the sun is rising' where 'the sun is rising' is muqaddam.
3
Identify the tālin (consequent)
The remaining part of the conditional is the tālin, which follows the muqaddam in logical order.
4
Apply to examples
Practice by parsing conditional statements from Islamic texts, e.g., 'If the water is pure, then wudu is valid'.
Muqaddam: 'the water is pure'; tālin: 'wudu is valid'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الشرطية الجزء الأول منها يسمى مقدما؛ لتقدمه غالبا، وقيل: لتقدمه ولو كان حكما، كما في صورة تأخر الشرط لفظا كقولنا النهار موجود إن كانت الشمس طالعة و) الجزء الثاني منها يسمى (تاليا؛ لأنه يتلو أي: يتبع المقدم غالبا. (وهي) أي: القضية الشرطية متصلة لزومية إن كان صدق التالي) أي: إن كان الحكم بصدق التالي فيها على تقدير صدق المقدم لزوما) أي: لعلاقة بينهما توجب ذلك كالعلية والمعلولية والتضايف، واتفاقية إن كان ذلك) أي: صدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم (بمجرد الاتفاق أي بمجرد توافق الطرفين على الصدق من ملاحظة علاقة تقتضي ذلك، وبهذا ينحل ما أورد ههنا تأمل كقولنا: إن كان الإنسان ناطقا فالحمار ناهق فإن العلاقة بينهما غير ملحوظة ومنظور إليها في نظر الحاكم. (ومنفصلة، إما حقيقية إن حكم فيها بالتنافي بين جزئيها في الصدق والكذب معا) أي: بامتناع اجتماعهما في الصدق والكذب معا، أي: لا يصدقان ولا يكذبان على ما هو حقيقة الانفصال، كقولنا : هذا العدد إما الزوج أو فرد بمعنى أن العدد الواحد لا يجوز أن يكون زوجا وفردا معا، ولا يجوز أن ينتفي كونه زوجا وفردا معا. غير (وإما مانعة الجمع إن حكم فيها بالتنافي بين جزئيها في الصدق فقط) أي: من غير أن يتنافيا في الكذب بل يمكن اجتماعهما في الكذب كقولنا: هذا الشيء إما شجر أو حجر) فإن الشجر والحجر لا يجتمعان فلا يجوز أن يكون الشيء الواحد شجرا وحجرا معا. (وإما مانعة الخلو إن حكم فيها به أي بالتنافي (بينهما) أي: بين جزئيها (في الكذب فقط) أي من غير التنافي في الصدق، فيجوز اجتماعهما في الوجود، (كقولنا: إما أن يكون زيد في البحر أو لا يغرق) فإن الكون في البحر وعدم الغرق قد يجتمعان وجودا، لكنهما لا يجتمعان عدما؛ لاستحالة انتفاء الكون في البحر وانتفاء عدم الغرق.
Section on the conditional proposition: its first part is called the muqaddam (antecedent) because of its precedence usually, and it is said: because of its precedence even if it is a judgement, as in the case of the condition being delayed in wording like our saying 'The day exists if the sun is rising'. And the second part is called the tālin (consequent) because it follows, i.e., it follows the muqaddam usually. And it (i.e., the conditional proposition) is a connected (muttaṣila) [and] implicative (lāzimiyya) if the truth of the consequent is judged to be necessary upon the supposition of the truth of the antecedent due to a relationship that necessitates that, like causality and effect and correlation; and accidental (ittifāqiyya) if that is merely by coincidence, i.e., merely by the agreement of the two sides in truth without observing a relationship that necessitates that. And by this is resolved what is presented here as a consideration like our saying 'If man is rational then the donkey is braying' for the relationship between them is not observed and considered in the view of the judge. And it is disjunctive (munfaṣila); either genuine (ḥaqīqiyya) if it is judged to be mutually exclusive between its two parts in both truth and falsehood together, i.e., impossible for them to be both true and both false together, like our saying 'This number is either even or odd' meaning that a single number cannot be both even and odd together, nor can it be devoid of being even and odd together. Or restrictive of union (māni'at al-jam') if it is judged to be mutually exclusive between its two parts in truth only, i.e., without them being mutually exclusive in falsehood, rather it is possible for them to be together in falsehood, like our saying 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' for tree and stone do not combine, so it is not permissible for a single thing to be a tree and a stone together. Or restrictive of emptiness (māni'at al-khuluw) if it is judged to be mutually exclusive between them in falsehood only, i.e., without mutual exclusivity in truth, so it is permissible for them to coexist in existence, like our saying 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown' for being in the sea and not drowning may coexist in existence, but they cannot be both absent because it is impossible for the absence of being in the sea and the absence of not drowning.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional proposition and its parts, then classifies it into connected and disjunctive, each with subtypes. First, it states that the first part is called the muqaddam (antecedent) because it usually precedes, and the second part the tālin (consequent) because it follows. Then it divides the connected conditional into two: implicative (lāzimiyya), where the truth of the consequent necessarily follows from the truth of the antecedent due to a real relationship like causality, and accidental (ittifāqiyya), where the truth of the consequent merely coincides with the truth of the antecedent without any necessary relationship. The author gives the example 'If man is rational, the donkey is braying' to illustrate accidental connectedness: the relationship is not considered by the judge. Next, the disjunctive conditional is divided into three types. Genuine (ḥaqīqiyya) excludes both truth and falsehood together; example: 'This number is either even or odd' — a number cannot be both and cannot be neither. Restrictive of union (māni'at al-jam') excludes truth only; example: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' — it cannot be both, but could be neither (e.g., an animal). Restrictive of emptiness (māni'at al-khuluw) excludes falsehood only; example: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown' — both can be true (if he is safe on land), but they cannot both be false (it is impossible for him to be not in the sea and also to drown). This classification is essential for understanding the logical behaviour of conditionals in syllogisms.

Key Terms
مقدم
تالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The muqaddam is the antecedent that usually precedes, and the tālin is the consequent that follows.

Connected Conditionals: Implicative vs Accidental
Author classifies muttasila into lāzimiyya and ittifāqiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between implicative (lāzimiyya) and accidental (ittifāqiyya) connected conditionals using the criterion of a real relationship.

Core Idea
A connected conditional can be either necessary or coincidental – the difference is whether the connection is real or accidental.

The author divides connected conditionals into two types based on whether the truth of the consequent necessarily follows from the antecedent due to a real relationship, or merely coincides. This distinction is vital for evaluating the strength of conditional arguments in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional as muttasila (connected)
Check that the conditional uses the particle 'in' (if) and connects two statements.
Connected conditionals are introduced in the passage as 'muttaṣila'.
2
Check for a necessary relationship
Ask: Is the truth of the consequent guaranteed by the truth of the antecedent due to a real connection like causality or correlation? If yes, it is lāzimiyya.
Examples: 'If the sun rises, it is day' – a real causal relationship.
3
Check for mere coincidence
If the truth of the consequent merely coincides with the antecedent without a necessary relationship, it is ittifāqiyya.
The example given: 'If man is rational, the donkey is braying' – no real connection.
4
Apply the classification
Practice by evaluating conditional statements: 'If the world is created, it has a creator' is lāzimiyya; 'If it rains, the grass is wet' could be either depending on context.
The passage emphasizes that the relationship must be considered from the judge's perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الشرطية الجزء الأول منها يسمى مقدما؛ لتقدمه غالبا، وقيل: لتقدمه ولو كان حكما، كما في صورة تأخر الشرط لفظا كقولنا النهار موجود إن كانت الشمس طالعة و) الجزء الثاني منها يسمى (تاليا؛ لأنه يتلو أي: يتبع المقدم غالبا. (وهي) أي: القضية الشرطية متصلة لزومية إن كان صدق التالي) أي: إن كان الحكم بصدق التالي فيها على تقدير صدق المقدم لزوما) أي: لعلاقة بينهما توجب ذلك كالعلية والمعلولية والتضايف، واتفاقية إن كان ذلك) أي: صدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم (بمجرد الاتفاق أي بمجرد توافق الطرفين على الصدق من ملاحظة علاقة تقتضي ذلك، وبهذا ينحل ما أورد ههنا تأمل كقولنا: إن كان الإنسان ناطقا فالحمار ناهق فإن العلاقة بينهما غير ملحوظة ومنظور إليها في نظر الحاكم. (ومنفصلة، إما حقيقية إن حكم فيها بالتنافي بين جزئيها في الصدق والكذب معا) أي: بامتناع اجتماعهما في الصدق والكذب معا، أي: لا يصدقان ولا يكذبان على ما هو حقيقة الانفصال، كقولنا : هذا العدد إما الزوج أو فرد بمعنى أن العدد الواحد لا يجوز أن يكون زوجا وفردا معا، ولا يجوز أن ينتفي كونه زوجا وفردا معا. غير (وإما مانعة الجمع إن حكم فيها بالتنافي بين جزئيها في الصدق فقط) أي: من غير أن يتنافيا في الكذب بل يمكن اجتماعهما في الكذب كقولنا: هذا الشيء إما شجر أو حجر) فإن الشجر والحجر لا يجتمعان فلا يجوز أن يكون الشيء الواحد شجرا وحجرا معا. (وإما مانعة الخلو إن حكم فيها به أي بالتنافي (بينهما) أي: بين جزئيها (في الكذب فقط) أي من غير التنافي في الصدق، فيجوز اجتماعهما في الوجود، (كقولنا: إما أن يكون زيد في البحر أو لا يغرق) فإن الكون في البحر وعدم الغرق قد يجتمعان وجودا، لكنهما لا يجتمعان عدما؛ لاستحالة انتفاء الكون في البحر وانتفاء عدم الغرق.
Section on the conditional proposition: its first part is called the muqaddam (antecedent) because of its precedence usually, and it is said: because of its precedence even if it is a judgement, as in the case of the condition being delayed in wording like our saying 'The day exists if the sun is rising'. And the second part is called the tālin (consequent) because it follows, i.e., it follows the muqaddam usually. And it (i.e., the conditional proposition) is a connected (muttaṣila) [and] implicative (lāzimiyya) if the truth of the consequent is judged to be necessary upon the supposition of the truth of the antecedent due to a relationship that necessitates that, like causality and effect and correlation; and accidental (ittifāqiyya) if that is merely by coincidence, i.e., merely by the agreement of the two sides in truth without observing a relationship that necessitates that. And by this is resolved what is presented here as a consideration like our saying 'If man is rational then the donkey is braying' for the relationship between them is not observed and considered in the view of the judge. And it is disjunctive (munfaṣila); either genuine (ḥaqīqiyya) if it is judged to be mutually exclusive between its two parts in both truth and falsehood together, i.e., impossible for them to be both true and both false together, like our saying 'This number is either even or odd' meaning that a single number cannot be both even and odd together, nor can it be devoid of being even and odd together. Or restrictive of union (māni'at al-jam') if it is judged to be mutually exclusive between its two parts in truth only, i.e., without them being mutually exclusive in falsehood, rather it is possible for them to be together in falsehood, like our saying 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' for tree and stone do not combine, so it is not permissible for a single thing to be a tree and a stone together. Or restrictive of emptiness (māni'at al-khuluw) if it is judged to be mutually exclusive between them in falsehood only, i.e., without mutual exclusivity in truth, so it is permissible for them to coexist in existence, like our saying 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown' for being in the sea and not drowning may coexist in existence, but they cannot be both absent because it is impossible for the absence of being in the sea and the absence of not drowning.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional proposition and its parts, then classifies it into connected and disjunctive, each with subtypes. First, it states that the first part is called the muqaddam (antecedent) because it usually precedes, and the second part the tālin (consequent) because it follows. Then it divides the connected conditional into two: implicative (lāzimiyya), where the truth of the consequent necessarily follows from the truth of the antecedent due to a real relationship like causality, and accidental (ittifāqiyya), where the truth of the consequent merely coincides with the truth of the antecedent without any necessary relationship. The author gives the example 'If man is rational, the donkey is braying' to illustrate accidental connectedness: the relationship is not considered by the judge. Next, the disjunctive conditional is divided into three types. Genuine (ḥaqīqiyya) excludes both truth and falsehood together; example: 'This number is either even or odd' — a number cannot be both and cannot be neither. Restrictive of union (māni'at al-jam') excludes truth only; example: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' — it cannot be both, but could be neither (e.g., an animal). Restrictive of emptiness (māni'at al-khuluw) excludes falsehood only; example: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown' — both can be true (if he is safe on land), but they cannot both be false (it is impossible for him to be not in the sea and also to drown). This classification is essential for understanding the logical behaviour of conditionals in syllogisms.

Key Terms
متصلة
لزومية
اتفاقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of connected conditional is the example 'If man is rational, the donkey is braying'?
A) Lāzimiyya
B) Ittifāqiyya
C) Genuine disjunctive
D) Restrictive of union

Key Takeaway
Lāzimiyya conditionals rely on a real relationship; ittifāqiyya conditionals are merely coincidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Disjunctive Conditionals: Three Types
Author classifies munfasila into three subtypes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate the three types of disjunctive conditionals: genuine (ḥaqīqiyya), restrictive of union (māni'at al-jam'), and restrictive of emptiness (māni'at al-khuluw).

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals differ in how strictly they exclude the two parts.

The passage classifies disjunctive conditionals into three types based on mutual exclusivity in truth and falsehood. This classification is essential for understanding disjunctive syllogisms used in Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the disjunctive conditional
Look for the particle 'immā...immā' (either...or) indicating a disjunction.
2
Test for genuine disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya)
Check if the two parts cannot be both true and cannot be both false. If so, it is genuine.
Example: 'This number is either even or odd' – a number cannot be both, and must be one.
3
Test for restrictive of union (māni'at al-jam')
Check if the two parts cannot be both true, but can be both false. If yes, it is restrictive of union.
Example: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' – cannot be both, but could be neither (e.g., animal).
4
Test for restrictive of emptiness (māni'at al-khuluw)
Check if the two parts cannot be both false, but can be both true. If yes, it is restrictive of emptiness.
Example: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown' – both can be true (if safe on land), but cannot both be false (impossible).

Negation of Conditional Propositions
Author explains negation of each conditional type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to form the negative of any given conditional proposition (implicative connected, accidental connected, genuine disjunctive, restrictive of union, restrictive of emptiness) by identifying the judgment in the affirmative and negating it.

Core Idea
To negate a conditional, negate the specific relation it asserts — implication, agreement, or disjunction type.

In this passage, the author shows how to construct the negative for each type of conditional proposition. Just as categorical negatives negate the predicate's connection to the subject, conditional negatives negate the specific relationship (implication, agreement, or exclusion) that the affirmative asserts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Determine whether it is an implicative connected (متصل لزومي), accidental connected (متصل اتفاقي), genuine disjunctive (منفصل حقيقي), restrictive of union (مانعة الجمع), or restrictive of emptiness (مانعة الخلو).
2
Determine the affirmative judgment
For implicative: the judgment is the necessity (لزوم) of the consequent. For accidental: the agreement of the two sides in truth (توافق في الصدق). For genuine disjunctive: mutual exclusion in truth. For restrictive of union: both cannot be true simultaneously. For restrictive of emptiness: both cannot be false simultaneously.
3
Form the negative by negating that judgment
The negative asserts the opposite of the specific relation. For implicative: negate the necessity (رفع اللزوم). For accidental: negate the agreement in truth (رفع توافق الطرفين في الصدق). And so on.
4
Verify the result
Check that the negative matches the author's definition: 'the negative of each is established by negating what was judged in its affirmative.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسالبة كل واحدة من هذه القضايا) أي المتصلة اللزومية والاتفاقية والمنفصلة الحقيقية ومانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو (تثبت برفع ما حكم به في موجباتها) فإن السالبة اللزومية ما حكم فيها برفع اللزوم، والاتفاقية ما حكم فيها برفع توافق الطرفين في الصدق، وعلى هذا فقس.
ثم أشار إلى تقسيم الشرطية إلى المحصورة والمهملة والمخصوصة بحسب انقسام القضية الحملية إليها؛ لأن الأوضاع في الشرطية كالأفراد في الحملية، فقال: (اعلم أن كليـة الشرطية) أي كون الشرطية كلية (أن يكون التالي (لازما) في المتصلة اللزومية، (أو معاندا) في المنفصلة العنادية (للمقدّم) متعلق بقوله : (معاندا) أو (لازما) على طريق التنازع، وكذا الحال في قوله : ( على جميع التقادير) أي: الأوضاع (التي لا تنافي مقدمية المقدم) أي: يمكن حصول المقدم عليها، سواء كانت محالة في أنفسها كقولنا: كلما كان الفرس إنسانا كان حيوانا، فإن معناه أن لزوم حيوانية الفرس ثابت لإنسانية الفرس مع جميع الأوضاع التي يمكن اجتماعها مع إنسانية الفرس من كونه ضاحكا أو كاتبا أو ناطقا إلى غير ذلك، وهي محالة في أنفسها، أو لا كقولنا كلما كان زيد إنسانا فهو حيوان، فمعناه
And the negative of each one of these propositions – i.e., the implicative connected, the accidental connected, the genuine disjunctive, the restrictive of union, and the restrictive of emptiness – is established by negating what was judged in their affirmatives. For the implicative negative is that in which the implication is negated, the accidental negative is that in which the agreement of the two sides in truth is negated, and so on. Then he indicated the division of the conditional into the quantified (maḥṣūra), the unrestricted (muhmala), and the particular (mukhṣaṣa) according to the division of the categorical proposition into them, because the states (awḍā') in the conditional are like the individuals in the categorical. So he said: Know that the universality of the conditional – i.e., the conditional being universal – is that the consequent is necessary in the implicative connected, or contrary in the disjunctive contrary, to the antecedent, with respect to his saying 'contrary' or 'necessary' by way of mutual dependency. And likewise in his saying: 'upon all the states' i.e., the circumstances that do not negate the antecendence of the antecedent, i.e., it is possible for the antecedent to occur with them, whether those circumstances are impossible in themselves like our saying: 'Whenever a horse is a human, it is an animal' for its meaning is that the necessity of the animality of the horse is established for the humanity of the horse with all the states that can combine with the humanity of the horse, such as it being laughing, writing, speaking, etc., and those are impossible in themselves, or not, like our saying: 'Whenever Zayd is a human, he is an animal' so its meaning is...
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage first explains how to form the negative (sāliba) of each type of conditional proposition. For each affirmative type, the negative is formed by negating the specific judgement made in that affirmative. For example, the negative of an implicative connected conditional negates the implication; the negative of an accidental connected conditional negates the coincidence in truth; and similarly for the disjunctive types. The author then moves to the topic of universality in conditionals. He notes that conditionals are divided into quantified (maḥṣūra), unrestricted (muhmala), and particular (mukhṣaṣa), analogous to categorical propositions. The universality of a conditional means that the consequent holds for the antecedent under all states (awḍā') that do not negate the antecedent's role. For a connected implicative conditional, the consequent must be necessary (lāzim); for a disjunctive contrary conditional, the consequent must be contrary (mu'ānid) to the antecedent. The states include even impossible ones, as in the example 'Whenever a horse is a human, it is an animal' – here the antecedent is impossible, but the conditional is universal because the necessity holds under all hypothetical states that can be combined with the antecedent (like being laughing, writing, etc., even though those are impossible for a horse). The lesson ends abruptly on the page, but the point about universality is clear: it requires the consequent to follow under all possible antecedents, not just actual ones.

Propositions

Universality of Conditional Propositions
Author defines universality of conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conditional proposition is universal by checking if the consequent holds as necessary (for implicative) or contrary (for disjunctive) under all states that do not negate the antecedent.

Core Idea
A conditional is universal when the consequent follows under every possible state that preserves the antecedent as antecedent.

The author explains that universality in conditionals is analogous to universal quantifiers in categorical propositions. Just as a universal categorical applies to all individuals, a universal conditional applies to all states (awḍā') that do not negate the antecedent's role. He gives the example 'Whenever a horse is a human, it is an animal' to show that even impossible antecedents can yield a universal conditional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Is it an implicative connected (متصلة لزومية) or a disjunctive contrary (منفصلة عنادية)?
2
For implicative, check that the consequent is necessary (لازم)
The author states universality requires the consequent to be necessary for the antecedent under all states.
3
For disjunctive, check that the consequent is contrary (معاند)
For a disjunctive contrary conditional, universality requires the consequent to be contrary to the antecedent under all states.
4
Consider all states that do not negate the antecedent
The author says 'upon all the states (awḍā') that do not negate the antecendence of the antecedent.' This includes impossible states where the antecedent is still conceivable.
5
Test with an example: 'Whenever a horse is a human, it is an animal'
Even though the antecedent is impossible, the conditional is universal because the necessity holds under all states that can combine with the antecedent (e.g., being laughing, writing).

وسالبة كل واحدة من هذه القضايا) أي المتصلة اللزومية والاتفاقية والمنفصلة الحقيقية ومانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو (تثبت برفع ما حكم به في موجباتها) فإن السالبة اللزومية ما حكم فيها برفع اللزوم، والاتفاقية ما حكم فيها برفع توافق الطرفين في الصدق، وعلى هذا فقس.
ثم أشار إلى تقسيم الشرطية إلى المحصورة والمهملة والمخصوصة بحسب انقسام القضية الحملية إليها؛ لأن الأوضاع في الشرطية كالأفراد في الحملية، فقال: (اعلم أن كليـة الشرطية) أي كون الشرطية كلية (أن يكون التالي (لازما) في المتصلة اللزومية، (أو معاندا) في المنفصلة العنادية (للمقدّم) متعلق بقوله : (معاندا) أو (لازما) على طريق التنازع، وكذا الحال في قوله : ( على جميع التقادير) أي: الأوضاع (التي لا تنافي مقدمية المقدم) أي: يمكن حصول المقدم عليها، سواء كانت محالة في أنفسها كقولنا: كلما كان الفرس إنسانا كان حيوانا، فإن معناه أن لزوم حيوانية الفرس ثابت لإنسانية الفرس مع جميع الأوضاع التي يمكن اجتماعها مع إنسانية الفرس من كونه ضاحكا أو كاتبا أو ناطقا إلى غير ذلك، وهي محالة في أنفسها، أو لا كقولنا كلما كان زيد إنسانا فهو حيوان، فمعناه
And the negative of each one of these propositions – i.e., the implicative connected, the accidental connected, the genuine disjunctive, the restrictive of union, and the restrictive of emptiness – is established by negating what was judged in their affirmatives. For the implicative negative is that in which the implication is negated, the accidental negative is that in which the agreement of the two sides in truth is negated, and so on. Then he indicated the division of the conditional into the quantified (maḥṣūra), the unrestricted (muhmala), and the particular (mukhṣaṣa) according to the division of the categorical proposition into them, because the states (awḍā') in the conditional are like the individuals in the categorical. So he said: Know that the universality of the conditional – i.e., the conditional being universal – is that the consequent is necessary in the implicative connected, or contrary in the disjunctive contrary, to the antecedent, with respect to his saying 'contrary' or 'necessary' by way of mutual dependency. And likewise in his saying: 'upon all the states' i.e., the circumstances that do not negate the antecendence of the antecedent, i.e., it is possible for the antecedent to occur with them, whether those circumstances are impossible in themselves like our saying: 'Whenever a horse is a human, it is an animal' for its meaning is that the necessity of the animality of the horse is established for the humanity of the horse with all the states that can combine with the humanity of the horse, such as it being laughing, writing, speaking, etc., and those are impossible in themselves, or not, like our saying: 'Whenever Zayd is a human, he is an animal' so its meaning is...
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage first explains how to form the negative (sāliba) of each type of conditional proposition. For each affirmative type, the negative is formed by negating the specific judgement made in that affirmative. For example, the negative of an implicative connected conditional negates the implication; the negative of an accidental connected conditional negates the coincidence in truth; and similarly for the disjunctive types. The author then moves to the topic of universality in conditionals. He notes that conditionals are divided into quantified (maḥṣūra), unrestricted (muhmala), and particular (mukhṣaṣa), analogous to categorical propositions. The universality of a conditional means that the consequent holds for the antecedent under all states (awḍā') that do not negate the antecedent's role. For a connected implicative conditional, the consequent must be necessary (lāzim); for a disjunctive contrary conditional, the consequent must be contrary (mu'ānid) to the antecedent. The states include even impossible ones, as in the example 'Whenever a horse is a human, it is an animal' – here the antecedent is impossible, but the conditional is universal because the necessity holds under all hypothetical states that can be combined with the antecedent (like being laughing, writing, etc., even though those are impossible for a horse). The lesson ends abruptly on the page, but the point about universality is clear: it requires the consequent to follow under all possible antecedents, not just actual ones.

Exclusive Disjunction in Falsity (Man‘at al-Khuluw) and Negation of Conditionals
Definition of man'at al-khuluw and rule for negation of conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify man'at al-khuluw and distinguish it from man'at al-jam', and construct the negation of each conditional type according to the rule that the negative removes what the affirmative judged.

Core Idea
In man'at al-khuluw, both parts can be true but not both false.

We have seen disjunctions that exclude both truth and falsity. Now the author introduces a new type: man'at al-khuluw, where only falsity is excluded. He also gives a general rule for negating any conditional: the negative removes what the affirmative affirmed. Understanding this rule is key to manipulating conditional propositions in logical and theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the disjunction
In the example 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown', the two parts are 'Zayd is in the sea' and 'he does not drown'. Note that the second part is already negative in form, but we treat it as a proposition.
The disjunction uses 'إما أن يكون...أو'.
2
Determine the truth possibilities
Check if the two parts can both be true: can Zayd be in the sea and not drown? Yes, so they are compatible in truth. Can they both be false? If Zayd is not in the sea, then he drowns (since 'not drown' is false), so they cannot both be false. Hence, incompatibility only in falsity.
This is the crux of man'at al-khuluw.
3
Contrast with man'at al-jam'
Recall that man'at al-jam' disallows both true but allows both false. Here it's the opposite. Use the 'tree or stone' example from the passage: a single thing cannot be both tree and stone (both true impossible), but can be neither (false).
Memorize the difference: jam' = truth exclusion; khuluw = falsehood exclusion.
4
Apply the negation rule
The passage says: the negative of any proposition is formed by removing what the affirmative judged. For the implicative conditional, the affirmative judges implication, so its negative denies implication. For man'at al-khuluw, the affirmative judges that both parts cannot be false, so its negative allows them both to be false.
Practice with a few examples from fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شجر أو حجر) فإن الشجر والحجر لا يجتمعان فلا يجوز أن يكون الشيء الواحد شجرا وحجرا معا. (وإما مانعة الخلو إن حكم فيها به أي بالتنافي (بينهما) أي: بين جزئيها (في الكذب فقط) أي من غير التنافي في الصدق، فيجوز اجتماعهما في الوجود، (كقولنا: إما أن يكون زيد في البحر أو لا يغرق) فإن الكون في البحر وعدم الغرق قد يجتمعان وجودا، لكنهما لا يجتمعان عدما؛ لاستحالة انتفاء الكون في البحر وانتفاء عدم الغرق. وسالبة كل واحدة من هذه القضايا) أي المتصلة اللزومية والاتفاقية والمنفصلة الحقيقية ومانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو (تثبت برفع ما حكم به في موجباتها) فإن السالبة اللزومية ما حكم فيها برفع اللزوم، والاتفاقية ما حكم فيها برفع توافق الطرفين في الصدق، وعلى هذا فقس.
Tree or stone) For the tree and the stone do not coexist, so it is not permissible for a single thing to be tree and stone together. (And as for the disjunction that excludes emptiness [man'at al-khuluw], if it is judged therein to be [with incompatibility] (between them) i.e., between its two parts, (in falsity only) i.e., without incompatibility in truth, so their coexistence in existence is permissible, (like our saying: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown') for being in the sea and not drowning may coexist existentially, but they do not coexist in non-existence; because it is impossible for both the negation of being in the sea and the negation of not drowning to obtain. And the negative of each of these propositions) i.e., the implicative conditional, the coincidental conditional, the true disjunction, the disjunction that excludes collection, and the disjunction that excludes emptiness (is confirmed by removing what was judged in their affirmatives). For the implicative negative is that in which the removal of implication is judged, and the coincidental negative is that in which the removal of agreement of the two sides in truth is judged, and upon this analogize.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage completes the author's discussion of the types of disjunctive conditionals by defining man'at al-khuluw (exclusive disjunction in falsity) and then providing the general rule for constructing the negative of each type of conditional. The passage begins with the tail end of an example for man'at al-jam' (exclusive disjunction in truth) — the example 'tree or stone' illustrates that a single thing cannot be both simultaneously, which is the hallmark of man'at al-jam'. The author then transitions to man'at al-khuluw, explaining that in this type, the two parts are incompatible only in falsity (i.e., they cannot both be false), but they may be true together. The example given is 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown' — Zayd could be in the sea and not drown (both true), but it is impossible that he is not in the sea and also does not fail to drown (i.e., that he drowns while not in the sea is impossible). The author then states the rule for the negative of each conditional type: the negative is formed by removing what the affirmative judged. For instance, the negative of an implicative conditional (mutasila luzumiyya) denies the implication; the negative of a coincidental conditional (mutasila ittifaqiyya) denies the agreement in truth; and by extension, the negative of each disjunction type removes the specific incompatibility that the affirmative asserted.

Key Takeaway
Man'at al-khuluw is defined by the inability of both parts to be false while allowing both to be true; the negation of any conditional is formed by removing the specific incompatibility the affirmative judged.

Quantification of Conditional Propositions
Author introduces quantification of conditionals by analogy to categoricals and lists quantifier words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish universal, particular, and singular conditional propositions and apply the appropriate quantifier words in Islamic logical contexts.

Core Idea
Conditionals are quantified by conditions (awda'), like categoricals by individuals.

In categorical logic, propositions are quantified by the number of individuals covered. The same principle applies to conditional propositions, but instead of individuals, we consider the conditions (awda') under which the antecedent holds. This lesson unpacks that analogy and provides the quantifier words used for each type of conditional.

Guided Path
1
Recall categorical quantification
Categorical propositions are universal, particular, or singular based on the quantity of their subject term. This framework is now extended to conditionals.
2
Understand the analogy: awda' as individuals
In conditionals, the antecedent's occurrence can happen under various 'conditions' (awda'), such as time, circumstance, or state. These awda' are treated like the individuals in categoricals for quantification.
Impossible conditions (e.g., a horse being human) are included as valid for universal quantification.
3
Define the universal conditional
A conditional is universal if the consequent is necessary (in implicative) or incompatible (in disjunctive) for the antecedent under all awda' that do not contradict the antecedent's being the antecedent.
Examples: 'Whenever a horse is human, it is animal' (impossible awda') or 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is animal' (possible awda').
4
Define particular and singular conditionals
A conditional is particular if the consequent holds for some but not all awda', and singular if it holds for exactly one specific awda'.
If no quantification is specified, the conditional is 'absolved' (muhmala).
5
List quantifier words
Universal affirmative: 'kullamā', 'mahmā', 'matā' for connected; 'dā'iman' for disjunctive. Universal negative: 'laysa al-battata'. Particular affirmative: 'qad yakūnu'. Particular negative: 'qad lā yakūnu'. Negation of universal quantifiers by adding 'laysa'.
Example: 'laysa kullamā' negates the universal quantifier.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the quantifier of the particular negative in both of them, i.e., in the connected and the disjunctive, is ('qad lā yakūnu') — like our saying: 'qad lā yakūnu' if the sun is rising, night is existing, and 'qad lā yakūnu' either the sun is rising or daytime is existing. And by inserting the particle of negation onto the quantifiers of universal affirmation, like 'laysa kullamā', 'laysa mahmā', and 'laysa matā' in the connected, and 'laysa dā'iman' in the disjunctive.

Commentary
This passage explains how conditional propositions are quantified by analogy to categorical propositions. The author begins by noting that just as categoricals are divided into universal, particular, and singular based on their subjects' individuals, conditionals are divided based on the 'conditions' (awda') under which the antecedent holds — these conditions are treated like individuals. The author then defines universality of a conditional: the consequent is either necessary (in implicative) or incompatible (in adversative disjunction) for the antecedent under all conditions that do not contradict the antecedent's being the antecedent. Two examples illustrate: the impossible condition (e.g., 'whenever a horse is a human, it is an animal' — the condition 'being a human' for a horse is impossible in itself) and the possible condition (e.g., 'whenever Zayd is a human, he is an animal' — conditions like standing or sitting are possible). Particularity is defined as the consequent holding under some, not all, such conditions. Specificity (singular) is when it holds under one specific condition. The absoluteness (absolved) is when conditions are not specified. The author then lists the quantifier words: for universal affirmative in connected conditionals: 'kullamā', 'mahmā', 'matā' (e.g., 'whenever the sun rises, daytime exists'); for universal affirmative in disjunctives: 'dā'iman' (e.g., 'always either the sun is rising or daytime is not existing' – this is a disjunctive universal). For universal negative in both: 'laysa al-battata' (not at all). For particular affirmative: 'qad yakūnu' (it may be). For particular negative: 'qad lā yakūnu' (it may not be). Finally, the negation of universal quantifiers is formed by adding the negation particle, e.g., 'laysa kullamā' (not whenever) for connected conditionals.

Propositions

5 min
Quantifiers for Conditional Propositions
Author enumerates quantifiers for conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantifier (sūr) for any given conditional proposition and recognize omission markers.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions have six quantifiers: three for conjunctive, three for disjunctive, and omission is marked by 'law', 'in', 'idhā', 'immā', 'aw'.

Just as categorical propositions have quantifiers (all, no, some, some not), conditional propositions also have quantifiers specifying the scope of the connection. This passage lists the quantifiers for conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfaṣila) conditionals, and explains how omission of the quantifier is indicated by specific particles.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish conjunctive from disjunctive conditionals
Conjunctive conditionals (متصلة) connect antecedent and consequent with 'if...then', while disjunctive conditionals (منفصلة) present mutually exclusive alternatives. This distinction determines which quantifiers apply.
e.g., 'If the sun rises, then it is day' is conjunctive; 'Either it is day or night' is disjunctive.
2
Identify the six quantifiers
For universal affirmative conjunctive: 'kullamā', 'mahmā', 'matā'. For universal affirmative disjunctive: 'dā'iman'. For universal negative (both): 'laysa al-battata'. For particular affirmative (both): 'qad yakūnu'. For particular negative (both): 'qad lā yakūnu'. Also, universal negative can be formed by prefixing 'laysa' to the universal affirmative quantifier.
Memorize the Arabic terms: كلما, مهما, متى, دائمًا, ليس البتة, قد يكون, قد لا يكون.
3
Recognize omission markers (ihmāl)
When the quantifier is omitted, specific particles are used: 'law', 'in', 'idhā' for conjunctive conditionals; 'immā' and 'aw' for disjunctive conditionals. These indicate that the proposition lacks an explicit quantifier.
Example: 'Law (لو) the sun rises, then it is day' omits 'kullamā'.
4
Understand the dialectical objection about 'mahmā'
Some object that 'mahmā' linguistically means 'whatever' (for individuals), but logicians have transferred it to situations, making it valid as a universal conjunctive quantifier. This shows that technical logical terms may diverge from ordinary language.
This objection and response highlight the conventional nature of logical terminology.
