فسور الموجبة الكلية في الشرطية (المتصلة «كلما» و «مهما» و «متی» نحو كلما ومهما ومتى كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، (و) سور الموجبة الكلية (في) الشرطية المنفصلة «دائمًا») نحو: دائمًا إما أن يكون الشمس طالعة أو لا يكون النهار موجودًا، وسور السالبة الكلية فيهما أي في المتصلة والمنفصلة «ليس البتة نحو ليس البتة إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود، وليس البتة إما أن يكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون النهار موجودا. (و) سور (الموجبة الجزئية فيهما أي في المتصلة والمنفصلة («قد يكون») نحو قد يكون إذا كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودا، وقد يكون إما أن يكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجودا. وسور السالبة الجزئية فيهما أي في المتصلة والمنفصلة («قد لا يكون») نحو قولنا: قد لا يكون إذا كانت الشمس طالعة كان الليل موجودا، وقد لا يكون إما أن يكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون النهار موجودًا، وبإدخال حرف السلب على سور الإيجاب الكلي كـ ليس كلما وليس مهما وليس متى في المتصلة، وليس دائما في المنفصلة. (وإهمالها) أي إهمال القضية الشرطية بلفظ «لو» (أي: بإدخال لفظ «لو») (و«إن» و «إذا» في المتصلة و «إما» و «أو» في المنفصلة). فإن قيل: لفظة «مهما» لا تكون سور الكلية المتصلة؛ لأنها موضوعة لعموم الأفراد، قلنا: إن «مهما» وإن كانت بحسب اللغة موضوعة لعموم الأفراد لكنهم نقلوها إلى عموم الأوضاع، فجعلوها سور الكلية المتصلة.
The quantifier of the universal affirmative in the conditional (conjunctive is 'kullamā' and 'mahmā' and 'matā', like: kullamā, mahmā, matā the sun is rising then daytime exists. And the quantifier of the universal affirmative in the disjunctive conditional is 'dā'iman': like: dā'iman either the sun is rising or daytime does not exist. And the quantifier of the universal negative in both, i.e., in the conjunctive and disjunctive, is 'laysa al-battata': like: laysa al-battata if the sun is rising then night exists, and laysa al-battata either the sun is rising or daytime exists. And the quantifier of the particular affirmative in both, i.e., in the conjunctive and disjunctive, is 'qad yakūnu': like: qad yakūnu if the sun is rising then daytime exists, and qad yakūnu either the sun is rising or night exists. And the quantifier of the particular negative in both, i.e., in the conjunctive and disjunctive, is 'qad lā yakūnu': like our saying: qad lā yakūnu if the sun is rising then night exists, and qad lā yakūnu either the sun is rising or daytime exists. And by inserting the negative particle onto the quantifier of universal affirmative, like: laysa kullamā, and laysa mahmā, and laysa matā in the conjunctive, and laysa dā'iman in the disjunctive. (And their omission) i.e., the omission of the conditional proposition by the word 'law' (i.e., by inserting the word 'law') and 'in' and 'idhā' in the conjunctive, and 'immā' and 'aw' in the disjunctive. For if it is said: The word 'mahmā' is not a quantifier of the universal conjunctive; because it is posited for the generality of individuals, we say: Indeed 'mahmā' although according to language it is posited for the generality of individuals, they have transferred it to the generality of situations, making it a quantifier of the universal conjunctive.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines the quantifiers (sūr) used for conditional propositions (sharṭiyya) in logic, distinguishing between conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfaṣila) types. The author lists quantifiers for each combination of affirmative/negative and universal/particular. For universal affirmative conjunctive: 'kullamā', 'mahmā', 'matā' (e.g., 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists'). For universal affirmative disjunctive: 'dā'iman' (e.g., 'Always either the sun is rising or daytime does not exist'). For universal negative in both: 'laysa al-battata' (e.g., 'It is never the case that if the sun is rising then night exists'). For particular affirmative: 'qad yakūnu' (e.g., 'Sometimes if the sun is rising, daytime exists'). For particular negative: 'qad lā yakūnu' (e.g., 'Sometimes it is not the case that if the sun is rising, night exists'). Additionally, the author notes that universal negatives can also be formed by adding the negation particle to universal affirmative quantifiers (e.g., 'laysa kullamā'). Then he explains omission (ihmāl) of quantifiers: in conjunctive conditionals, words like 'law', 'in', 'idhā' indicate omission; in disjunctive, 'immā' and 'aw'. Finally, a dialectical point: someone might object that 'mahmā' is for general individuals, not situations. The author responds that logicians have transferred its usage to the generality of situations, making it valid as a universal conjunctive quantifier. This passage is foundational for understanding the formal structure of conditional propositions in Islamic logic.

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions have distinct quantifiers depending on conjunction/disjunction and universal/particular, and omission is signalled by specific particles.

?

The Quantifier 'Mahma' for Universal Conditionals
Author responds to an objection about 'mahma' as a conditional quantifier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the word 'mahma' is extended from individual to conditional generality and defend it against the objection that it is only for individuals.

Core Idea
Technical terms in logic can be extended beyond their ordinary language meaning.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that the word 'mahma' cannot serve as a quantifier for universal connected conditionals because it originally indicates generality of individuals. The author argues that logicians have deliberately transferred its usage to generality of states (conditions). This shows how technical terminology in logic can diverge from common language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
What is the claim that 'mahma' cannot be a quantifier for conditionals? The objector says it cannot be a quantifier (sawr) for universal connected conditionals (al-kulliyya al-muttasila).
Look for the phrase 'fa-in qila'.
2
Identify the basis of the objection
Why does the objector think 'mahma' is only for individuals? Because in language (lugha), 'mahma' is placed for the generality of individuals (umum al-afrad).
The objector assumes linguistic meaning dictates logical usage.
3
Identify the author's response
How does the author justify using 'mahma' for conditionals? He concedes the original linguistic meaning but argues that logicians have transferred (naql) it to the generality of states (umum al-awda'). Thus it becomes a quantifier for conditionals.
The response uses the concept of 'naql' (technical transfer).
4
Understand the concept of 'transfer'
Logicians can repurpose common words for technical definitions. Here, 'mahma' shifts from referring to all individuals to referring to all conditions or states.
This is common in Islamic sciences (e.g., 'salat' in language means 'prayer').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإهمالها) أي إهمال القضية الشرطية بلفظ «لو» ) أي: بإدخال لفظ «لو» (و«إن» و «إذا» في المتصلة و «إما» و «أو» في المنفصلة فإن قيل: لفظة «مهما» لا تكون سور الكلية المتصلة؛ لأنها موضوعة لعموم الأفراد، قلنا: إن «مهما» وإن كانت بحسب اللغة موضوعة لعموم الأفراد لكنهم نقلوها إلى عموم الأوضاع، فجعلوها سور الكلية المتصلة.
And neglecting them, i.e., neglecting the conditional proposition by the word 'law', i.e., by inserting the word 'law', 'in', and 'idha' in the connected [conditional], and 'imma' and 'aw' in the disjunctive. For if it is said: The word 'mahma' cannot be the quantifier of the universal connected conditional because it is placed for the generality of individuals, we say: 'Mahma', even if in language it is placed for the generality of individuals, they have transferred it to the generality of states, so they made it the quantifier of the universal connected conditional.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange about the quantifier for universal connected conditionals. The author first mentions that neglecting conditional propositions is done by using words like 'law', 'in', 'idha' for connected conditionals, and 'imma' and 'aw' for disjunctives. Then an objection is raised: the word 'mahma' cannot serve as a quantifier for universal connected conditionals because in language it is used for generality of individuals (i.e., for universal categorical propositions). The author responds that although 'mahma' originally indicates generality of individuals, the logicians have transferred its usage to generality of states (i.e., conditions), thus it becomes a quantifier for universal connected conditionals. This shows that technical terms in logic can be extended from their common linguistic meanings.

Key Takeaway
The word 'mahma' is legitimately used as a quantifier for universal conditionals because logicians have extended its meaning from individuals to states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition of Contradiction (Tanāquḍ)
Author defines contradiction and guards against misinterpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of the definition of contradiction and explain why each exclusion is necessary.

Core Idea
Contradiction is not just any difference—it must necessitate truth and falsehood by itself.

Contradiction is a foundational concept in logic, especially for dialectics and proof. The author presents a precise definition that distinguishes genuine contradiction from other forms of difference. Let's examine each part of the definition to understand what is included and excluded.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition
Find the sentence: 'He said: (It is the difference of two propositions by negation and affirmation such that it necessitates by itself that one of them is true and the other false.)' This is the definiens.
2
Identify the genus and differentia
The genus is 'difference of two propositions' (اختيلاف قضيتين). The differentia is 'by negation and affirmation such that it necessitates by itself that one is true and the other false'. Note the conditions.
The differentia includes multiple conditions.
3
Understand each exclusionary clause
The author explains: 'two propositions' excludes singular terms (like names). 'by negation and affirmation' excludes other differences (e.g., difference in quantity). 'such that it necessitates' excludes differences that do not force truth values. 'by itself' excludes accidental necessity due to external factors or specific matter.
These clauses guard against misinterpretations.
4
Reject an alternative reading
The commentator rejects the claim that 'by negation and affirmation' alone suffices to guard against non-proposition differences, arguing that the aspect of necessitating truth conditional is sufficient.
This shows the precision required in definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في التناقض ثم أورد تعريف ماهية تناقض القضايا؛ لأنه المقصود بالنظر فقال: (وهو اختلاف قضيتين بالسلب والإيجاب بحيث يقتضي لذاته أن تكون إحداهما صادقة والأخرى كاذبة) واحترز به عن اختلاف غير القضيتين كالمفردين وكالمفرد والقضية والأولى أن يقال: إن قوله (قضيتين) وقوله : (بالسلب والإيجاب) تحقيق لمفهوم التناقض، وإلا فالحيثية المذكورة بعده تغني عنه؛ لأن اختلاف غير القضيتين والاختلاف بغير الإيجاب والسلب لا يكون بهذه الحيثية، وقوله: (بحيث يقتضي) يخرج الاختلاف الغير المقتضي، وقوله: (لذاته) يخرج الاختلاف المقتضى لأن يكون إحداهما صادقة والأخرى كاذبة لا لذاته، بل بواسطة أو بخصوص المادة، وما قيل إنه حصل الاحتراز عن الأول بقوله : (بالسلب والإيجاب) ممنوع. واختلفوا في أن التناقض متى يتحقق، فقال القدماء: إنه إنما يتحقق بعد اشتراك القضيتين في ثمان وحدات واكتفى بعضهم بثلاث وحدات وحدة الموضوع، ووحدة المحمول، ووحدة الزمان؛ زعما منه أن وحدة الشرط والجزء والكل داخله تحت وحدة الموضوع، ووحدة المكان والإضافة والقوة والفعل مندرجة تحت وحدة المحمول، واكتفى المتأخرون بوحدتين وحدة الموضوع، ووحدة المحمول؛ زعما منهم أن وحدة الموضوع يندرج فيها وحدة الشرط والكل والجزء، ووحدة المحمول يندرج فيها الوحدات الباقية. والمصنف رحمه الله اكتفى بوحدة النسبة الحكمية فقال : ( ولا يتحقق) أي: التناقض بين القضيتين إلا باتحاد النسبة الحكمية بينهما حتى يكون السلب واردا على ما ورد عليه الإيجاب، هذا فإن القول ما قاله المصنف.
A chapter on contradiction. Then he presented the definition of the essence of contradiction of propositions because it is the intended object of consideration. He said: (It is the difference of two propositions by negation and affirmation such that it necessitates by itself that one of them is true and the other false.) And he guarded by it against the difference of non-propositions, like two individuals and an individual with a proposition. It is better to say: His saying 'two propositions' and his saying 'by negation and affirmation' are a verification of the concept of contradiction. Otherwise, the aspect mentioned afterwards suffices for it, because the difference of non-propositions and the difference by other than affirmation and negation does not happen with this aspect. His saying 'such that it necessitates' excludes difference that does not necessitate. His saying 'by itself' excludes difference that necessitates one of them to be true and the other false not by itself but by an intermediary or by specific matter. And what is said that he secured guarding against the first by his saying 'by negation and affirmation' is rejected. And they differed regarding when contradiction occurs. The ancients said: It only occurs after the two propositions share in eight unities. Some sufficed with three unities: unity of subject, unity of predicate, and unity of time; claiming that unity of condition, part, and whole are included under unity of subject, and unity of place, relation, potentiality, and actuality are subsumed under unity of predicate. The latecomers sufficed with two unities: unity of subject and unity of predicate; claiming that under unity of subject is included unity of condition, whole, and part, and under unity of predicate are included the remaining unities. The author, may God have mercy on him, sufficed with the unity of the judgmental relation and said: (It does not occur) i.e., contradiction between two propositions except by the unity of the judgmental relation between them so that the negation applies to what affirmation applied to. This is the statement as the author said.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage defines contradiction (tanāquḍ) and discusses the number of conditions required for its occurrence. The commentator first clarifies the definition: contradiction is the difference of two propositions by negation and affirmation such that it necessitates by itself that one is true and the other false. Each part of the definition is explained: 'two propositions' excludes singular terms; 'by negation and affirmation' specifies the type of difference; 'such that it necessitates' excludes differences that do not necessitate truth values; 'by itself' excludes accidental necessity due to external factors or specific matter. A misinterpretation is rejected. Then the text reviews historical differences on the conditions for contradiction: the ancients required eight unities (subject, predicate, time, place, condition, part, whole, etc.), some reduced to three (subject, predicate, time), later scholars reduced to two (subject and predicate). The author (may Allah have mercy on him) simplifies to one essential condition: unity of the judgmental relation (al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya), meaning that the negation must apply to exactly the same relation as the affirmation. This view is presented as the correct one.

Key Terms
تناقض
النسبة الحكمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition, which of the following is excluded by the clause 'by itself'?
A) Difference of two individuals (e.g., Zayd and Amr).
B) Difference that necessitates truth values only because of specific material (e.g., 'The sky is blue' and 'The sky is not blue' when the sky is actually blue).
C) Difference by quantity (e.g., 'All men are mortal' vs 'Some men are mortal').
D) Difference that does not necessitate any truth assignment.

Key Takeaway
A true contradiction must be a genuine pair of propositions differing by negation and affirmation, with the necessary truth of one and falsehood of the other arising from the propositions themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the pair: 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is sitting'. Explain why this pair does NOT satisfy the definition of contradiction. Which condition is violated?
?
Take the pair: 'Khamr is haram' and 'Khamr is not haram'. Verify against the definition: are they two propositions? Is the difference by negation and affirmation? Does it necessitate by itself? Provide your reasoning.
